Order and Conditions of a Valid Definition
Author explains necessary order and conditions of definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify whether a definition has the correct order (proximate genus before species) and satisfies the conditions of constancy and converse.

Core Idea
A valid definition must be ordered and satisfy both constancy and converse.

In this passage, the author explains that a definition is not merely a random collection of essential attributes. It must follow a specific order and meet two crucial conditions: constancy (iṭṭirād) and converse (inʿikās). These ensure that the definition is جامع مانع – inclusive of all members and exclusive of non-members. He illustrates with the perfect definition of human as 'rational animal' and warns against amplification or conciseness that break these conditions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defined term and its definition
Locate the term being defined (e.g., 'human') and the definition proposed (e.g., 'rational animal').
2
Check the order of parts
Verify that the proximate genus (e.g., 'animal') comes before the species (e.g., 'rational'). The author states that what is prior by nature must be prior in position.
3
Test constancy (ṭard)
Ask: Does this definition apply to every individual of the defined? For 'rational animal', every human is indeed rational animal. If any human were not rational, constancy would fail.
The definition must be inclusive (jāmiʿ).
4
Test converse (ʿaks)
Ask: Does every individual that satisfies the definition belong to the defined? For 'rational animal', every rational animal is human. If some rational animals were not human (e.g., hypothetical aliens), converse would fail.
The definition must be exclusive (māniʿ).
5
Identify violations: amplification or conciseness
Check if the definition has extra attributes (amplification) – this breaks constancy because not all humans may have that attribute. Also check if it omits necessary attributes (conciseness) – this breaks converse because other beings may share the remaining attributes.
The author's examples: 'rational reflective animal' breaks constancy; 'understanding animal' breaks converse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم التأليف بين الذاتيات لا يكفي وجوده كيف اتفق بل لا بد فيه من هيئة وترتيب، فإن معنى الحد في الذهن مثال مطابق للمحدود في الوجود فكما أن المحدود لا يوجد إلا بتأليف مخصوص وهيئة مخصوصة فكذلك الحد، وكيف لا ؟ وقد كان كل واحد منهما مشتملا على الأجزاء المرتبة بعضها على البعض. فترتيب الحد هو أن يكون مسبوقا فصله أي فصل المحدود بجنسه القريب فإنه من اللوازم فإن ما يكون مقدمًا طبعًا يكون مقدمًا وضعًا. وكما أنه وهو الترتيب من اللوازم فكذلك الإطراد والانعكاس؛ إذ هما من الشرائط للحد. والحد هو الجامع المانع على ما عرف فيقال كل إنسان حيوان ناطق وكل حیوان ناطق إنسان. ولهذا لا تحتمل الوجازة ولا الإطناب المعنوي؛ إذ الإطناب يبطل الطرد كما يقال في حد الإنسان حيوان ناطق متفطن» مثلا، فإن كل حيوان بهذه الصفة. وإن كان إنسانًا فكل إنسان لا يكون حيوانا بهذه. والإيجاز يبطل العكس كما يقال حيوان متفهم مثلا؛ إذ كل إنسان. وإن كان حيوانا متفهما فكل حيوان متفهم لا يلزمه أن يكون إنسانًا. فالحد المشتمل على ما مر ذكره من الترتيب والشرط هو أن يقال حيوان ناطق وإنه حد كامل فدل على ماهية الإنسان كما هي لما أن الجنس القريب وهو الحيوان يتضمن الدلالة على تمام ما به المشاركة من الذاتيات، وفصله وهو الناطق يتضمن
Then the combination of essential parts is not sufficient however it occurs; rather, it must have a specific form and order. For the meaning of definition in the mind is a model corresponding to the defined in existence, just as the defined does not exist except with a specific composition and specific form, so too the definition, and why not? For each of them includes parts arranged sequentially upon one another. The order of the definition is that its species, i.e., the species of the defined, be preceded by its proximate genus, for that is necessary. For what is prior by nature must be prior in position. And just as this order is necessary, so are constancy (iṭṭirād) and converse (inʿikās); for they are conditions for the definition. The definition is all-inclusive and exclusive as is known, so it is said: 'Every human is a rational animal' and 'Every rational animal is human'. For this reason, definition does not tolerate conciseness (ījāz) nor meaningful amplification (iṭnāb). For amplification invalidates constancy, as is said in the definition of human: 'rational, reflective animal', for example. For every animal with this description? And if it is a human, every human is not an animal with this description? And conciseness invalidates the converse, as is said: 'understanding animal', for example. For every human, even if it is an understanding animal, not every understanding animal is necessarily human. So the definition that includes what has been mentioned of order and condition is to say 'rational animal', and it is a complete definition that indicates the essence of human as it is, because the proximate genus – animal – includes the indication of all that is shared in terms of essential attributes, and its species – rational – includes...
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage is a definitional exposition on the requirements of a valid hadd definition. The author begins by stating that mere collection of essential parts (al-dhātiyyāt) is not enough; they must be arranged in a specific order (tartīb). He justifies this by analogy: the definition is a mental model of the defined, and just as the defined has its parts ordered in existence, so must the definition. Specifically, the order requires that the proximal genus (al-jins al-qarīb) precede the species (al-faṣl), because what is prior by nature should be prior in verbal statement (mā kāna muqaddaman ṭabʿan yakūnu muqaddaman waḍʿan). Then he adds two further necessary conditions: 'constancy' (al-iṭṭirād) and 'converse' (al-inʿikās). Constancy means that the definition applies to every individual of the defined term; converse means that every individual satisfying the definition is an individual of the defined term. Together they ensure the definition is جامع مانع (jāmiʿ māniʿ): inclusive of all members and exclusive of non-members. He illustrates with the famous example: 'Every human is a rational animal' (constancy) and 'Every rational animal is human' (converse). He then shows how violating these conditions by conciseness (ījāz) or amplification (iṭnāb) spoils the definition. Amplification adds an extra condition (e.g., 'reflective') which may break constancy because not all humans are reflective; the Arabic text here is somewhat garbled but the principle is clear. Conciseness drops a necessary component (e.g., 'understanding' instead of 'rational'), breaking the converse because other animals may be understanding. The perfect definition for human is therefore 'rational animal' — the complete hadd. The passage ends incomplete, mentioning that the genus (animal) conveys all shared essential attributes and the species (rational) conveys the distinctive one.

Key Terms
الترتيب
الإطراد
الانعكاس
الجامع المانع
الإطناب
الإيجاز

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the effect of amplification (iṭnāb) in a definition?
A) It breaks the converse (ʿaks).
B) It breaks constancy (ṭard).
C) It changes the order of genus and species.
D) It makes the definition more precise and comprehensive.

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must have the proximate genus before the species, and must satisfy both constancy (inclusion of all members) and converse (exclusion of non-members), making it جامع مانع.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the definition of 'salah' (prayer) as 'specific actions and words beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'. Check constancy: does every salah include these? Check converse: does every action with takbir and taslim count as salah? Identify any possible violations and propose a more precise definition.
?
The definition of 'khamr' (wine) is sometimes given as 'intoxicating grape drink'. Test constancy (is every intoxicating grape drink intoxicating? does every grape drink that intoxicates count?) and converse (is every intoxicating drink made from grapes? consider non-grape intoxicants). Discuss whether this definition is جامع مانع.

The Three Essential Conditions of a Definition: Order, Comprehensiveness, and Conversion
Author defines the nature of 'hadd' and its necessary conditions: order, comprehensiveness, and conversion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and test whether a definition satisfies the conditions of order, ittiraad, and in'ikaas.

Core Idea
A valid definition must include all instances and exclude all non-instances.

In this passage, the author explains that a definition is not just a random collection of essential attributes; it must have a specific order and satisfy two key logical properties: comprehensiveness (ittiraad) and conversion (in'ikaas). These conditions ensure that the definition perfectly corresponds to the defined thing. Let's examine how these conditions work using the standard example of defining 'insan' (human).

Guided Path
1
Identify the definition's parts
Locate the genus and differentia in the definition. For the example of human, the genus is 'animal' and the differentia is 'rational'.
2
Check order
Ensure that the differentia follows the proximate genus. The author says the differentia must be preceded by the proximate genus in the definition.
3
Test comprehensiveness (ittiraad)
Verify that every instance of the defined term falls under the definition. For 'human', is every human a rational animal? Yes.
4
Test conversion (in'ikaas)
Verify that everything that satisfies the definition is an instance of the defined term. Is every rational animal a human? Yes (assuming no other rational animals).
5
Apply to a deficient example
Try adding an extra attribute (e.g., 'attentive') and see why it breaks ittiraad or in'ikaas.
The author says adding 'attentive' breaks ittiraad because not every human is attentive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نقول إنه وهو الحد لما صار محدودًا كان حده داخلا تحت الحد المطلق؛ وهو قول مصور ماهية الشيء في النفس كما مر. فحده إذن هو الحد المطلق كحد غيره أي شيء كان ذلك الغير. وإنه إنما يمتاز عن سائر الحدود من حيث إنه مضاف إلى الحد، والإضافة التي بها يتميز حد الشيء عن سائر الحدود غنية عن التعريف لما أن طبائع النسب معلومة عند العلم بالمنسوبات. ثم التأليف بين الذاتيات لا يكفي وجوده كيف اتفق بل لا بد فيه من هيئة وترتيب، فإن معنى الحد في الذهن مثال مطابق للمحدود في الوجود فكما أن المحدود لا يوجد إلا بتأليف مخصوص وهيئة مخصوصة فكذلك الحد، وكيف لا؟ وقد كان كل واحد منهما مشتملا على الأجزاء المرتبة بعضها على البعض. فترتيب الحد هو أن يكون مسبوقا فصله أي فصل المحدود بجنسه القريب فإنه من اللوازم فإن ما يكون مقدمًا طبعًا يكون مقدمًا وضعًا. وكما أنه وهو الترتيب من اللوازم فكذلك الإطراد والانعكاس؛ إذ هما من الشرائط للحد. والحد هو الجامع المانع على ما عرف فيقال كل إنسان حيوان ناطق وكل حيوان ناطق إنسان. ولهذا لا تحتمل الوجازة ولا الإطناب المعنوي؛ إذ الإطناب يبطل الطرد كما يقال في حد الإنسان حيوان ناطق متفطن» مثلا، فإن كل حيوان بهذه الصفة. وإن كان إنسانًا فكل إنسان لا يكون حيوانا بهذه. والإيجاز يبطل العكس كما يقال حيوان متفهم مثلا؛ إذ كل إنسان. وإن كان حيوانا متفهما فكل حيوان متفهم لا يلزمه أن يكون إنسانًا. فالحد المشتمل على ما مر ذكره من الترتيب والشرط هو أن يقال حيوان ناطق وإنه حد كامل فدل على ماهية الإنسان كما هي لما أن الجنس القريب وهو الحيوان يتضمن الدلالة على تمام ما به المشاركة من الذاتيات، وفصله وهو الناطق يتضمن الدلالة على تمام ما به الممايزة منها وإنه هو الغاية في الإيجاز اللفظي، وهذا الإيجاز من اللوازم. فإن التعرض للإطناب لا يكون على وفق العقل إلا لضرورة؛ وذلك في صورة لم يوجد للجنس القريب اسم موضوع مطابق يورد ذلك ويقرن به الفصل: فيورد بدل ذلك الاسم حده كما يقال في حد الحيوان جسم ذو نفس حساس متحرك بالإرادة»؛ إذ الجسم ذو النفس حد الجنس القريب للحيوان. ثم إنه إذا كان له اسم موضوع يطابقه فإذا ترك ذلك في الذكر وأورد للحد بدله عمدًا أو سهوا فإنه لا يكون حدًّا عند بعضهم؛ فإن من شرط الحد أن يكون قولاً وجيزا. والحق فيه أن يكون حدا؛ إذ الإعراض عن الإيجاز لا يخرجه عن كونه حدا؛ وقد كان كل واحد منهما في معنى صاحبه فلا تغابن بينهما إلا في اللفظ. وأما قولهم إن من شرط الحد أن يكون قولاً وجيزا»، فذلك لا يكون كما ينبغي؛ إذ الوجيز أمر إضافي غير محدود بحد معلوم. فربَّ شيء هو وجيز بالإضافة إلى شيء طويل بالإضافة إلى شيء آخر. والأمور الإضافية من جملة ما لا يصح استعماله في تعريف ما ليس بإضافي، والحد ليس من قبيل الإضافيات. ثم الشيء الواحد لا يمكن أن يعرف تعريفا حديًّا إلا بحد واحد؛ إذ للحد بيانه وهو شيء واحد. غير أنه بحسب المعنى لا غير فإنه يمكن أن يكون متعددًا بحسب اللفظ كما مر؛ وإنه هو الحد الكامل لما أن الفصل مسبوق بجنسه القريب على ما عرف. فأما إذا كان مسبوقاً ببعض أجناسه البعيدة أو عوارضه أو غير مسبوق فإنه أي الحد ناقص لما أنه لا يستوي في جميع ذاتيات الشيء ولا يكون مطابقا له في المعنى فيحصل منه التمييز الذاتي دون (١) النسخة : لا يستو. وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق.
We say that the definition (hadd), when it becomes the defined (mahdud), its definition falls under the absolute definition; and it is a statement depicting the quiddity of a thing in the soul as has passed. So its definition is thus the absolute definition like the definition of anything else, whatever that other thing may be. And it is distinguished from other definitions only by being added to the definition, and the addition by which the definition of a thing is distinguished from other definitions is independent of definition because the natures of relations are known when the relata are known. Then, the combination of essential attributes is not sufficient by its mere existence however it happens, but rather a form and order are necessary. For the meaning of definition in the mind is an example corresponding to the defined in existence. Just as the defined does not exist except through a specific combination and a specific form, so too the definition. And how could it be otherwise? Each of them comprises parts arranged one after another. The order of the definition is that its differentia, i.e., the differentia of the defined, be preceded by its proximate genus, for this is among the necessities. For what is prior by nature is made prior in position. And just as order is among the necessities, so too are comprehensiveness (ittiraad) and conversion (in'ikaas), for they are among the conditions for the definition. The definition is that which includes and excludes, as known. So it is said: 'Every human is a rational animal, and every rational animal is a human.' For this reason, it does not tolerate conciseness (ijaaz) nor excessive verbosity (itnaab) in meaning. Indeed, verbosity invalidates comprehensiveness, as if one says in the definition of human: 'rational animal attentive' for example, for every animal with this attribute [is human]? But if it is human, not every human is an animal with this attribute. And conciseness invalidates conversion, as if one says: 'understanding animal' for example, for every human is an understanding animal, but not every understanding animal is necessarily human. So the definition comprising what has been mentioned of order and condition is that one says: 'rational animal', and it is a complete definition, for it indicates the quiddity of man as it is, because the proximate genus, which is animal, includes indication of all the essential attributes of commonality, and its differentia, which is rational, includes indication of all the essential attributes of differentiation. And it is the ultimate in verbal conciseness, and this conciseness is among the necessities. For engaging in verbosity is not in accordance with reason except out of necessity. That occurs in a situation where there is no conventional name corresponding to the proximate genus to be used together with the differentia; so one uses in its place its definition, as is said in the definition of animal: 'a body possessing a sensitive soul, moving by will' — for 'body possessing a soul' is the definition of the proximate genus of animal. Then, if there is a conventional name corresponding to it, and one omits that in mention and substitutes its definition intentionally or forgetfully, it is not a definition according to some, for among the conditions of definition is that it be a concise statement. The truth in the matter is that it is a definition, because turning away from conciseness does not remove it from being a definition; each of them is equivalent in meaning, so there is no difference except in wording. As for their saying that among the conditions of definition is that it be a concise statement, that is not as it should be, for 'concise' is a relative matter, not bounded by a known limit. For something is concise relative to one thing and lengthy relative to another. Relative matters are among what cannot be used to define what is not relative, and definition is not among relatives. Then, a single thing cannot be known by a definitional definition except through one definition, for definition has a single exposition, but only in respect of meaning. It is possible to be multiple in wording, as has passed. And this is the complete definition, because the differentia is preceded by the proximate genus as known. But if it is preceded by some distant genus or accidents, or is not preceded, then it (i.e., the definition) is deficient, because it does not cover all the essential attributes of the thing and does not correspond to it in meaning, so it yields essential distinction only. (1) [Footnote: The manuscript has 'la yastaw' (does not equal), and what we have established is correct as appears from the context.]
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of 'hadd' (definition) itself and explains its necessary conditions. The author begins by noting that when we define 'hadd', its definition falls under the absolute definition (i.e., a statement depicting quiddity), but it is distinguished by being a second-order definition — a definition of definition. This distinction via relation (idaafa) is self-evident because relations are known once the relata are known. The core point is that a valid definition requires a specific order (tartib): the differentia must be preceded by the proximate genus. This order is a natural necessity because the defined exists with a specific composition, so its mental image (the definition) must mirror that composition. Along with order, the definition must satisfy two conditions: ittiraad (comprehensiveness: every instance of the defined is described by the definition) and in'ikaas (conversion: everything described by the definition is an instance of the defined). The author illustrates with the standard example: 'Every human is a rational animal' (ittiraad) and 'Every rational animal is a human' (in'ikaas). From this, the author argues that excessive verbosity (itnaab) breaks ittiraad — e.g., adding 'attentive' to 'rational animal' would exclude some humans (who may not be attentive) and include non-humans. Conversely, excessive conciseness (ijaaz) breaks in'ikaas — e.g., defining human as 'understanding animal' would include animals that understand but are not human (like some animals). Therefore, the ideal complete definition is simply 'rational animal', using the proximate genus and the specific differentia. However, if there is no single word for the proximate genus, its definition may be used (e.g., for 'animal', one says 'body with a soul, sensitive, moving by will'). The author then addresses a dispute: if a single-word genus exists but someone deliberately or accidentally uses its definition instead, does the result remain a definition? Some say no, because conciseness is a condition. The author argues yes, because the meaning is identical — only wording differs. He further refutes the claim that conciseness is an absolute condition, noting that 'concise' is relative. Finally, the author states that a single thing can only have one true definition in meaning (one complete exposition of quiddity), though multiple wordings are possible. The passage concludes by distinguishing a complete definition (using proximate genus and differentia) from a deficient one (using distant genera or accidents), which yields only essential distinction (tamyiz dhati) but not full correspondence.

Key Terms
الحد
الإطراد
الانعكاس
الجنس القريب
الفصل

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which condition is violated if you define human as 'rational animal attentive'?
A) الترتيب (order)
B) الإطراد (comprehensiveness)
C) الانعكاس (conversion)
D) الإيجاز (conciseness)

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must have the differentia preceded by the proximate genus, and must satisfy both ittiraad (all instances included) and in'ikaas (non-instances excluded).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a definition of 'sawm' (fasting) from a fiqh text (e.g., 'abstaining from specific things with intention from dawn to sunset'). Test whether it satisfies ittiraad (does it cover all valid fasts?) and in'ikaas (does it exclude non-fasting actions?).

The Dispute over Conciseness and the Use of Definitions in Place of Single-Word Genera
Author discusses conciseness as a relative condition and the permissibility of using a definition in place of a single-word genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a definition remains valid when conciseness is compromised, and when it is permissible to use a description in place of a single-word genus.

Core Idea
Conciseness is relative; a definition does not become invalid just because it uses more words than necessary.

The author addresses a subtle point: is conciseness an absolute condition for a definition? Some logicians say yes, but the author argues that 'concise' is relative. He also discusses when it is necessary to use a definition instead of a single-word genus, such as when no convenient name exists for the proximate genus. Let's examine these nuances.

Guided Path
1
Understand the issue of conciseness
The author says that some consider conciseness a condition, but he argues it is relative. Something may be concise compared to one thing and verbose compared to another.
2
Examine the example of verbosity
If you define 'human' as 'rational animal attentive', it breaks ittiraad. But if you define 'animal' as 'body with a soul, sensitive, moving by will', that is permissible because there is no single word for that combination (the definition of animal uses its proximate genus and differentia). The author says that if there is a single-word genus, one should use it; but using its definition instead does not invalidate the definition because the meaning is the same.
3
Distinguish between complete and deficient definition
A complete definition uses the proximate genus and specific differentia. If you use a distant genus or accidents, it becomes deficient (only gives essential distinction, not full correspondence).
4
Apply to a legal example
In fiqh, when defining a term like 'ghusl', if you say 'washing the whole body with water in a specific manner' instead of using a single-word genus like 'washing', it is still a valid definition as long as it meets ittiraad and in'ikaas.

Source Text

إنه مضاف إلى الحد، والإضافة التي بها يتميز حد الشيء عن سائر الحدود غنية عن التعريف لما أن طبائع النسب معلومة عند العلم بالمنسوبات. ثم التأليف بين الذاتيات لا يكفي وجوده كيف اتفق بل لا بد فيه من هيئة وترتيب، فإن معنى الحد في الذهن مثال مطابق للمحدود في الوجود فكما أن المحدود لا يوجد إلا بتأليف مخصوص وهيئة مخصوصة فكذلك الحد، وكيف لا؟ وقد كان كل واحد منهما مشتملا على الأجزاء المرتبة بعضها على البعض. فترتيب الحد هو أن يكون مسبوقا فصله أي فصل المحدود بجنسه القريب فإنه من اللوازم فإن ما يكون مقدمًا طبعًا يكون مقدمًا وضعًا. وكما أنه وهو الترتيب من اللوازم فكذلك الإطراد والانعكاس؛ إذ هما من الشرائط للحد. والحد هو الجامع المانع على ما عرف فيقال كل إنسان حيوان ناطق وكل حيوان ناطق إنسان. ولهذا لا تحتمل الوجازة ولا الإطناب المعنوي؛ إذ الإطناب يبطل الطرد كما يقال في حد الإنسان حيوان ناطق متفطن» مثلا، فإن كل حيوان بهذه الصفة. وإن كان إنسانًا فكل إنسان لا يكون حيوانا بهذه. والإيجاز يبطل العكس كما يقال حيوان متفهم مثلا؛ إذ كل إنسان. وإن كان حيوانا متفهما فكل حيوان متفهم لا يلزمه أن يكون إنسانًا. فالحد المشتمل على ما مر ذكره من الترتيب والشرط هو أن يقال حيوان ناطق وإنه حد كامل فدل على ماهية الإنسان كما هي لما أن الجنس القريب وهو الحيوان يتضمن الدلالة على تمام ما به المشاركة من الذاتيات، وفصله وهو الناطق يتضمن الدلالة على تمام ما به الممايزة منها وإنه هو الغاية في الإيجاز اللفظي، وهذا الإيجاز من اللوازم. فإن التعرض للإطناب لا يكون على وفق العقل إلا لضرورة؛ وذلك في صورة لم يوجد للجنس القريب اسم موضوع مطابق يورد ذلك ويقرن به الفصل: فيورد بدل ذلك الاسم حده كما يقال في حد الحيوان جسم ذو نفس حساس متحرك بالإرادة»؛ إذ الجسم ذو النفس حد الجنس القريب للحيوان. ثم إنه إذا كان له اسم موضوع يطابقه فإذا ترك ذلك في الذكر وأورد للحد بدله عمدًا أو سهوا فإنه لا يكون حدًّا عند بعضهم؛ فإن من شرط الحد أن يكون قولاً وجيزا. والحق فيه أن يكون حدا؛ إذ الإعراض عن الإيجاز لا يخرجه عن كونه حدا؛ وقد كان كل واحد منهما في معنى صاحبه فلا تغابن بينهما إلا في اللفظ. وأما قولهم إن من شرط الحد أن يكون قولاً وجيزا»، فذلك لا يكون كما ينبغي؛ إذ الوجيز أمر إضافي غير محدود بحد معلوم. فربَّ شيء هو وجيز بالإضافة إلى شيء طويل بالإضافة إلى شيء آخر. والأمور الإضافية من جملة ما لا يصح استعماله في تعريف ما ليس بإضافي، والحد ليس من قبيل الإضافيات. ثم الشيء الواحد لا يمكن أن يعرف تعريفا حديًّا إلا بحد واحد؛ إذ للحد بيانه وهو شيء واحد. غير أنه بحسب المعنى لا غير فإنه يمكن أن يكون متعددًا بحسب اللفظ كما مر؛ وإنه هو الحد الكامل لما أن الفصل مسبوق بجنسه القريب على ما عرف. فأما إذا كان مسبوقاً ببعض أجناسه البعيدة أو عوارضه أو غير مسبوق فإنه أي الحد ناقص لما أنه لا يستوي في جميع ذاتيات الشيء ولا يكون مطابقا له في المعنى فيحصل منه التمييز الذاتي دون (١) النسخة : لا يستو. وما أثبتناه هو الصواب كما هو الظاهر من السياق.
We say that the definition (hadd), when it becomes the defined (mahdud), its definition falls under the absolute definition; and it is a statement depicting the quiddity of a thing in the soul as has passed. So its definition is thus the absolute definition like the definition of anything else, whatever that other thing may be. And it is distinguished from other definitions only by being added to the definition, and the addition by which the definition of a thing is distinguished from other definitions is independent of definition because the natures of relations are known when the relata are known. Then, the combination of essential attributes is not sufficient by its mere existence however it happens, but rather a form and order are necessary. For the meaning of definition in the mind is an example corresponding to the defined in existence. Just as the defined does not exist except through a specific combination and a specific form, so too the definition. And how could it be otherwise? Each of them comprises parts arranged one after another. The order of the definition is that its differentia, i.e., the differentia of the defined, be preceded by its proximate genus, for this is among the necessities. For what is prior by nature is made prior in position. And just as order is among the necessities, so too are comprehensiveness (ittiraad) and conversion (in'ikaas), for they are among the conditions for the definition. The definition is that which includes and excludes, as known. So it is said: 'Every human is a rational animal, and every rational animal is a human.' For this reason, it does not tolerate conciseness (ijaaz) nor excessive verbosity (itnaab) in meaning. Indeed, verbosity invalidates comprehensiveness, as if one says in the definition of human: 'rational animal attentive' for example, for every animal with this attribute [is human]? But if it is human, not every human is an animal with this attribute. And conciseness invalidates conversion, as if one says: 'understanding animal' for example, for every human is an understanding animal, but not every understanding animal is necessarily human. So the definition comprising what has been mentioned of order and condition is that one says: 'rational animal', and it is a complete definition, for it indicates the quiddity of man as it is, because the proximate genus, which is animal, includes indication of all the essential attributes of commonality, and its differentia, which is rational, includes indication of all the essential attributes of differentiation. And it is the ultimate in verbal conciseness, and this conciseness is among the necessities. For engaging in verbosity is not in accordance with reason except out of necessity. That occurs in a situation where there is no conventional name corresponding to the proximate genus to be used together with the differentia; so one uses in its place its definition, as is said in the definition of animal: 'a body possessing a sensitive soul, moving by will' — for 'body possessing a soul' is the definition of the proximate genus of animal. Then, if there is a conventional name corresponding to it, and one omits that in mention and substitutes its definition intentionally or forgetfully, it is not a definition according to some, for among the conditions of definition is that it be a concise statement. The truth in the matter is that it is a definition, because turning away from conciseness does not remove it from being a definition; each of them is equivalent in meaning, so there is no difference except in wording. As for their saying that among the conditions of definition is that it be a concise statement, that is not as it should be, for 'concise' is a relative matter, not bounded by a known limit. For something is concise relative to one thing and lengthy relative to another. Relative matters are among what cannot be used to define what is not relative, and definition is not among relatives. Then, a single thing cannot be known by a definitional definition except through one definition, for definition has a single exposition, but only in respect of meaning. It is possible to be multiple in wording, as has passed. And this is the complete definition, because the differentia is preceded by the proximate genus as known. But if it is preceded by some distant genus or accidents, or is not preceded, then it (i.e., the definition) is deficient, because it does not cover all the essential attributes of the thing and does not correspond to it in meaning, so it yields essential distinction only. (1) [Footnote: The manuscript has 'la yastaw' (does not equal), and what we have established is correct as appears from the context.]
Source pages p. 302

Commentary
This passage defines the nature of 'hadd' (definition) itself and explains its necessary conditions. The author begins by noting that when we define 'hadd', its definition falls under the absolute definition (i.e., a statement depicting quiddity), but it is distinguished by being a second-order definition — a definition of definition. This distinction via relation (idaafa) is self-evident because relations are known once the relata are known. The core point is that a valid definition requires a specific order (tartib): the differentia must be preceded by the proximate genus. This order is a natural necessity because the defined exists with a specific composition, so its mental image (the definition) must mirror that composition. Along with order, the definition must satisfy two conditions: ittiraad (comprehensiveness: every instance of the defined is described by the definition) and in'ikaas (conversion: everything described by the definition is an instance of the defined). The author illustrates with the standard example: 'Every human is a rational animal' (ittiraad) and 'Every rational animal is a human' (in'ikaas). From this, the author argues that excessive verbosity (itnaab) breaks ittiraad — e.g., adding 'attentive' to 'rational animal' would exclude some humans (who may not be attentive) and include non-humans. Conversely, excessive conciseness (ijaaz) breaks in'ikaas — e.g., defining human as 'understanding animal' would include animals that understand but are not human (like some animals). Therefore, the ideal complete definition is simply 'rational animal', using the proximate genus and the specific differentia. However, if there is no single word for the proximate genus, its definition may be used (e.g., for 'animal', one says 'body with a soul, sensitive, moving by will'). The author then addresses a dispute: if a single-word genus exists but someone deliberately or accidentally uses its definition instead, does the result remain a definition? Some say no, because conciseness is a condition. The author argues yes, because the meaning is identical — only wording differs. He further refutes the claim that conciseness is an absolute condition, noting that 'concise' is relative. Finally, the author states that a single thing can only have one true definition in meaning (one complete exposition of quiddity), though multiple wordings are possible. The passage concludes by distinguishing a complete definition (using proximate genus and differentia) from a deficient one (using distant genera or accidents), which yields only essential distinction (tamyiz dhati) but not full correspondence.

Key Terms

الإطناب
الحد الكامل
الحد الناقص

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when is it permissible to use a multi-word description instead of a single-word genus in a definition?
A) Always, because conciseness is not a condition
B) Only when there is no single-word name for the proximate genus
C) When the definition is for a distant genus
D) Never, because it invalidates the definition

Key Takeaway
Conciseness is relative and not an absolute condition; what matters is that the definition matches the meaning of the defined, and it is acceptable to use a definition in place of a missing single-word genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'wudu'' in fiqh: 'washing the face, hands, and wiping the head and feet'. Is this a hadd or rasm? Does it satisfy the condition of conversion? (Hint: Does everything that meets this description count as wudu'? What about the intention?)

Complete vs. Incomplete Definitions: The Proximate Genus Rule
Author discusses the requirement of conciseness in definition and distinguishes complete from incomplete definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a complete definition (hadd kamil) from an incomplete definition (hadd naqis) by checking whether the proximate genus is included.

Core Idea
A complete definition always includes the proximate genus; incomplete definitions use remote genera or accidents.

In this passage, the author clarifies what makes a definition complete or incomplete. You will learn to spot the key difference: whether the definition includes the immediate genus (al-jins al-qarib) or falls back on a remote genus or accidental feature. This skill is crucial for evaluating definitions in any Islamic science.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum (term being defined)
Find the term that the author is defining. In the passage, the author uses 'human' (insan) as the example. Note that the Arabic examples for human are: 'jism natiq' (rational body), 'jawhar natiq' (rational substance), 'mutanaffis natiq' (rational breather), and 'natiq' alone.
2
Extract the genus and differentia from each candidate definition
For each proposed definition of human, identify what is given as the genus (jins) and what is given as the differentia (fasl). For example, in 'jism natiq', the genus is 'jism' (body) and the differentia is 'natiq' (rational). The author says the proximate genus of human is 'animal' (hayawan), not 'body' or 'substance'.
3
Distinguish complete vs. incomplete using the proximity rule
According to the passage, a complete definition must be preceded by the proximate genus. If the genus used is remote (e.g., body instead of animal) or if accidents are used instead of genus, the definition is incomplete. The author ranks the examples from stronger to weaker: 'jism natiq' is stronger than 'jawhar natiq', which is stronger than 'mutanaffis natiq', and 'natiq' alone is weakest. All are incomplete.
4
Understand why conciseness is not an absolute condition
The author argues that conciseness (ijaz) is relative and therefore cannot be a strict condition for definition. Two definitions that are equivalent in meaning differ only in wording; omitting the genus name and using its definition instead does not invalidate the definition. This relativizes the conciseness requirement.

الدلالة على تمام ما به الممايزة منها وإنه هو الغاية في الإيجاز اللفظي، وهذا الإيجاز من اللوازم. فإن التعرض للإطناب لا يكون على وفق العقل إلا لضرورة؛ وذلك في صورة لم يوجد للجنس القريب اسم موضوع مطابق يورد ذلك ويقرن به الفصل: فيورد بدل ذلك الاسم حده كما يقال في حد الحيوان جسم ذو نفس حساس متحرك بالإرادة» ؛ إذ الجسم ذو النفس حد الجنس القريب للحيوان. ثم إنه إذا كان له اسم موضوع يطابقه فإذا ترك ذلك في الذكر وأورد للحد بدله عمدًا أو سهوا فإنه لا يكون حدًّا عند بعضهم؛ فإن من شرط الحد أن يكون قولاً وجيزا. والحق فيه أن يكون حدا؛ إذ الإعراض عن الإيجاز لا يخرجه عن كونه حدا؛ وقد كان كل واحد منهما في معنى صاحبه فلا تغابن بينهما إلا في اللفظ. وأما قولهم إن من شرط الحد أن يكون قولاً وجيزا»، / فذلك لا يكون كما ينبغي؛ إذ الوجيز أمر إضافي غير محدود بحد معلوم. فربَّ شيء هو وجيز بالإضافة إلى شيء طويل بالإضافة إلى شيء آخر. والأمور الإضافية من جملة ما لا يصح استعماله في تعريف ما ليس بإضافي، والحد ليس من قبيل الإضافيات. ثم الشيء الواحد لا يمكن أن يعرف تعريفا حديًّا إلا بحد واحد؛ إذ للحد بيانه وهو شيء واحد. غير أنه بحسب المعنى لا غير فإنه يمكن أن يكون متعددًا بحسب اللفظ كما مر ؛ وإنه هو الحد الكامل لما أن الفصل مسبوق بجنسه القريب على ما عرف. فأما إذا كان مسبوقاً ببعض أجناسه البعيدة أو عوارضه أو غير مسبوق فإنه أي الحد ناقص لما أنه لا يستوي في جميع ذاتيات الشيء ولا يكون مطابقا له في المعنى فيحصل منه التمييز الذاتي دون معرفة الذات. وذلك مثل ما يقال في حد الإنسان جسم ناطق أو جوهر ناطق، أو يقال متنفس ناطق أو ناطق فقط. فإن كل واحد من هذه الأقوال وأمثالها حد ناقص لا يحصل منه ما يحصل من الكامل أصلا لكن الأول منها أقوى وأقرب من الكامل بالنسبة إلى الثاني والثاني بالنسبة إلى الثالث هلم جرا، وهذا ظاهر.
The indication of the entirety of what constitutes distinctiveness from it, and that it is the utmost in verbal conciseness. And this conciseness is of the necessary concomitants. For engaging in verbosity is not in accordance with reason except for necessity; that is in the case where there is no name for the proximate genus that corresponds, so that one can mention that and pair it with the differentia: then one mentions instead of that name its definition, as is said in the definition of animal: 'a body possessing a sensitive soul, moving by will'; since 'body possessing a soul' is the definition of the proximate genus of animal. Then if it has a name that corresponds, if one omits that in the mention and mentions the definition instead of it deliberately or forgetfully, then it is not a definition according to some; for one of the conditions of definition is that it be a concise statement. But the correct view is that it is a definition; for refraining from conciseness does not remove it from being a definition; and each of them is equivalent in meaning, so there is no difference between them except in wording. As for their saying that one of the conditions of definition is that it be a concise statement, that is not as it should be; for conciseness is a relative matter not limited by a known boundary. For something is concise in relation to something lengthy in relation to something else. And relative matters are among those that cannot be used in defining what is not relative, and definition is not of the category of relatives. Then one thing cannot be defined by a definitional definition except by one definition; for definition has its own expression, and it is one thing. However, in terms of meaning only, it can be multiple in terms of wording, as has passed. And it is the complete definition because the differentia is preceded by its proximate genus, as known. But if it is preceded by some of its remote genera or its accidents, or not preceded, then it, i.e. the definition, is incomplete because it does not cover all the essential properties of the thing and does not correspond to it in meaning, so from it comes essential distinction without knowledge of the essence. That is like what is said in the definition of human: 'a rational body' or 'a rational substance', or 'a rational breather', or 'rational' only. For each of these statements and their likes is an incomplete definition that does not yield what the complete definition yields at all, but the first of them is stronger and closer to the complete relative to the second, and the second relative to the third, and so on. And this is clear.
Source pages p. 303

Commentary
This passage clarifies conditions for a valid definition (hadd). The author begins by defining the goal of definition: complete indication of what distinguishes a thing, with maximum verbal conciseness. He then notes that conciseness is a necessary concomitant, but verbosity is only allowed when the proximate genus has no name. In that case, one replaces the genus name with its definition. If a genus name exists, deliberately omitting it and using its definition instead does not invalidate the definition according to the author, contrary to some who require conciseness. The author argues that conciseness is relative and cannot be an absolute condition. He then distinguishes complete from incomplete definitions: a complete definition includes the proximate genus and differentia. If a definition uses a remote genus or accidents, it is incomplete. Examples: for human, 'jism natiq' (rational body) is incomplete because body is a remote genus. The author lists 'jism natiq', 'jawhar natiq', 'mutanaffis natiq', and 'natiq' alone as increasingly incomplete. This shows that definitions vary in completeness. The key takeaway: a definition must capture all essential properties.

الإيجاز
الجنس القريب
الفصل

Incomplete Definitions and Their Epistemological Condition
Author explains incomplete definitions and their epistemological condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze the structure of incomplete definitions (hadd naqis), rank them by closeness to completeness, and explain why a definition requires prior knowledge of existence to conceptualize the essence.

Core Idea
A definition reveals quiddity only after existence; before that, it only names.

We often think a definition gives complete knowledge. But the author shows that definitions come in degrees, and more importantly, they depend on prior knowledge of existence. This lesson unpacks the hierarchy of incomplete definitions of 'man' and the subtle epistemology of tasawwur and tasdiq.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the examples of incomplete definitions of 'man'
The author lists four definitions: 'rational body', 'rational substance', 'rational breather', and 'rational'. Identify the genus and differentia in each.
The first two use a genus (body/substance) and differentia (rational); the latter two lack a clear genus.
2
Rank them in order of closeness to completeness
The author says the first is strongest, then the second, then the third. Understand why: 'rational body' specifies both matter and form, while 'rational' alone is vague.
3
Identify the key epistemological claim
The author states that a definition only indicates the quiddity for someone who already knows the thing exists. For others, it only conveys the meaning of the name.
This distinguishes two types of conceptualization (tasawwur): of the name and of the essence.
4
Examine the potential circularity and its resolution
If definition required prior conceptualization of essence, and assent requires prior conceptualization, we would have a circle. The author resolves by noting that the conceptualization prior to assent is of the name, not the essence.
Thus the conceptualization that precedes assent is not the same as the one that definition provides after existence.
5
Understand the role of accidental properties
The author adds that the prior conceptualization is often not of the true essence but of a property or accident, which still suffices for judging existence.
This further weakens the circularity objection.

معرفة الذات. وذلك مثل ما يقال في حد الإنسان جسم ناطق أو جوهر ناطق، أو يقال متنفس ناطق أو ناطق فقط. فإن كل واحد من هذه الأقوال وأمثالها حد ناقص لا يحصل منه ما يحصل من الكامل أصلا لكن الأول منها أقوى وأقرب من الكامل بالنسبة إلى الثاني والثاني بالنسبة إلى الثالث هلم جرا، وهذا ظاهر. ثم من اللوازم أن تعلم من بعد أن الحد وإن كان دالا على ماهية الشيء ومفيدا لتصور ذاته فإنه إنما يكون مفيدا بالقياس إلى من يعلم وجود الشيء. فإما بالقياس إلى من لا يعلم فإنه يدل على معنى اسم ذلك الشيء. فإذا حصل له العلم بوجود ذلك الشيء صار للحد بعينه دالا على الشيء بحسب ذات ذلك الشيء مفيدا التصوره بحسب الذات في حقه. والتصور الذي مرَّ ذكره وهو الذي يحكم عليه بتقدمه على التصديق فذلك هو التصور بحس التصور بحسب معنى الاسم لا بحسب الذات؛ إذ التصور بحسب الذات فذلك بعد العلم بوجود الشيء والتصديق به. ولا يقال إذا كان الحد لا يفيد التصور إلا بعد العلم بالوجود والتصديق به والتصديق لا يمكن إلا بعد التصور؛ فالحد لا يفيد التصور إلا بعد التصور. وذلك محال لاستحالة الدور فإن التصور الذي يفتقر إليه التصديق هو تصور معنى الاسم عند استعماله وإلا لا يمكن أن يحكم بوجوده أو بعدمه فيشترط تقدمه على التصديق بخلاف التصور بحسب الذات فإنه لا يشترط تقدمه على التصديق بل هو بعده كما مر . ثم التصور السابق على التصديق ليس من شرطه أن يكون بحيث لو علم وجود الشيء كان هو بعينه تصورًا للحقيقة ذات الشيء وماهيته بل ربما كان تصورا له من جهة لازم من لوازمه أو عارض من عوارضه كذلك. والغالب أن يكون على خلاف ما هو عليه؛ فاعتبر بما عرفت.
Knowledge of the essence. That is like what is said in the definition of man: 'a rational body' or 'a rational substance', or it is said 'a rational breather' or just 'rational'. For each of these sayings and their like is an incomplete definition; it does not yield what the complete definition yields at all. However, the first of them is stronger and closer to completeness relative to the second, and the second relative to the third, and so on. And this is apparent. Then from the necessary consequences, you should know afterwards that although the definition indicates the quiddity of the thing and benefits the conceptualization of its essence, it only benefits relative to one who knows the existence of the thing. As for relative to one who does not know, it indicates the meaning of the name of that thing. So when he gains knowledge of the existence of that thing, the same definition becomes indicative of the thing according to the essence of that thing, benefitting its conceptualization according to the essence with respect to him. And the conceptualization that was mentioned earlier—that which is judged to precede assent—that is conceptualization according to the meaning of the name, not according to the essence. For conceptualization according to the essence occurs only after knowledge of the thing and assent to it. And one should not say: If the definition does not benefit conceptualization except after knowledge of existence and assent, and assent is impossible except after conceptualization, then the definition does not benefit conceptualization except after conceptualization. That is impossible due to the impossibility of circularity. For the conceptualization that assent depends on is the conceptualization of the meaning of the name when using it; otherwise, one cannot judge its existence or non-existence. Thus its priority over assent is required, unlike conceptualization according to essence, which does not require priority over assent but rather comes after it, as mentioned. Moreover, the conceptualization prior to assent does not have to be such that if one knew the existence of the thing, it would be exactly the conceptualization of the reality of the thing's essence and quiddity. Rather, perhaps it is a conceptualization of it from the perspective of one of its necessary properties or accidents. And most often it is contrary to what it really is. So consider what you have learned.
Source pages p. 304

Commentary
This passage is a detailed explanation of the nature of definition (hadd) and its epistemological conditions. The author begins by illustrating incomplete definitions with examples of defining 'man' (insan) using different combinations of 'rational' (natiq) and 'body' (jism) or 'substance' (jawhar). He notes that incomplete definitions vary in their degree of closeness to a complete definition. Then he introduces a crucial point: a definition only provides conceptualization (tasawwur) of the essence for someone who already knows that the thing exists. For someone unaware of existence, the definition only conveys the meaning of the name (ma'na al-ism). This distinction solves a potential circularity: if definition requires prior conceptualization of existence, and assent (tasdiq) requires prior conceptualization, then definition would require conceptualization before conceptualization. The author resolves this by explaining that the conceptualization needed for assent is of the meaning of the name, not of the essence. The conceptualization of essence comes after knowledge of existence. Finally, he notes that the prior conceptualization is often not of the true essence but of some property or accident, which suffices for judging existence. This passage is crucial for understanding the relationship between definition, conceptualization, and assent in Islamic logic.

According to the author, what kind of conceptualization does a definition provide for someone who does not know the existence of the thing?
A) Conceptualization of the essence.
B) Conceptualization of the meaning of the name.
C) Conceptualization of the necessary properties.
D) No conceptualization at all.

Key Takeaway
A definition can only reveal the true essence after the existence of the thing is established; before that, it merely clarifies the meaning of the name.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Rank them by strength and justify using the author's criteria.

Hadd and Rasm
beginner
10 min
Definition and Types of Rasm (Description)
Author defines rasm and divides it into complete and incomplete.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between complete and incomplete rasm by identifying whether the definition includes the proximate genus and whether it conveys essential attributes.

Core Idea
Rasm defines through accidents, not essence; complete rasm uses genus, incomplete only distinguishes.

This passage introduces rasm (description) as a type of definition that relies on necessary properties and accidents. Unlike hadd, which reveals the essence, rasm only distinguishes the thing. The author provides multiple definitions and divides rasm into complete and incomplete.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three definitions of rasm
Read the three definitions: (1) a statement defining by specific properties and accidents, (2) a statement distinguishing from all else, (3) a statement distinguishing from others within the genus. Notice the progression from specific properties to genus-based distinction.
The third definition excludes single words.
2
Distinguish complete vs incomplete rasm
Complete rasm has its special properties preceded by the proximate genus. Incomplete rasm lacks the genus and consists only of properties. Check examples: 'laughing animal' is complete because 'animal' is the genus; 'laughing' alone is incomplete.
Complete rasm may or may not include a differentia.
3
Check for essential knowledge
The incomplete rasm does not convey any essential attributes via correspondence (mutabaqa) or inclusion (tadammun). It only provides distinction (tamyiz). Complete rasm may include some essential elements if it includes a differentia.
This is why incomplete rasm is called 'incomplete'.
4
Apply to the given examples
Analyze the examples: 'animal, laughing, disposed to knowledge, walking on two feet...' is a complete rasm because it has genus and multiple properties. 'laughing animal' is also complete but simpler. 'laughing' alone is incomplete.
Also consider the substance example: 'existent not in a subject' – this is a rasm using general accidents.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الرسم: فإنه يعرف بتعريفات مختلفة أيضًا : [أ] منها أن يقال قول معرف للشيء بما يخصه من اللوازم والأعراض، [ب] ومنها أن يقال قول معرف بجملة لشيء واحد هو المرسوم تعريفا يتميز به الشيء عن جميع ما عداه، ت] ومنها أن يقال قول مميز للشيء عما عداه من جنسه فقولنا قول مميز يُخرج المفرد عن كونه رسما . وكما أن الحد على قسمين كامل وناقص فكذلك الرسم. والكامل منه ما يكون خواصه مسبوقة بجنسه القريب؛ وإنه قد يكون فاصلا مثل قولنا في تعريف الإنسان حيوان ضحاك مستعد للعلم مشاء على قدميه عريض الأظفار بادي البشرة، وقد لا يكون مثل قولنا حيوان ضحاك مثلا . وأما الناقص فإنه قد يكون فاصلا مثل قولنا ضحاك مستعد للعلم إلى آخر ما ذكرناه من الخواص، وقد لا يكون مثل قولنا ضحاك مثلا. وإنه أي الناقص لا يفيد معرفة شيء من الذاتيات أصلا لا بطريق المطابقة ولا بطريق التضمن؛ وإنما قصارى، وإفادة التمييز. ولهذا كان ناقصا . ولا يقال كيف هو والحد على التعريف المذكور لا يكون شاملا للأقسام المذكورة فيه. وكذلك الرسم إذا التعريف المذكور على حسب الكامل في كل واحد منهما وإلا لا يكون ذلك التعريف تعريفا صحيحًا. فالناقص من الحد لا يكون جيدا بالحقيقة وإن أطلق لفظ الحد عليه ، وكذلك الناقص من الرسم. ثم الحد وإن كان أتم لكونه معرفًا ماهية الشيء على التفصيل كما هي لكن الرسم أكثر وأعم لجزئياته في البسيط وغيره. وكما أن الإنسان يعرف بالرسم فكذلك الجوهر، ولأن الرسم لا يقتصر على الواحد نحو الحد فإنه يقتصر على الفصل، وذلك واحد ليس إلا بخلاف الخاصة؛ إذ هي متعددة مفردة ومركبة والمركبة منها قد تكون من الأعراض الخاصة كما مر . وقد تكون من الأعراض العامة كما يقال في تعريف الجوهر مثلا موجود لا في موضوع»؛ إذ
As for description (rasm): it is also defined by various definitions: [A] one of them is that it is said: a statement defining the thing by what is specific to it of necessary properties and accidents. [B] And among them is that it is said: a defining statement as a whole for a single thing which is the described, a definition by which the thing is distinguished from everything other than it. [C] And among them is that it is said: a statement distinguishing the thing from whatever is other than it from its genus. So our saying 'distinguishing statement' excludes the single (word) from being a rasm. And just as hadd is of two types, complete and incomplete, likewise rasm. The complete one is that whose special properties are preceded by its proximate genus; and it may be a differentia, like our saying in the definition of man: 'animal, laughing, disposed to knowledge, walking on two feet, broad-nailed, bare-skinned', and it may not be, like our saying 'laughing animal' for example. As for the incomplete, it may be a differentia like our saying 'laughing, disposed to knowledge' up to the rest of the properties we mentioned, and it may not be, like our saying 'laughing' for example. And it (the incomplete) does not benefit knowledge of any of the essential properties at all, neither by way of correspondence nor by way of inclusion; its utmost is providing distinction. For this reason it is incomplete. And one should not say: How is it and hadd, according to the mentioned definition, does not include the mentioned divisions? And likewise rasm if the mentioned definition is according to the complete in each of them; otherwise that definition would not be a correct definition. So the incomplete of hadd is not good in reality even if the term hadd is applied to it, and likewise the incomplete of rasm. Then, although hadd is more complete because it defines the quiddity of the thing in detail as it is, yet rasm is more abundant and more general in its particulars in simple things and others. And just as man is known by rasm, so is substance. And because rasm is not restricted to a single item like hadd, for hadd is restricted to the differentia, and that is only one, unlike the special property (khassa), for they are multiple, simple and compound. And the compound of them may be from special accidents as mentioned, and may be from general accidents, as is said in the definition of substance, for example: 'existent not in a subject'.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage defines and classifies rasm (description) in detail. The author begins by providing three definitions of rasm: (1) a statement defining a thing by its specific necessary properties and accidents, (2) a statement that defines a thing as a whole distinguishing it from all else, and (3) a statement that distinguishes a thing from what is other than it within its genus. The third definition excludes single words (like proper names) from being rasm. Then, like hadd, rasm is divided into complete (kamil) and incomplete (naqis). A complete rasm is one where the special properties are preceded by the proximate genus; it may include a differentia (as in the detailed definition of man with many properties) or not (as in 'laughing animal'). An incomplete rasm lacks the genus and may consist only of properties (e.g., 'laughing, disposed to knowledge') or just one property (e.g., 'laughing'). The incomplete rasm does not convey any essential attributes; its only function is to distinguish the thing. The author then addresses a possible objection about the definitions not covering the divisions, stating that the definitions of hadd and rasm are based on the complete types, and incomplete ones are not truly good definitions even if the terms are applied to them. Finally, the author compares hadd and rasm: hadd is more complete because it reveals the quiddity in detail, but rasm is more common and general because it applies to simple things and allows multiple properties, unlike hadd which relies on a single differentia. Examples include defining man by multiple properties and substance by 'existent not in a subject' (a general accident).

Key Terms

خاصة
فصل
مطابقة
تضمن

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a complete rasm from an incomplete rasm?
A) Complete rasm uses a differentia, incomplete does not.
B) Complete rasm includes the proximate genus, incomplete lacks it.
C) Complete rasm is used for man, incomplete for substance.
D) Complete rasm is always a hadd, incomplete is not.

Key Takeaway
Rasm is a definition using accidents; complete rasm includes proximate genus, incomplete rasm only distinguishes without conveying essence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Look at the definition of 'wudu' as 'washing specific limbs with water'. Is this a complete rasm or incomplete? Explain why, using the criteria from this lesson.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate

Hadd vs Rasm: Which is More Common?
Author compares hadd and rasm in terms of comprehensiveness and frequency.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why rasm is more common than hadd in defining simple entities and provide an example from Islamic logic.

Core Idea
Hadd reveals essence; rasm works where essence is unknown, using multiple properties.

The author concludes the passage by comparing hadd and rasm. Although hadd is more complete because it defines the quiddity in detail, rasm is more abundant and general. It applies to simple things (which have no genus) and allows multiple distinguishing properties, unlike hadd which relies on a single differentia.

Guided Path
1
Recall the nature of hadd
Hadd (real definition) defines the essence of a thing by its genus and differentia. It is restricted to a single differentia.
Example: 'rational animal' for man – only one differentia.
2
Understand the nature of rasm
Rasm uses accidents and can include multiple properties (khassa). It does not require a single differentia.
Example: 'laughing animal' or 'existent not in a subject'.
3
Apply to simple entities
Simple entities (e.g., substance, qualities) cannot be defined by genus-differentia if they have no genus. They can only be defined by rasm.
The author gives substance as an example: defined by 'existent not in a subject'.
4
Compare comprehensiveness and frequency
Hadd is more complete but less common; rasm is more common because it works for more things (including simples) and allows multiple properties.
The author states: 'rasm is more abundant and more general in its particulars in simple things and others.'

[B] And among them is that it is said: a defining statement as a whole for a single thing which is the described, a definition by which the thing is distinguished from everything other than it. [C] And among them is that it is said: a statement distinguishing the thing from whatever is other than it from its genus. So our saying 'distinguishing statement' excludes the single (word) from being a rasm. And just as hadd is of two types, complete and incomplete, likewise rasm. The complete one is that whose special properties are preceded by its proximate genus; and it may be a differentia, like our saying in the definition of man: 'animal, laughing, disposed to knowledge, walking on two feet, broad-nailed, bare-skinned', and it may not be, like our saying 'laughing animal' for example. Hadd and Rasm
intermediate

Comparing Hadd and Rasm
Author compares hadd and rasm, showing rasm is more widespread.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between hadd (essential definition) and rasm (descriptive definition) and identify which type is appropriate for a given term.

Core Idea
Hadd reveals essence; rasm reveals properties – rasm is more common and often necessary.

In this passage, the author contrasts the complete definition (hadd) with the descriptive definition (rasm). Although hadd is more perfect because it details the quiddity, rasm is more widely used because it applies to both simple and composite things. Understanding when to use each is crucial for precise discourse in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
In the passage, which terms are being defined? (e.g., man, substance, accident)
2
Extract the definition type
For each, is the definition a hadd or rasm? Hadd uses a single differentia; rasm uses multiple properties.
3
Analyze the composition of rasm
Are the properties special accidents, general accidents, or a mix? Look at the examples: substance defined as 'existent not in a subject' uses a general accident.
4
Determine why rasm is more general
The passage says rasm is more numerous and general because it is not limited to one differentia and applies to simple things.
5
Apply to an Islamic science term
Choose a term like 'salat' (prayer) and try to give both a hadd and a rasm. Which one is easier?
Salat's essence involves specific actions and intentions; a rasm might be 'a set of actions starting with takbir and ending with taslim'.

ثم الحد وإن كان أتم لكونه معرفًا ماهية الشيء على التفصيل كما هي لكن الرسم أكثر وأعم لجزئياته في البسيط وغيره. وكما أن الإنسان يعرف بالرسم فكذلك الجوهر، ولأن الرسم لا يقتصر على الواحد نحو الحد فإنه يقتصر على الفصل، وذلك واحد ليس إلا بخلاف الخاصة؛ إذ هي متعددة مفردة ومركبة والمركبة منها قد تكون من الأعراض الخاصة كما مر. وقد تكون من الأعراض العامة كما يقال في تعريف الجوهر مثلا موجود لا في موضوع»؛ إذ المجموع وإن كان من خواص الجوهر فكل جزء من المجموع عرض عام يعمه وغيره. وقد يكون من الأعراض الخاصة والعامة، كما يقال في تعريف العرض موجود في شيء لا كجزء منه؛ إذ الموجود من هذا المجموع عام دون ما عداه. ثم من اللوازم أن تكون الأعراض وغيرها في التعريفات الرسمية بينة للشيء وإلا لا يحصل منها التعريف. وكيف يحصل والذهن لا ينتقل منها إليه حينئذ. فلو قلت في تعريف المثلث إنه شكل زواياه الثلاث مساوية لقائمتين لا يحصل منه التعريف إلا لأصحاب علم الهندسة لأنه لا يكون بينا لهم ولغيرهم.
Then the definition (al-hadd), even though it is more complete because it makes known the quiddity of a thing in detail as it is, yet the description (al-rasm) is more numerous and more general for its particulars in the simple (al-basit) and other things. And just as man is known by rasm, similarly is the substance (al-jawhar). And because rasm is not restricted to one [line] like hadd, for hadd is restricted to the differentia, and that is only one, unlike the special property (al-khassa); for they are multiple, simple and compound, and the compound of them may be from special accidents as has passed. And they may be from general accidents as is said in the definition of substance, for example: 'an existent not in a subject'—for the whole, even if it is from the properties of substance, each part of the whole is a general accident comprising it and other than it. And it may be from special and general accidents, as is said in the definition of accident: 'an existent in something not as a part of it'—for the existent from this whole is general without what is other than it. Then, it is necessary that the accidents and others in descriptive definitions (al-ta'rifat al-rasmiyya) be clear (bayyina) for the thing; otherwise the definition is not obtained from them. And how would it be obtained while the mind does not transfer from them to it at that time? So if you said in the definition of the triangle that it is a shape whose three angles are equal to two right angles, the definition would not be obtained except for the people of geometry, because it is not clear (bayyin) for them and for others.
Source pages p. 305

Commentary
This passage contrasts hadd with rasm. Although hadd is more complete because it gives the essence (mahiyya) in detail, rasm is more frequently used and more general because it applies to both simple and composite things. The author gives the example of man and substance (jawhar) as things that can be known by rasm. Then he explains that rasm is not limited to a single line of definition; while hadd is restricted to the single differentia (fasl), rasm can use multiple properties, both simple and compound. These properties may be special accidents (a'rad khassa) or general accidents (a'rad 'amma). For example, the definition of substance as 'an existent not in a subject' uses a general accident (since 'existent' is general). Similarly, the definition of accident as 'an existent in something not as a part of it' also uses a mixture. The author then states a crucial condition: in descriptive definitions, the accidents and other properties must be clearer (abayan) than the thing being defined. If they are not clear, the mind will not transfer from them to the defined thing. He illustrates with the definition of a triangle as 'a shape whose three angles equal two right angles'—this is only useful for those versed in geometry, not for others, because the property is not self-evidently connected to the essence of a triangle for a layperson. This condition ensures that rasm actually informs.

Key Terms
الحد (al-hadd): Essential definition; reveals the quiddity via genus and differentia.
الرسم (al-rasm): Descriptive definition; uses accidents or properties to identify the thing.
البسيط (al-basit): Simple; a thing without parts, where a full hadd may be impossible, so rasm is needed.
الجوهر (al-jawhar): Substance; an entity that exists not in a subject, used here as an example of a thing that can be defined by rasm. [Note: Standard philosophical term.]
العرض العام (al-'arad al-'amm): General accident; an accident that applies to multiple species, e.g., 'existent' in the definition of substance.
الخاصة (al-khassa): Special property; a non-essential property unique to a species, used in rasm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say rasm is more numerous/general than hadd?
A) Because hadd requires a single differentia, while rasm can use multiple properties.
B) Because hadd is only for simple things.
C) Because rasm gives essence.
D) Because rasm is always clearer.

Key Takeaway
Rasm is a flexible, often necessary tool for definition, especially when a full essential definition is impossible or impractical.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Consider the term 'wudu' (ablution). Attempt to give both a hadd (essential definition) and a rasm (descriptive definition) based on the categories in the passage. Which one is easier or more useful? Why?
?
2. Look at the definition of 'iman' (faith) in a kalam text. Is it a hadd or a rasm? Identify the properties used.

The Clearness Requirement in Rasm
Author states the requirement that properties in rasm must be clear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate whether a given rasm satisfies the condition of clearness (bayyin) and explain why unclear rasms fail to define.

Core Idea
Rasm defines only if its properties are clearer than the thing defined.

The author concludes the passage with a critical condition: the properties used in a rasm must be clearer (abayan) than the thing being defined. If they are not, the mind cannot move from the properties to the defined thing, and no definition is achieved. This lesson dissects that condition and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition stated in the passage
Look for the Arabic phrase about clearness (bayan). What does the author say?
The phrase 'بينة للشيء' appears.
2
Understand the epistemological reason
Why must the properties be clearer? Because definition is supposed to transfer the mind from unknown to known.
The author says: 'the mind does not transfer from them to it'.
3
Examine the triangle example
Why does the definition of triangle using angle sum fail for non-geometers? Because the property is not clear (bayyin) to them; it is expert knowledge.
The property 'angles equal two right angles' is not self-evident to most people.
4
Apply the condition to an Islamic example
Consider defining 'khamr' (wine) as 'an intoxicating beverage'. Is 'intoxicating' clearer than 'wine'? For most people, yes. What about defining 'salah' as 'a set of actions starting with takbir and ending with taslim'? Is that clear enough?
For a beginner, 'takbir' and 'taslim' may need further explanation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And just as man is known by rasm, similarly is the substance (al-jawhar). Commentary

Key Terms
البيان (al-bayan): Clearness; the condition that the properties used in rasm must be more known or clearer than the defined thing.
التعريف الرسمي (al-ta'rif al-rasmi): Descriptive definition; definition by properties or accidents.
المثلث (al-muthallath): Triangle; used as an example of an unclear rasm.

D) Because triangles are simple things.

Key Takeaway
A rasm must use properties that are more evident than the thing defined; otherwise, it is not a true definition.

Objection and Response: Why Multiple Properties in Definitions Matter
Objection that definition by one property suffices, and response explaining that multiple properties yield greater knowledge, plus discussion of defining simple things.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the structure of a dialectical objection-response in definition theory and explain why multiple properties can provide better knowledge.

Core Idea
A definition with one property suffices, but multiple properties give deeper knowledge.

In the science of mantiq, definitions (hadd and rasm) are meant to convey knowledge of a thing. Some argue that a single distinctive property is enough. But the author of this passage responds that knowledge has levels – different definitions provide different aspects of understanding. This lesson walks through the objection, the response, and the special case of simple entities that cannot be defined by genus and differentia.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector says: if a rasm using one property transfers the mind to the essence, then adding more properties is wasteful. Locate this claim in the Arabic.
See the opening: 'لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان التعريف بخاصة واحدة ينتقل الذهن منها إليه رسمًا'
2
Extract the author's initial response
The author counters by stating that definition is establishing knowledge of essence and attributes, and knowledge has levels (مراتب), so different definitions yield different knowledge.
The response begins with 'لكنا نقول تعريف الشيء هو إثبات معرفة ذلك الشيء بحسب ذات الشيء وصفاته'
3
Analyze the special case of simple things
Simple things (البسائط) have no parts, so they cannot have a proper hadd. The author argues that if they are defined by clear accompaniments (لوازم بينة), that rasm effectively substitutes for hadd because it achieves knowledge of the essence.
The passage says 'تعريف البسائط إذا كان بلوازم بينة ينتقل الذهن منها إلى معرفة الذات كان ذلك التعريف في حقها تعريفا'
4
Understand the broader concept of ta'rif
The author concludes that the term 'definition' (التعريف) is broader than just hadd and rasm; it includes linguistic definitions, which are more general.
The final line quotes: 'التعريف اللغوي أعم من التعريف الاصطلاحي'
5
Reflect on the practical implication
Multiple properties are not wasteful; they give different levels of knowledge. This principle applies to defining complex entities (e.g., in fiqh or kalam) where one property alone cannot capture all essential aspects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم لقائل أن يقول لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان التعريف بخاصة واحدة ينتقل الذهن منها إليه رسمًا يحصل منه المقصود وهو التعريف بما يخصه من اللوازم والأعراض وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون التعرض لما هو الزائد عليها ضائعاً خالياً عن الفائدة. لكنا نقول تعريف الشيء هو إثبات معرفة ذلك الشيء بحسب ذات الشيء وصفاته. وللمعرفة مراتب يمكن أن تحصل من المعرفة بهذا التعريف ما لا يحصل بذلك وإن كان يحصل من ذلك ما لا يحصل بهذا. واعلم بأن تعريف البسائط إذا كان بلوازم بينة ينتقل الذهن منها إلى معرفة الذات كان ذلك التعريف في حقها تعريفا. فإنما مقام الحد وإن لم يكن حدا؛ إذ المقصود من الحد معرفة ذات الشيء. وذلك حاصل في هذه الصورة فلا افتراق بينهما إذن في توصل الذهن إلى حقيقة الشيء. وعلى هذا في الغير فإن من التعريفات الرسمية ما يقوم مقام الحد وذلك بالنسبة إلى ما هو المقصود. وأما لفظ التعريف فكما أنه يطلق على الحد والرسم فكذلك يطلق على الغير؛ إذ «التعريف اللغوي أعم من التعريف الاصطلاحي»، ومن ذلك هو.
Then a speaker may say: if it were as mentioned, then the definition by a single special property from which the mind transfers to it would be a rasm that achieves the goal, which is the definition by what is specific to it from necessary accompaniments and accidents, and then it would be necessary that the attention to what is in excess of it is wasteful, empty of benefit. But we say: the definition of a thing is the establishing of knowledge of that thing according to the essence of the thing and its attributes. And knowledge has levels; it is possible that by this definition there is obtained what is not obtained by that, even though there is obtained by that what is not obtained by this. And know that the definition of simple things (al-basa'it) if it is with clear accompaniments (lawazim bayyina) from which the mind transfers to knowledge of the essence, then that definition is, in their respect, a definition; it is in the position of the hadd even though it is not a hadd; for the purpose of hadd is knowledge of the essence of the thing, and that is attained in this form, so there is no difference between them then in the mind's reaching the reality of the thing. And on this [basis] for other [things], among descriptive definitions there are those that take the place of hadd relative to the intended purpose. As for the word 'definition' (al-ta'rif), just as it is applied to hadd and rasm, so too it is applied to others; for 'the linguistic definition is more general than the technical definition,' and among that is this.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage presents an objection and its response. The objector argues: if the preceding condition is correct, then a definition by a single special property (khassa wahida) from which the mind transfers to the defined thing should be sufficient as a rasm, achieving the goal of definition by what is specific. Therefore, adding extra properties would be wasteful and pointless. The author responds by asserting that the definition of a thing is the establishment of knowledge of it according to its essence and attributes. Knowledge has levels (maratib); one definition may give knowledge that another does not, and vice versa. So multiple properties can provide a more complete knowledge. Then the author discusses simple things (al-basa'it), like substance or form, which cannot be defined by a hadd because they have no genus and differentia. For such things, if they are defined by clear accompaniments (lawazim bayyina) from which the mind transfers to the essence, that definition effectively serves as a hadd in achieving the purpose of making the essence known. Thus there is no difference between hadd and such a rasm regarding the mind's access to the reality. The author also notes that the term 'definition' (al-ta'rif) is applied more broadly, encompassing both hadd and rasm, and even other types (like linguistic definitions). This passage shows the practicality of rasm, especially for simple entities, and defends the value of multiple properties in definitions.

Key Terms
البسائط
اللوازم البينة
مراتب المعرفة
التعريف اللغوي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author's response, why might a definition with multiple properties be beneficial even if one property suffices?
A) Multiple properties are required for simple things.
B) Knowledge has levels, and different definitions provide different degrees of knowledge.
C) It is a requirement of hadd to have multiple properties.
D) A single property defines only accidents, not essence.

Key Takeaway
A definition using multiple properties is not wasteful; it enriches knowledge by addressing different aspects of the thing defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'qiyas' (analogy) in Usul al-Fiqh: 'Qiyas is the extension of a ruling from an original case to a new case due to a shared effective cause.' Identify the single property (extension of ruling) and the additional property (shared effective cause). Explain how each property contributes to knowledge, using the author's argument about levels of knowledge.
