Conditions and Epistemic Value of Rasm Definitions
Conditions for rasm definitions and their epistemic value

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the necessary condition for a valid rasm definition and explain why a rasm can function like a hadd for simple entities.

Core Idea
A clear rasm definition achieves the same epistemic goal as a hadd for simple entities.

The passage establishes that for a rasm definition to be effective, the properties used must be clear (bayyina). It then addresses an objection about using multiple properties and defends that different definitions provide different levels of knowledge. Finally, for simple entities (basa'it), a rasm with clear properties stands in the place of hadd because the goal—knowledge of the essence—is attained.

Guided Path
1
Identify the condition for rasm definitions
Find where the author states that the accidents (الأعراض) in rasm definitions must be clear (بينة) to the thing being defined, otherwise the mind cannot transfer to it.
2
Examine the objection and response
Notice the objection: if one clear property suffices, why use more? Then read the author's response: definition has levels of knowledge, and multiple properties can provide different insights.
3
Analyze equivalence for simple entities
For simple entities (البسائط), the author says a rasm with clear necessary concomitants (اللوازم البينة) stands in the place of hadd because the purpose—knowledge of the essence—is achieved.
4
Apply to an Islamic example
Think of a simple entity like the soul (النفس) in Kalam. How would you define it by a clear necessary concomitant? Does it convey the essence?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من اللوازم أن تكون الأعراض وغيرها في التعريفات الرسمية بينة للشيء وإلا لا يحصل منها التعريف. وكيف يحصل والذهن لا ينتقل منها إليه حينئذ. فلو قلت في تعريف المثلث إنه شكل زواياه الثلاث مساوية لقائمتين لا يحصل منه التعريف إلا لأصحاب علم الهندسة لأنه لا يكون بينا لهم ولغيرهم. ثم لقائل أن يقول لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان التعريف بخاصة واحدة ينتقل الذهن منها إليه رسمًا يحصل منه المقصود وهو التعريف بما يخصه من اللوازم والأعراض وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون التعرض لما هو الزائد عليها ضائعاً خالياً عن الفائدة. لكنا نقول تعريف الشيء هو إثبات معرفة ذلك الشيء بحسب [۷۹] ذات الشيء وصفاته. وللمعرفة مراتب يمكن أن تحصل من المعرفة بهذا التعريف ما لا يحصل بذلك وإن كان يحصل من ذلك ما لا يحصل بهذا. واعلم بأن تعريف البسائط إذا كان بلوازم بينة ينتقل الذهن منها إلى معرفة الذات كان ذلك التعريف في حقها تعريفا. فإنما مقام الحد وإن لم يكن حدا؛ إذ المقصود من الحد معرفة ذات الشيء. وذلك حاصل في هذه الصورة فلا افتراق بينهما إذن في توصل الذهن إلى حقيقة الشيء. وعلى هذا في الغير فإن من التعريفات الرسمية ما يقوم مقام الحد وذلك بالنسبة إلى ما هو المقصود. وأما لفظ التعريف فكما أنه يطلق على الحد والرسم فكذلك يطلق على الغير؛ إذ «التعريف اللغوي أعم من التعريف الاصطلاحي»، ومن ذلك هو «التعريف بالمثال» كتعريف العقل بالنور مثلا، وتعريف الكلي بالجزئي كتعريف الجنس بالحيوان والنوع بالإنسان، والشخص بزيد مثلا؛ ومثل هذه التعريفات هو تعريف المعقول بالمحسوس»، فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.
Then among the necessary [conditions] is that accidents and others in rasm definitions be clear (bayyina) for the thing, otherwise definition is not obtained from them. How can it be obtained when the mind does not transfer from them to it at that time? So if you said in the definition of a triangle that it is a shape whose three angles equal two right angles, definition is not obtained from it except for those skilled in geometry, because it is not clear to them and others. Then one might say: if it were as mentioned, then definition by a single property from which the mind transfers to it would be a rasm achieving the intended purpose, which is definition by what is peculiar to it of necessary concomitants and accidents; and then it would follow that engaging in what is additional to it is wasted and devoid of benefit. But we say: definition of a thing is establishing knowledge of that thing according to the essence of the thing and its attributes. Knowledge has levels; from knowledge through this definition may be obtained what is not obtained through that, though from that may be obtained what is not obtained through this. And know that the definition of simple entities (basa'it), if it is through clear necessary concomitants from which the mind transfers to knowledge of the essence, that definition is for them a definition. For it is in the position of hadd even if it is not a hadd; since the purpose of hadd is knowledge of the thing's essence. That is obtained in this case, so there is no difference between them in terms of the mind reaching the reality of the thing. And upon this [applies] to others, for among rasm definitions are those that stand in the place of hadd, relative to what is intended. And as for the word 'definition' (ta'rif), just as it is applied to hadd and rasm, so too it is applied to other [types]; indeed 'the lexical definition is more general than the technical definition.' Among that is 'definition by example,' like defining reason by light, and definition of the universal by the particular, like defining genus by 'animal' and species by 'human' and individual by 'Zayd'; and such definitions are defining the intelligible by the sensible.' So consider what you have known, and with Allah is success.
Source pages p. 306

Commentary
This passage is a discussion about the conditions of definition by description (rasm) and its relationship to true definition (hadd). The author begins by stating a necessary condition for rasm definitions: the accidents or concomitant properties used must be clear (bayyina) to the thing being defined; otherwise, the mind cannot transfer from them to the thing, and no definition is achieved. The example given is defining a triangle as 'a shape whose three angles equal two right angles'—this is only clear to those who know geometry, not to everyone. Then a potential objection is raised: if a single property suffices for a rasm definition, why include additional properties? The author responds that definition has levels of knowledge; different definitions may provide different degrees of understanding. Next, the author addresses simple entities (basa'it)—things with no conceptual parts. For them, a rasm using clear necessary concomitants can serve the same epistemic role as a hadd, because the goal of hadd is knowledge of the essence, and that is achieved. Thus, for simple entities, a well-chosen rasm is equivalent to a hadd in conveying the reality. The author then expands on the terminology of 'ta'rif' (definition): it applies not only to hadd and rasm but also to other types, such as definition by example (like defining reason by light), definition of a universal by a particular (like defining genus by 'animal'), and definition of the intelligible by the sensible. The lesson closes by urging the reader to reflect on this.

Key Terms
اللوازم (al-lawāzim): Necessary concomitants; properties that necessarily accompany an essence but are not part of its constitution. Used in rasm definitions to point to the essence. [Note: Stable term across the tradition.]
التعريف الرسمي (al-ta'rīf al-rasmī): Definition by description; identifying a thing through its peculiar accidents or necessary concomitants, not through essential parts (genus and differentia). [Note: In later texts, rasm is often contrasted with hadd as the only alternative for defining simple entities.]
البسائط (al-basā'iṭ): Simple entities; essences that have no conceptual parts (e.g., the soul, intellect). They cannot have a true hadd because they lack genus and differentia. [Note: Used consistently in post-Avicennan logic.]
الحد (al-ḥadd): True definition; a statement composed of genus and differentia that captures the essence of a composite thing. [Note: Stable in Mantiq tradition; sometimes distinguished from rasm as the only way to define composite essences.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is a rasm definition using a single clear property considered sufficient?
A) Because it is concise
B) Because the mind can transfer from the clear property to the essence, achieving the purpose of definition
C) Because a single property is always essential
D) Because rasm definitions are always better than hadd

Key Takeaway
A clear rasm definition, especially for simple entities, can achieve the same epistemological goal as a hadd.

Beyond Hadd and Rasm: Broader Types of Definition
Broader types of definition beyond hadd and rasm

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three types of definition beyond hadd and rasm mentioned in the passage and provide an Islamic example for each.

Core Idea
The term 'definition' extends beyond technical hadd and rasm to include examples, particulars, and sensible analogies.

The author expands the concept of definition (ta'rif) to include lexical definitions and other forms that are more general than the logical definition. These include definition by example (like defining reason by light), definition of the universal by a particular (like genus by 'animal'), and definition of the intelligible by the sensible. This shows that the logical tradition acknowledges multiple ways to convey meaning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the author's claim about the word definition
Find where the author says that lexical definition (التعريف اللغوي) is more general than technical definition (التعريف الاصطلاحي).
2
List the additional types of definition
Extract three types: definition by example (بالمثال), definition of universal by particular (الكلي بالجزئي), definition of intelligible by sensible (المعقول بالمحسوس).
3
Provide Islamic examples for each
For definition by example: defining 'justice' by 'the Prophet's conduct'. For universal by particular: defining 'animal' by 'human'. For intelligible by sensible: defining 'reason' by 'light' as in the passage.

ثم من اللوازم أن تكون الأعراض وغيرها في التعريفات الرسمية بينة للشيء وإلا لا يحصل منها التعريف. وكيف يحصل والذهن لا ينتقل منها إليه حينئذ. فلو قلت في تعريف المثلث إنه شكل زواياه الثلاث مساوية لقائمتين لا يحصل منه التعريف إلا لأصحاب علم الهندسة لأنه لا يكون بينا لهم ولغيرهم. ثم لقائل أن يقول لو كان كما ذكر تم لكان التعريف بخاصة واحدة ينتقل الذهن منها إليه رسمًا يحصل منه المقصود وهو التعريف بما يخصه من اللوازم والأعراض وحينئذ يلزم أن يكون التعرض لما هو الزائد عليها ضائعاً خالياً عن الفائدة. لكنا نقول تعريف الشيء هو إثبات معرفة ذلك الشيء بحسب [۷۹] ذات الشيء وصفاته. وللمعرفة مراتب يمكن أن تحصل من المعرفة بهذا التعريف ما لا يحصل بذلك وإن كان يحصل من ذلك ما لا يحصل بهذا. واعلم بأن تعريف البسائط إذا كان بلوازم بينة ينتقل الذهن منها إلى معرفة الذات كان ذلك التعريف في حقها تعريفا. فإنما مقام الحد وإن لم يكن حدا؛ إذ المقصود من الحد معرفة ذات الشيء. وذلك حاصل في هذه الصورة فلا افتراق بينهما إذن في توصل الذهن إلى حقيقة الشيء. وعلى هذا في الغير فإن من التعريفات الرسمية ما يقوم مقام الحد وذلك بالنسبة إلى ما هو المقصود. وأما لفظ التعريف فكما أنه يطلق على الحد والرسم فكذلك يطلق على الغير؛ إذ «التعريف اللغوي أعم من التعريف الاصطلاحي»، ومن ذلك هو «التعريف بالمثال» كتعريف العقل بالنور مثلا، وتعريف الكلي بالجزئي كتعريف الجنس بالحيوان والنوع بالإنسان، والشخص بزيد مثلا؛ ومثل هذه التعريفات هو تعريف المعقول بالمحسوس»، فاعتبر بما عرفت وبالله التوفيق.

The Impossibility of Defining Essence by Hadd
Author presents and refutes five ways of defining mahiyya by hadd, then begins a second objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the argument that defining mahiyya by hadd is impossible by identifying the five possible ways and explaining why each leads to circularity.

Core Idea
Defining essence by hadd is impossible because all five rational approaches collapse into circularity.

This passage presents a powerful logical objection to the very possibility of true definition (hadd). The author systematically enumerates five possible ways a definition could capture an essence, and then shows each collapses into circular reasoning (dawr) or self-definition (tahsil al-hasil). This exercise will sharpen your ability to detect circularity in any definitional argument.

Guided Path
1
List the five possible ways
The author states that a definition of mahiyya by hadd can only be one of five: by the essence itself, by all its parts, by something internal, by something external, or by a composite of internal and external. Write them down.
The enumeration is meant to be exhaustive of rational possibilities.
2
Analyze the first way: by itself
If the mahiyya is defined by itself, the definiens is identical to the definiendum. Knowledge of the definiens must be prior to knowledge of the definiendum, but they are the same, so circularity (dawr) results.
3
Analyze the second way: by all its parts
The mahiyya is the sum of its parts. Defining it by all parts makes the definiens equivalent to the definiendum, again leading to self-definition and circularity.
4
Analyze the third and fourth ways: internal and external
For internal parts: defining by one part requires defining that part (self-definition) or defining the rest (which becomes external). External definition requires the external property to be peculiar to the essence, but that peculiarity is known only after knowing the essence—circularity.
The fourth way fails because the external definer's specialization depends on prior knowledge of the mahiyya.
5
Summarize the fifth way and conclusion
The fifth way combines internal and external, inheriting both faults. Since all five ways are impossible, definition by hadd is impossible.
The author then begins a second objection about tasawwur, but that part is incomplete.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في تعريف الماهية بالحد] وقد يقال في تعريف الماهية بالحد إنه ممتنع بوجوه: [1] منها أنه لا يخلو من أن يكون بنفس الماهية [ب] أو بجميع أجزائها، [ت] أو بما يكون داخلا فيها، [ث] أو بما يكون خارجًا عنها، [ج] أو بما يكون مركباً منهما. والأول محال فإن من اللوازم أن يكون العلم بما يعرف به الماهية متقدمًا على العلم بالماهية ولا يمكن أن يكون العلم بالشيء متقدمًا على العلم بذلك الشيء. والثاني كذلك؛ إذ الماهية عبارة عن جميع تلك الأجزاء فيلزم أن يكون المعرف للماهية معرفًا لنفسه؛ وذلك باطل لما مر . وكذلك الثالث، فإن تعريف الماهية حينئذ إما بتعريف كل جزء من أجزائها، وإنه باطل فإن من المحال أن يكون ذلك الجزء معرفًا لنفسه، أو بتعريف ما عداه من الأجزاء، وإنه هو التعريف بالخارج. والتعريف بالخارج هو الرابع ، وإنه باطل أيضًا فإن الخارج عن الماهية لا يكون معرفًا إلا وأن يكون مختصا بتلك الماهية؛ وذلك لا يعرف إلا بعد معرفة الماهية فلو كانت معرفة الماهية بذلك يلزم الدور. وعلى هذا في الخامس، فإنه يشتمل على ما يشتمل عليه الثالث والرابع من الفساد؛ وبهذا يعرف الفساد في الغير من الأقسام العقلية. [1] ومنها أن ما يستفاد من الحد فذلك تصور ماهية الشيء؛ وذلك لا يمكن لما مرّ من قبل أنه لا يخلو من أن يكون مشعورًا به ؛ وإنه من جملة ما يستحيل طلبه فإن تحصيل الحاصل محال، أو لا يكون مشعورًا به : وإنه من جملة ما
Chapter: On Defining the Essence by Hadd. And it may be said that defining the essence by hadd is impossible in several ways: [1] Among them, it cannot be other than by the essence itself [b] or by all its parts [t] or by what is internal to it [th] or by what is external to it [j] or by a composite of the two. The first is impossible, for among necessary [implications] is that the knowledge of that by which the essence is defined must be prior to knowledge of the essence, and it is impossible for knowledge of a thing to be prior to knowledge of that same thing. The second is likewise; for the essence is all of those parts, so it would follow that the definer of the essence is a definer of itself; that is false as previously [mentioned]. Likewise the third, for the definition of the essence then is either by defining each part of its parts, which is false because it is impossible for that part to define itself, or by defining the other parts, which is definition by what is external. And definition by what is external is the fourth, and it is also false, for what is external to the essence cannot be a definer unless it is peculiar to that essence; and that is not known except after knowledge of the essence, so if knowledge of the essence were by that, circularity would follow. And similarly for the fifth, for it includes the corruption that the third and fourth include; and thereby the corruption is known in other rational divisions. [1] And among them, that what is gained from hadd is conceptualization (tasawwur) of the thing's essence; and that is impossible for what has passed before, because it cannot be other than either it is already perceived; and it is among what is impossible to seek, for obtaining the obtained (tahsil al-hasil) is impossible, or it is not perceived: and it is among what
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage is a new section (fasl) presenting objections to the possibility of defining an essence (mahiyya) by a true definition (hadd). The author enumerates five possible ways such a definition could be attempted, and shows each leads to absurdity. The first way is defining the essence by itself; this is impossible because the knowledge of what is used to define (the definiens) must be prior to knowledge of the defined (definiendum), but they are the same, so circular. The second way is defining by all its parts; since the essence is just the sum of its parts, this amounts to self-definition, again circular. The third way is defining by something internal (a part); either you define that part (making it definir, which is self-definition) or you define the other parts (which is definition by what is external). The fourth way is defining by something external; but an external definer must be peculiar to that essence, and its peculiarity cannot be known without prior knowledge of the essence, leading to circularity. The fifth way combines internal and external, inheriting both problems. Thus, all rational divisions for defining mahiyya by hadd are corrupt. The author then begins a second objection: what is obtained from hadd is tasawwur (conceptualization) of the thing's essence. This tasawwur either is already present (making the hadd an attempt to obtain what is already obtained—impossible) or is not present and not perceived, in which case seeking it is also impossible (the text cuts off). This second objection is incomplete in the provided text.

الماهية
الحد
الدور
تحصيل الحاصل

Key Takeaway
A definition that attempts to capture the essence cannot avoid circularity whether it uses the essence itself, its parts, internal components, external properties, or a combination.

The Five Objections to Definition by Hadd
Author enumerates five arguments against definition by hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and explain the five objections against the possibility of definition by hadd, and recognize the logical flaws (circularity, regress, etc.) in each.

Core Idea
Definition by hadd is attacked from five angles: circularity, regress, identity, intuition, and incompleteness.

In this passage, the author presents a series of five arguments that aim to show the impossibility of defining essence (mahiyya) by a real definition (hadd). These objections are foundational in Islamic logic, as they challenge the very possibility of attaining new conceptual knowledge through definition. You will analyze each objection step by step, understanding the logical structure and the specific flaws identified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the First Objection and its Five Subcases
The first objection breaks down definition into five possibilities: by the essence itself, by all its parts, by an internal part, by an external part, or by a composite. For each, note how the author shows it leads to circularity or impossibility. Focus on the reasoning that 'knowing the definer must precede knowing the defined'.
The subcases are exhaustive categories of what a definition could be.
2
Analyze the Second Objection on Conceptualization
The second objection argues that what is obtained from definition is either already known (so seeking it is superfluous) or unknown (so you cannot seek it without prior distinction). Explain why both options are impossible according to the author.
This is a dilemma: either the knowledge is already present or it is completely unknown.
3
Examine the Third Objection on Identity of Definition and Defined
The third objection asks whether the definition is identical to the thing defined or something else. If identical, knowledge of the definition cannot be prior. If different, a thing cannot be known by something else. Analyze why both horns lead to contradiction.
The author rejects the middle ground of 'both same and different' as incoherent.
4
Evaluate the Fourth Objection on Intuitive vs. Acquired Knowledge
The fourth objection says knowledge of the definition must be either intuitive (badihi) or acquired (kasbi). If intuitive, definition is unnecessary; if acquired, it leads to circularity or regress. Explain the argument for each case.
This objection assumes definition is meant to produce new knowledge, not remind of what is already known.
5
Summarize the Fifth Objection on Knowing the Exclusivity of the Differentia
The fifth objection states that definition requires knowing the differentia is exclusive to all species of that genus, which is beyond human capacity. Why does the author consider this impossible?
This objection is empirical: humans cannot survey all species.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في تعريف الماهية بالحد]
وقد يقال في تعريف الماهية بالحد إنه ممتنع بوجوه: [1] منها أنه لا يخلو من أن يكون بنفس الماهية [ب] أو بجميع أجزائها، [ت] أو بما يكون داخلا فيها، [ث] أو بما يكون خارجًا عنها، [ج] أو بما يكون مركباً منهما. والأول محال فإن من اللوازم أن يكون العلم بما يعرف به الماهية متقدمًا على العلم بالماهية ولا يمكن أن يكون العلم بالشيء متقدمًا على العلم بذلك الشيء. والثاني كذلك؛ إذ الماهية عبارة عن جميع تلك الأجزاء فيلزم أن يكون المعرف للماهية معرفًا لنفسه؛ وذلك باطل لما مر . وكذلك الثالث، فإن تعريف الماهية حينئذ إما بتعريف كل جزء من أجزائها، وإنه باطل فإن من المحال أن يكون ذلك الجزء معرفًا لنفسه، أو بتعريف ما عداه من الأجزاء، وإنه هو التعريف بالخارج. والتعريف بالخارج هو الرابع ، وإنه باطل أيضًا فإن الخارج عن الماهية لا يكون معرفًا إلا وأن يكون مختصا بتلك الماهية؛ وذلك لا يعرف إلا بعد معرفة الماهية فلو كانت معرفة الماهية بذلك يلزم الدور. وعلى هذا في الخامس، فإنه يشتمل على ما يشتمل عليه الثالث والرابع من الفساد؛ وبهذا يعرف الفساد في الغير من الأقسام العقلية.
[1] ومنها أن ما يستفاد من الحد فذلك تصور ماهية الشيء؛ وذلك لا يمكن لما مرّ من قبل أنه لا يخلو من أن يكون مشعورًا به ؛ وإنه من جملة ما يستحيل طلبه فإن تحصيل الحاصل محال، أو لا يكون مشعورًا به : وإنه من جملة ما يستحيل طلبه كذلك فإن من اللوازم أن يكون المطلوب متميزا عن غيره؛ وذلك لا يمكن عند النُّهول (۱) عنه.
[ب] ومنها أن حد الشيء لا يخلو من أن يكون عين ذلك الشيء، [ت] أو لا يكون بل غيره والأول باطل وإلا لا يمكن أن يكون العلم بالحد متقدمًا على العلم بالمحدود، وإنه متقدم على ما عرف؛ والثاني كذلك فإن الشيء لا يعرف بغيره ولا يلزم من العلم بماهية الغير العلم بماهيته أيضًا. ولا يقال يمكن أن يكون هو ولا غيره فإن المعني من الغير أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة عقلية حال عدم الإشارة إلى صاحبه.
[ث] ومنها أنه إذا أمكن تعريف الشيء بالحد فلا يخلو من أن يكون العلم بالحد علمًا بديهيا وذلك ممتنع فإن من البديهي أنه لا يكون بديهيا. ولأنه إذا كان بديهيا كان غنيا عن التعريف؛ والغني عن التعريف لا يعرف أصلا وإن كان يعرف على ما عرف، أو لا يكون بديهيا؛ وإنه ممتنع أيضًا وإلا يلزم الدور أو التسلسل كما مر .
[ج] ومنها أن التعريف بالحد لا يمكن إلا وأن يعلم بأن هذا الفصل فصل له من جميع أنواع هذا الجنس لا لغيره. وذلك لا يمكن فإنه لا يكون في وسع البشر أن يطلع على سائر الأنواع بالنسبة إلى البعض من الأجناس على الخصوص بالنسبة إلى الكل؛ وهذا ظاهر.
Section: On defining essence by definition. It may be said that defining essence by definition is impossible in several aspects: [1] Among them is that it is not free from being either by the essence itself, or by all its parts, or by something internal to it, or by something external to it, or by something composed of both. The first is impossible because necessarily knowledge of that by which the essence is known is prior to knowledge of the essence, and it is not possible for knowledge of a thing to be prior to knowledge of that thing. The second is likewise, for the essence is an expression of all those parts, so it would follow that the definer of the essence is a definer of itself; that is false for what has passed. Likewise the third, for then the definition of the essence is either by defining each of its parts, and that is false for it is impossible for that part to define itself, or by defining the other parts, and that is definition by what is external. Definition by what is external is the fourth, and it is also false, for what is external to the essence is only a definer if it is specific to that essence; and that is not known except after knowledge of the essence, so if knowledge of the essence were by that, circularity would follow. And thus for the fifth, for it contains the corruption contained in the third and fourth; and by this the corruption is known in the other intellectual divisions. [1] Among them is that what is obtained from definition is the conceptualization (tasawwur) of the essence of the thing; and that is impossible for what has passed before: it is not free from either being already known, and that is among what is impossible to seek, for obtaining the obtained is impossible, or not being known, and that is likewise among what is impossible to seek, for necessarily the sought must be distinguished from others; and that is not possible when one is heedless of it. [B] Among them is that the definition of a thing is not free from being either identical to that thing, [T] or not but other than it. The first is false, otherwise it would not be possible for knowledge of the definition to be prior to knowledge of the defined, and it is prior as known; and the second likewise, for a thing is not known by other than it, and knowledge of the essence of the other does not entail knowledge of its essence either. And it cannot be said that it can be both itself and not other, for the meaning of 'other' is that it can be pointed to by intellectual indication while not indicating its counterpart. [Th] Among them is that if defining a thing by definition were possible, it would not be free from knowledge of the definition being either intuitive; that is impossible, for it is intuitive that it is not intuitive. And because if it were intuitive, it would be independent of definition; and what is independent of definition is not known at all, even if it were known as known; or not intuitive; and that is also impossible, otherwise circularity or infinite regress would follow as before. [J] Among them is that definition by definition is not possible unless it is known that this differentia is a differentia of all the species of this genus and not of others. And that is not possible, for it is not within human capacity to know all species relative to some genera in particular relative to the whole; and this is apparent.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents a series of arguments by opponents of the logicians' claim that definition by hadd can yield new conceptual knowledge. The author lists five main objections. The first objection is subdivided into five possibilities for what the definition might consist of: the thing itself, all its parts, a part internal to it, a part external to it, or a composite of internal and external. Each is shown to lead to circularity or impossibility. The second objection argues that the knowledge gained from definition is either already known (so obtaining it is impossible) or unknown (so cannot be sought without prior distinction). The third objection considers whether the definition is identical to the thing defined or something else; both lead to problems. The fourth objection deals with whether knowledge of the definition is intuitive or acquired; both are impossible. The fifth objection points to the impossibility of knowing that a differentia is exclusive to all species of a given genus. These objections collectively aim to undermine the possibility of definition in logic.

ماهية
حد
دور
تسلسل
جزء

A) The essence is unknown, so the definition is impossible.
B) The definition would be defining itself, leading to circularity because knowledge of the definer must precede knowledge of the defined.
C) The essence has no parts, so it cannot be defined.

Key Takeaway
This passage shows the rigorous logical scrutiny applied to definitions in Islamic thought, highlighting the challenges of circularity, regress, and the limits of human knowledge in capturing essences.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Then, apply it to a specific definition in Islamic jurisprudence, such as the definition of 'qiyas' (analogical reasoning) by its genus ('ijtihad') and differentia ('applying a ruling from a base case to a new case'). The Aggregate vs. the Composite: Refuting Circularity in Definition
Author refutes the first objection by distinguishing aggregate of parts from composite whole.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between an aggregate (majmu') and a composite whole (murakkab) and explain why the objection that defining a composite essence by its parts leads to circularity is invalid.

Core Idea
A composite whole is not merely the sum of its parts; it is a new entity with its own form.

In this passage, the author refutes the first objection against definition by hadd. Critics argued that defining a thing by its parts leads to circularity. The author concedes that self-definition is impossible but denies the rest. He introduces a crucial distinction: the composite essence (murakkab) is not the same as the aggregate of parts (majmu'). Understanding this distinction is essential for evaluating the validity of definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection being refuted
Read the first sentence. The author concedes that defining a thing by itself is impossible, but denies that the other cases (including defining by parts) are impossible.
The objection likely claimed that defining by parts is circular because the parts already constitute the whole.
2
Extract the distinction between majmu' and murakkab
The author states that the composite essence is not an expression of the aggregate of parts but of what results from that aggregate. Paraphrase this distinction in your own words.
This is the core logical move.
3
Examine the example of sakanjabin
Sakanjabin (oxymel) is composed of vinegar and sugar. The author argues that the mixture is not the same as the set of its ingredients. Why? Because it is a new substance with its own properties.
This example makes the distinction concrete.
4
Apply the distinction to the objection
If the composite is not the aggregate, then defining the composite by its parts does not define the aggregate, so there is no circularity. The definition captures the new form that arises from the parts.
Thus, the objection fails.
5
Summarize the logical move
The author uses denial (man') and a counterexample to block the objection. The key is that the whole is more than the sum of its parts.
This distinction is a standard tool in Islamic logic.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لكنا نقول في الأول من هذه الوجوه إن الأول منه وهو تعريف الشيء بنفسه؛ فذلك مسلم بأنه لا يمكن البتة فأما ما عداه من الأقسام فكلها في حيز المنع؛ وذلك في الثاني منها ظاهر؛ إذ الماهية المركبة من الأجزاء لا تكون عبارة عن مجموع تلك الأجزاء بل هي عبارة عما يحصل من مجموع تلك الأجزاء. فالسكنجبين مثلا وإن كان مركبًا من الخل والسكر فلا تكون بنفسها نفس مجموع أجزاء الخل والسكر وإلا لوجد حيث ما وجد ذلك المجموع؛ وليس
But we say regarding the first of these aspects: the first of them, which is defining the thing by itself, that is conceded as absolutely impossible. As for the other divisions, they are all within the domain of denial. And that is apparent in the second of them, for the composite essence is not an expression of the aggregate of those parts, but rather an expression of what results from the aggregate of those parts. For example, sakanjabin, even though it is composed of vinegar and sugar, is not itself the same as the aggregate of the parts of vinegar and sugar; otherwise it would be found wherever that aggregate is found, and it is not.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is the author's response to the first objection against definition by hadd. The first sub-case—defining a thing by itself—is conceded as impossible. However, the other sub-cases (defining by all parts, by an internal part, by an external part, or by a composite) are all denied. The author explains that a composite essence is not identical to the mere sum (aggregate) of its parts; rather, it is the resultant whole from that aggregate. He gives the example of sakanjabin (oxymel), which is composed of vinegar and sugar, but the mixture itself is not the same as the set of its ingredients. If it were, oxymel would exist wherever vinegar and sugar are present together, which is false. Thus, defining the essence by its parts does not entail defining the aggregate, and circularity is avoided. The argument hinges on the distinction between 'majmu' (aggregate) and 'murakkab' (composite), a key logical principle.

مجموع
مسلم
منع
سكنجبين

A) Because the parts are unknown.
B) Because the composite is not the aggregate.
C) Because self-definition is possible.

Key Takeaway
The composite essence is the resultant whole from its parts, not the aggregate of parts, so defining by parts does not lead to circularity.

Refuting Objections Against Definition (Hadd)
Author refutes five objections against definition by hadd.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify five objections against definition by hadd and reconstruct the author's refutation for each.

Core Idea
Definition survives attack: the author defeats five objections by logical distinction.

The author lists five objections that seem to show definition (hadd) is impossible. These objections are not straw men; they are serious challenges from within the logical tradition. The author responds point by point, conceding only where necessary and showing that the objections either misunderstand definition or confuse practical difficulty with logical impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five objections from the labels
In the Arabic, find the letters [ب], [ت], [ث], [ج] that introduce each objection. For each, state in one sentence what the objection claims about definition.
The first objection is about self-definition; the second is the dilemma of same vs. other; the third is about self-evidence or regress; the fourth is about knowing the differentia for all species; the fifth (not labeled) is about practical impossibility.
2
Analyze the author's response to the first two objections
Notice that the author concedes only the first part (self-definition is impossible) but denies the rest. For the second objection, he uses the example of oxymel (sakanjabin) to show that a composite essence is not merely the sum of its parts, so definition by parts is not circular.
3
Examine the third objection: self-evidence or regress
The author argues that knowledge of definition is acquired (kasbi) and terminates in self-evident principles, thus no infinite regress. This is a standard epistemological move in Islamic logic.
4
Consider the fourth objection: exhaustive knowledge of species
The author responds that this objection only shows practical impossibility (ta'addhur), not absolute impossibility (imtina'). Therefore, definition is still possible in principle, even if humans cannot fully survey all species.
This distinction is crucial: many objections in logic are about difficulty, not logical impossibility.
5
Summarize the author's overall strategy
Notice how the author concedes the weakest point, uses examples, and carefully defines terms like 'external attributes' and 'necessary concomitants' to preserve the possibility of definition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستحيل طلبه كذلك فإن من اللوازم أن يكون المطلوب متميزا عن غيره؛ وذلك لا يمكن عند النُّهول عنه. [ب] ومنها أن حد الشيء لا يخلو من أن يكون عين ذلك الشيء، [ت] أو لا يكون بل غيره والأول باطل وإلا لا يمكن أن يكون العلم بالحد متقدمًا على العلم بالمحدود، وإنه متقدم على ما عرف؛ والثاني كذلك فإن الشيء لا يعرف بغيره ولا يلزم من العلم بماهية الغير العلم بماهيته أيضًا. ولا يقال يمكن أن يكون هو ولا غيره فإن المعني من الغير أنه يمكن أن يشار إليه إشارة عقلية حال عدم الإشارة إلى صاحبه. [ث] ومنها أنه إذا أمكن تعريف الشيء بالحد فلا يخلو من أن يكون العلم بالحد علمًا بديهيا وذلك ممتنع فإن من البديهي أنه لا يكون بديهيا. ولأنه إذا كان بديهيا كان غنيا عن التعريف؛ والغني عن التعريف لا يعرف أصلا وإن كان يعرف على ما عرف، أو لا يكون بديهيا؛ وإنه ممتنع أيضًا وإلا يلزم الدور أو التسلسل كما مر . [ج] ومنها أن التعريف بالحد لا يمكن إلا وأن يعلم بأن هذا الفصل فصل له من جميع أنواع هذا الجنس لا لغيره. وذلك لا يمكن فإنه لا يكون في وسع البشر أن يطلع على سائر الأنواع بالنسبة إلى البعض من الأجناس على الخصوص بالنسبة إلى الكل؛ وهذا ظاهر. لكنا نقول في الأول من هذه الوجوه إن الأول منه وهو تعريف الشيء بنفسه؛ فذلك مسلم بأنه لا يمكن البتة فأما ما عداه من الأقسام فكلها في حيز المنع؛ وذلك في الثاني منها ظاهر؛ إذ الماهية المركبة من الأجزاء لا تكون عبارة عن مجموع تلك الأجزاء بل هي عبارة عما يحصل من مجموع تلك الأجزاء. فالسكنجبين مثلا وإن كان مركبًا من الخل والسكر فلا تكون بنفسها نفس مجموع أجزاء الخل والسكر وإلا لوجد حيث ما وجد ذلك المجموع؛ وليس كذلك. وكذلك في الثالث منها فإنه لا يتم إلا وأن لا يمكن تعريف الماهية بالأمور الخارجية عنها؛ وذلك في حيز المنع أيضًا على الخصوص بذلك الوجه الذي ذكرتموه. فإن من الجائز أن تكون الماهية موصوفة بصفة معلومة يلزم من العلم بها العلم باختصاص غيرها بتلك الماهية فيعلم اختصاصه بها وإن لم تكن هي معلومة وعلى هذا في الرابع والخامس اللهم إلا أن يقال تعريف الماهية إن أمكن تعريفها فلا يمكن إلا بالأمور الداخلة فيها كلا وجملة؛ إذ الخارجية مما يميزها عن الغير لا غير فيقال هب أنه كذلك لكن لم قلتم بأن التعريف بالأمور الداخلة غير ممكن بل هو ممكن ؛ إذ التعريف الحدي هو تفصيل ما دل الاسم عليه بطريق الإجمال وذلك بمعزل عما ذكرتم من الفساد. وأما في الثاني من الوجوه فنقول إنه من جملة ما قد مرَّ الكلام فيه من قبل. ولأنه لا يتم إلا وأن يكون لازم الشيء منافيًا لملزومه. فإن استحالة الطلب إذا كان من لوازم كونه مشعورًا به کان عدم کونه مشعورًا به من لوازم الاستحالة. ولو كان كما ذكرتم لكان هذا اللازم منافيا لملزومه وهو الاستحالة. وأما في الثالث فنقول إنه أي الحد غير المحدود لفظاً لا معنى ولو كان كذلك فلا يستحيل أن يعرف به؛ إذ التعريف هو البيان في الحقيقة. والبيان بحسب اللفظ لا محالة فيلزم من العلم به العلم بذلك إذن؛ فهو معناه. وأما في الرابع فنقول إنه وهو العلم بالحد لا يكون بديهيا بل كسبيا ولا يلزم منه الدور أو التسلسل لما أنه ينتهى إلى أمر لا يكون ذلك الأمر كسبيًا بل بديهيا كما في سائر الأمور الكسبية. وأما في الخامس فنقول إنه يدل على كونه متعذرًا لا على كونه ممتنعا والكلام في كونه ممتنعا؛ إذ كونه متعذرًا من جملة المسلمات فلا نزاع فيه أصلا؛ إذا عرفت هذا فنقول ما يعرف بالحد فذلك لا يخلو من أن يكون بسيطاً أو لا يكون بل يكون مركبا. والبسيط إما أن يتركب منه غيره وإما أن لا يتركب.
Its request is likewise impossible, for among the requirements is that the requested be distinguished from others; and that is not possible when neglecting it. [B] Among them is that the definition of a thing cannot but be either the very thing itself, [T] or something else. The first is false, otherwise knowledge of the definition could not precede knowledge of the defined, yet it does precede as known. The second is also false, for a thing is not known by what is other than it, nor does knowledge of another's essence necessitate knowledge of its own essence. Nor can it be said that it can be both itself and other, for 'other' means that it can be pointed to mentally when its companion is not pointed to. [Th] Among them is that if definition of a thing by hadd were possible, knowledge of the definition would either be self-evident—impossible, for it is self-evident that it is not self-evident; because if it were self-evident it would be independent of definition, and what is independent of definition is not known at all, though it is known as known—or not self-evident, which is also impossible, otherwise circularity or infinite regress would follow as before. [J] Among them is that definition by hadd is possible only if it is known that this differentia is a differentia of this species of all the types of this genus and not of another; that is impossible, for it is not within human capacity to survey all species relative to some genera, especially relative to all; this is clear. But we say regarding the first of these points: the first part, which is defining a thing by itself, is conceded to be absolutely impossible. As for the other divisions, they are all in the realm of being denied. That is clear in the second of them, for the composite essence of parts is not an expression of the sum of those parts but rather an expression of what results from the sum of those parts. For example, oxymel (sakanjabin), although composed of vinegar and sugar, is not itself the same as the sum of the parts of vinegar and sugar; otherwise it would exist wherever that sum exists, but it is not so. Likewise in the third of them, it cannot be completed unless it is impossible to define the essence by external matters; that is also in the realm of being denied, specifically in the manner you mentioned. For it is possible that the essence is characterized by a known attribute such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of the exclusivity of other things to that essence, so one knows its exclusivity to it even if the essence itself is not known. Similarly for the fourth and fifth, unless it is said that defining the essence, if possible, can only be by the internal matters, totally and collectively, for external matters only distinguish it from others—it is said: let it be so, but why do you claim that definition by internal matters is impossible? Rather it is possible, for the definition by hadd is the detailing of what the name indicates in a summary way, and that is far from the corruption you mentioned. As for the second of the points, we say: it is among what has been discussed before. For it cannot be completed unless a necessary concomitant of a thing is contrary to its necessitated. For if the impossibility of seeking is among the concomitants of its being perceived, then not being perceived would be among the concomitants of impossibility. And were it as you mentioned, this necessary concomitant would be contrary to its necessitated, which is impossibility. As for the third, we say: the definition is other than the defined in wording, not in meaning. And if it were so, it is not impossible to be known by it, for definition is explanation in reality. Explanation is necessarily by wording, so knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of that then; so it is its meaning. As for the fourth, we say: knowledge of the definition is not self-evident but acquired, and neither circularity nor infinite regress follows from it because it ends at something that is not acquired but self-evident, as in other acquired matters. As for the fifth, we say: it indicates impossibility in actuality, not impossibility in itself. The discussion is about impossibility in itself; its actual impossibility is among the conceded matters, so there is no dispute in it at all. If you understand this, we say: what is known by definition is either simple or composite. The simple is either compounded from something else or not compounded.
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage constitutes a comprehensive refutation of five objections raised against the possibility of definition (hadd) in logic. The author first enumerates the objections, labeled [ب], [ت], [ث], [ج], each attacking different aspects of definition: (1) the demand that the defined be distinct, (2) the dilemma that definition is either the same as the defined or something else (both problematic), (3) the claim that knowledge of definition would be self-evident (absurd) or lead to regress, (4) the requirement to know the differentia applies only to the species of that genus (beyond human capacity). The author then responds point by point. Regarding the first objection, he concedes that self-definition is impossible but denies that other forms of definition (e.g., by parts or external attributes) are invalid. He illustrates with 'oxymel' (sakanjabin): the composite essence is not merely the sum of its ingredients but what results from their combination, so definition via parts is not circular. For the second, he argues that a thing can be known by its necessary concomitants (lawaazim) without requiring prior knowledge of its essence, sidestepping the dilemma. For the third, he clarifies that definition differs in wording, not meaning, so it can convey knowledge. For the fourth, he asserts that knowledge of definition is acquired (kasbi), ending in self-evident principles, thus avoiding regress. For the fifth, he distinguishes between practical impossibility (ta'addhur) and absolute impossibility (imtina'), noting that the objection only shows the former, which is conceded. He then concludes with a classification of definienda: either simple (basit) or composite (murakkab). The simple further divides into that from which others are composed (like genus) and that which is not (like individuals). This classification prepares the ground for further discussion on how definitions apply to different types of beings. The author's approach is dialectical: he acknowledges the force of objections but carefully delineates the scope of definition to preserve its utility in logic and the sciences.

الماهية
البديهي
الكسبي

Classifying Definieda: Simple and Composite
Author classifies definienda into simple and composite.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish simple from composite definienda and determine whether a given entity can be defined by hadd.

Core Idea
Only composites can be defined by genus and differentia; simples are indefinable.

Not everything can be defined by hadd. The author now classifies what can be defined: it must be composite, i.e., made of genus and differentia. Simples, whether they are components of others (like genus) or standalone individuals, cannot be defined because they lack internal structure. This classification is essential for understanding the scope and limits of definition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the classification in the text
Find the sentence: 'ما يعرف بالحد فذلك لا يخلو من أن يكون بسيطاً أو لا يكون بل يكون مركبا' – what is known by definition is either simple or composite.
2
Define simple and composite in your own words
Simple means not composed of parts; composite means having parts (genus and differentia). The author gives an example of composite: oxymel (sakanjabin) from vinegar and sugar.
3
Distinguish two subtypes of simple
Some simples are components of composites (like genus or differentia) and some are not (like individual substances, e.g., a specific person).
4
Explain why simples cannot be defined by hadd
Definition by hadd requires detailing parts; if something has no parts, it cannot be detailed. Simples can only be pointed to or described by their effects (rasm).
5
Apply to an Islamic science example
In Usul al-Fiqh, 'wajib' is a composite definiendum: its genus is 'hukm taklifi' and differentia is 'reward for doing, punishment for leaving'. But a specific instance like 'the prayer of Fajr' is simple and cannot be defined by hadd.

أن لا يتركب.
Its request is likewise impossible, for among the requirements is that the requested be distinguished from others; and that is not possible when neglecting it. [B] Among them is that the definition of a thing cannot but be either the very thing itself, [T] or something else. The first is false, otherwise knowledge of the definition could not precede knowledge of the defined, yet it does precede as known. The second is also false, for a thing is not known by what is other than it, nor does knowledge of another's essence necessitate knowledge of its own essence. Nor can it be said that it can be both itself and other, for 'other' means that it can be pointed to mentally when its companion is not pointed to. [Th] Among them is that if definition of a thing by hadd were possible, knowledge of the definition would either be self-evident—impossible, for it is self-evident that it is not self-evident; because if it were self-evident it would be independent of definition, and what is independent of definition is not known at all, though it is known as known—or not self-evident, which is also impossible, otherwise circularity or infinite regress would follow as before. [J] Among them is that definition by hadd is possible only if it is known that this differentia is a differentia of this species of all the types of this genus and not of another; that is impossible, for it is not within human capacity to survey all species relative to some genera, especially relative to all; this is clear. But we say regarding the first of these points: the first part, which is defining a thing by itself, is conceded to be absolutely impossible. As for the other divisions, they are all in the realm of being denied. That is clear in the second of them, for the composite essence of parts is not an expression of the sum of those parts but rather an expression of what results from the sum of those parts. For example, oxymel (sakanjabin), although composed of vinegar and sugar, is not itself the same as the sum of the parts of vinegar and sugar; otherwise it would exist wherever that sum exists, but it is not so. Likewise in the third of them, it cannot be completed unless it is impossible to define the essence by external matters; that is also in the realm of being denied, specifically in the manner you mentioned. For it is possible that the essence is characterized by a known attribute such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of the exclusivity of other things to that essence, so one knows its exclusivity to it even if the essence itself is not known. Similarly for the fourth and fifth, unless it is said that defining the essence, if possible, can only be by the internal matters, totally and collectively, for external matters only distinguish it from others—it is said: let it be so, but why do you claim that definition by internal matters is impossible? Rather it is possible, for the definition by hadd is the detailing of what the name indicates in a summary way, and that is far from the corruption you mentioned. As for the second of the points, we say: it is among what has been discussed before. For it cannot be completed unless a necessary concomitant of a thing is contrary to its necessitated. For if the impossibility of seeking is among the concomitants of its being perceived, then not being perceived would be among the concomitants of impossibility. And were it as you mentioned, this necessary concomitant would be contrary to its necessitated, which is impossibility. As for the third, we say: the definition is other than the defined in wording, not in meaning. And if it were so, it is not impossible to be known by it, for definition is explanation in reality. Explanation is necessarily by wording, so knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of that then; so it is its meaning. As for the fourth, we say: knowledge of the definition is not self-evident but acquired, and neither circularity nor infinite regress follows from it because it ends at something that is not acquired but self-evident, as in other acquired matters. As for the fifth, we say: it indicates impossibility in actuality, not impossibility in itself. The discussion is about impossibility in itself; its actual impossibility is among the conceded matters, so there is no dispute in it at all. If you understand this, we say: what is known by definition is either simple or composite. The simple is either compounded from something else or not compounded.

Commentary
This passage constitutes a comprehensive refutation of five objections raised against the possibility of definition (hadd) in logic. The author first enumerates the objections, labeled [ب], [ت], [ث], [ج], each attacking different aspects of definition: (1) the demand that the defined be distinct, (2) the dilemma that definition is either the same as the defined or something else (both problematic), (3) the claim that knowledge of definition would be self-evident (absurd) or lead to regress, (4) the requirement to know the differentia applies only to the species of that genus (beyond human capacity). For the third, he clarifies that definition differs in wording, not meaning, so it can convey knowledge. For the fourth, he asserts that knowledge of definition is acquired (kasbi), ending in self-evident principles, thus avoiding regress. For the fifth, he distinguishes between practical impossibility (ta'addhur) and absolute impossibility (imtina'), noting that the objection only shows the former, which is conceded. The author's approach is dialectical: he acknowledges the force of objections but carefully delineates the scope of definition to preserve its utility in logic and the sciences.

Refuting Objections to Defining Essence by Internal Matters
Author refutes five objections against defining essence (mahiyya) via internal matters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and evaluate the author's refutations of five objections to defining an essence by its internal constituents.

Core Idea
Definition by internal matters is possible; objections confuse necessary concomitants, word and meaning, and impossibility with difficulty.

In this passage, the author systematically refutes five objections against the possibility of defining an essence (mahiyya) by its internal constituents. He uses key logical tools such as the distinction between a necessary concomitant (lazim) and its subject (malzum), the difference between word and meaning, and the distinction between difficulty and impossibility. Understanding these refutations equips you to defend rational definitions in Islamic theological and juristic debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five objections
Scan the Arabic for the sequential refutations: first (implied via 'likewise'), second (already discussed), third (definition by internal matters impossible), fourth (circularity/infinite regress), fifth (difficulty/impossibility). Each objection attacks the viability of defining essence internally.
The first objection is not explicitly stated but referenced; focus on the explicit refutations.
2
Analyze the refutation of the third objection
The objector claims that since the definition (hadd) differs in wording from the defined, it cannot convey knowledge. The author replies that the difference is only in word, not meaning. Definition is explanation (bayan) in reality, and by explaining the meaning through different words, knowledge is conveyed.
3
Examine the refutation of the fourth objection
The objection is that acquired knowledge of the definition leads to circularity or infinite regress. The author responds that acquired knowledge terminates at intuitive (badihi) knowledge, just like all other acquired matters. Thus, no vicious regress occurs.
4
Understand the refutation of the fifth objection
The objector argues definition is impossible. The author distinguishes between difficulty (ta‘adhdhur) and impossibility (imtina‘). The discussion is about impossibility, and everyone agrees that definition of sensible essences is difficult, but that does not make it impossible.
5
Apply the dialectical structure
Notice how the author uses the terms 'lazim' (necessary concomitant) and 'malzum' in the second objection's refutation: the impossibility of seeking is a concomitant of being perceived, and its negation would conflict with the subject. This shows the logical relationship between a property and its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Likewise. And likewise regarding the third of them, for it is not complete except that it is impossible to define the essence by matters external to it; and that is in the scope of denial also, specifically in that manner which you mentioned. For it is possible that the essence is described by a known attribute such that knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of the specificity of something else to that essence, so its specificity to it is known even if it (the essence) is not known. And similarly for the fourth and fifth, unless it is said: definition of the essence, if it is possible to define it, is only possible by the matters internal to it, entirely and collectively; for external matters only distinguish it from others, nothing more. So it is said: grant that it is so, but why have you said that definition by internal matters is impossible? Rather it is possible; for the real definition (al-ḥadd) is the detailing of what the name indicates in an allusive way, and that is remote from the corruption you mentioned. As for the second of the aspects, we say: it is among what has already been discussed before. And because it is not complete except that the necessary concomitant (lāzim) of a thing is incompatible with its subject (malzūm). For if the impossibility of seeking is among the concomitants of its being perceived, then its not being perceived is among the concomitants of impossibility. And if it were as you mentioned, then this concomitant would be incompatible with its subject, which is impossibility. As for the third, we say: the ḥadd is different from the limited in word, not in meaning. And if it were so, it is not impossible to know by it; for definition is explanation in reality. Explanation by word is inevitable, so knowledge of it necessitates knowledge of that, then it is its meaning. As for the fourth, we say: knowledge of the ḥadd is not intuitive but acquired, and it does not necessitate circularity or infinite regress because it terminates at something that is not acquired but intuitive, as in other acquired matters. As for the fifth, we say: it indicates that it is difficult (mut‘adhdhir), not impossible (mumtani‘), and the discussion is about its impossibility; for its difficulty is among the accepted premises, so there is no dispute about it at all.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical refutation of five objections against the possibility of defining an essence (mahiyya) by its internal constituents. The author addresses each objection in turn. The first objection (implied) is about external matters; the author argues that an external attribute may lead to knowledge of the essence's specificity even if the essence itself is unknown. The second objection is dismissed as already covered. The third objection confuses word and meaning: the real definition (ḥadd) differs in wording but not in meaning, so it can convey knowledge. The fourth objection about circularity is answered by noting that acquired knowledge ends at intuitive knowledge. The fifth objection distinguishes between difficulty and impossibility: the impossibility of defining sensibles is not the issue. The refutation uses technical terms like lāzim (necessary concomitant) and malzūm, and emphasizes that ḥadd is a detailed explanation of what the name signifies summarily.

التعريف الحدي
اللازم

C) He claims that all definitions are intuitive.

Key Takeaway
The author shows that objections to defining essence by internal matters are based on confusions that can be resolved by proper logical distinctions.

The Four Kinds of Definable Things: Simples and Composites
Classification of things definable by hadd into four categories based on simplicity/composition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any definable entity into one of four categories based on its simplicity/composition and its role in composition with other things.

Core Idea
Not everything can be defined by hadd; simples are defined by rasm, composites by hadd.

The author explains that things known by hadd are either simple or composite. But there are further distinctions: whether the simple enters into composition, and whether the composite itself enters into composition with others. This yields a fourfold classification that clarifies the scope and limitation of real definition.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish simple from composite
Identify whether the thing has parts (genus and differentia) or not. Simples have no parts, composites have parts.
Simples cannot be defined by hadd, but can be described by rasm.
2
For simples, check if it composes others
If a simple is a component in another thing, it can define others (as a part) but cannot be defined by hadd itself.
Example: The simple 'body' (jism) can be a component of 'animal', but body itself is not defined by hadd.
3
For composites, check if it is composed from others
If a composite is composed from simpler parts, it can be defined by those parts via hadd. If it also composes others, it can both define and be defined.
A composite that is not a component of others can only be defined by its parts.
4
Apply the four-case classification
Match the entity to one of the four categories: (1) simple not composing others, (2) simple composing others, (3) composite not composing others, (4) composite composing others. Determine what kind of definition applies.
Only composites in categories 3 and 4 can be defined by hadd.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إذا عرفت هذا فنقول ما يعرف بالحد فذلك لا يخلو من أن يكون بسيطاً أو لا يكون بل يكون مركبا. والبسيط إما أن يتركب منه غيره وإما أن لا يتركب. وكذلك المركب فيكون على أربعة أقسام: فالذي لا يتركب منه غيره من البسائط فلا يمكن تعريفه بالغير ولا تعريف الغير به كذلك. والذي يتركب منه غيره منها فلا يمكن تعريفه بالغير لكن يمكن تعريف الغير به. والذي لا يتركب منه غيره من المركبات فلا يمكن تعريف الغير به لكن يمكن تعريفه بالغير، والذي يتركب غيره منها فإنه مما يمكن تعريفه بالغير ويمكن تعريف الغير به كذلك. والمراد من التعريف في هذه الصور هو التعريف الحدي؛ إذ التعريف الرسمي شامل للبسائط وغيرها. والحد للنوع الواحد كما مرَّ من قبل واحد ليس إلا. وكما أن ماهية النوع لا تكون قابلة للزيادة والنقصان؛ فكذلك حده على خلاف الرسم فإنه يمكن أن يكون متعددًا، وقابلا للزيادة والنقصان على ما عرف
When you know this, we say: that which is known by ḥadd is either simple or not, but composite. The simple is either that from which something else is composed or not. Likewise the composite: thus it falls into four divisions: That which is simple and nothing else is composed from it — it cannot be defined by another, nor can another be defined by it. That which is simple and something else is composed from it — it cannot be defined by another, but another can be defined by it. That which is composite and nothing else is composed from it — another cannot be defined by it, but it can be defined by another. That which is composite and something else is composed from it — it can be defined by another and another can be defined by it. What is meant by 'definition' in these cases is the real definition (al-ḥadd); for the descriptive definition (al-rasm) includes simples and others. The ḥadd for a single species, as previously mentioned, is one and only one. And just as the essence of a species does not admit increase or decrease, so too its ḥadd, unlike the rasm, can be multiple and admit increase or decrease, as is known.
Source pages p. 309

Commentary
This passage provides a classification of things according to whether they can be defined by a real definition (ḥadd). The author first distinguishes between simple and composite. Then, based on whether the thing is a component of others (for simples) or composed from others (for composites), four cases arise: (1) Simple that does not enter into composition: neither defines nor is defined by another. (2) Simple that enters into composition: cannot be defined (since it is simple), but can define others (as a component). (3) Composite that is not a component of others: can be defined by another (its components), but cannot define others. (4) Composite that is a component of others: can both define and be defined. The author clarifies that this classification applies only to ḥadd, not rasm, because rasm can apply even to simples. Furthermore, ḥadd is unique for each species and does not admit variation, unlike rasm which can be multiple and flexible. The passage emphasizes that the essence (māhiyya) of a species is fixed, so its real definition must also be fixed.

Key Terms
بسيط
مركب
التعريف الحدي
التعريف الرسمي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is true according to the classification?

B) A composite that composes others can neither define nor be defined.
C) A composite that does not compose others can be defined by another.
D) The rasm is unique for each species just like the hadd.

Key Takeaway
The classification of definable things into four kinds based on simplicity/composition and participation in composition determines whether a thing can be defined by hadd, by rasm, or both.

The Indefinability of Sensibles and Inner Experiences
Lists examples of simples that are needless of definition: sensibles and wijdāniyyāt (inner experiences).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which things are needless of definition according to Islamic logicians and explain why they cannot be defined.

Core Idea
Sensibles and inner experiences cannot be defined because any definition would be either more obscure or equally unknown.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle in the theory of definition: some simple concepts are so basic that they cannot be defined. The author lists sensibles (like colors and tastes) and inner experiences (like knowledge and joy) as examples. Understanding why they are indefinable helps us avoid futile attempts at defining the obvious.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two categories of simples
Find in the passage the two groups of things that are needless of definition: sensibles (al-maḥsūsāt) and inner experiences (al-wijdāniyyāt).
Sensibles include colors, lights, tastes, smells. Inner experiences include knowledge, power, will, etc.
2
Understand why they cannot be defined
The author argues that any attempt to define them would use terms that are either more obscure (akhfā) than the thing itself, or equally known (mustawiya fī al-ma‘rifa). Neither leads to a genuine definition.
Definition requires moving from the unknown to the known; here no such move is possible.
3
Refute the objection of definition by intuitive matters
One might suggest defining sensibles by appealing to intuitive intellectual concepts (badīhiyyāt). The author rejects this because intuitive matters are intellectual (ma‘qūlāt) whereas sensibles are not known through the intellect.
Sensibles are known only through sense perception, not by reason.
4
Distinguish real definition from linguistic explanation
The author notes that definition concerns the reality (ḥaqīqa) and essence (māhiyya) of a thing, not its word. A thing may be known in itself but its name may be unknown to someone. In that case, a linguistic equivalent suffices, e.g., explaining 'layth' as 'asad'.
This shows that linguistic definition is different from real definition; real definition is impossible for these simples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فما يكون غنيا عن التعريف من البسائط ويفهم أنه لا يكون غنيًّا فذلك متعدد غاية التعداد نحو المحسوسات من الألوان والأضواء والطعوم والروائح وغيرها، أو الوجدانيات كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة والشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة والسرور والغضب وأمثالها فإنه لا يمكن تعريفها بغيرها؛ والغير أخفى منها لا محالة، ولا بالبعض منها كذلك؛ إذ هي متساوية في المعرفة ولا يقال يمكن أن يكون ذلك الغير من البديهيات؛ وذلك هو الأقوى؛ إذ البديهيات من المعقولات، والمحسوس لا يعرف بالمعقول. ثم التعريف بحسب حقيقة الشيء وماهيته لا بحسب الألفاظ الدالة عليها فإن من الممكن أن يكون الشيء معلوما من حيث هو ولا يكون معلوما من حيث اللغة أي لغة كانت فيسأل بما هو ويجاب بغير تلك اللغة؛ وذلك من الألفاظ ما هو أعرف عند السائل بالنسبة إلى غيره كما في الليث أنه أسد، وفي الغيث أنه مطر.
As for what is needless of definition among simples, and it is understood that it is not needless — that is numerous, extremely numerous, such as sensibles: colors, lights, tastes, smells, etc., or internal experiences (wijdāniyyāt) like knowledge, power, will, appetite, aversion, pain, pleasure, joy, anger and the like. For they cannot be defined by something else; the other would inevitably be more obscure than them, nor by some of them similarly, because they are equal in knowledge. And it cannot be said that that other can be from among the intuitive matters; that is the strongest, for intuitive matters are intellectual, and the sensible is not known by the intellectual. Then definition is according to the reality of the thing and its essence, not according to the words indicating it. For it is possible that a thing is known in itself but not known in terms of language, whatever language it may be; so one asks about it as it is and answers in a different language. And among words, some are more known to the questioner relative to others, like 'layth' (lion) being 'asad' (lion), and 'ghayth' (rain) being 'maṭar' (rain).
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage identifies things that are needless of definition (mustaghniyya 'an al-ta'rīf) among simples. The author gives two categories: (1) sensibles (maḥsūsāt) such as colors, lights, tastes, smells; (2) inner experiences (wijdāniyyāt) such as knowledge, power, will, appetite, aversion, pain, pleasure, joy, anger. These cannot be defined because any alternative expression would be either more obscure (akhfā) than the thing itself or equally known (mustawiya fī al-ma‘rifa). Attempting to define them by intuitive matters (badīhiyyāt) fails because intuitive matters are intellectual (ma‘qūlāt), while sensibles are not known through the intellect directly. The author then shifts to a linguistic point: definition concerns the reality of the thing, not its word. A thing may be known in itself but not by its name in a particular language. In such cases, one can answer with a more familiar term in another language, e.g., explaining 'layth' as 'asad' (both meaning 'lion') or 'ghayth' as 'maṭar' (both meaning 'rain'). This illustrates that linguistic definition differs from real definition.

Key Terms
المحسوسات
الوجدانيات
البديهيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because they are too complex.
B) Because any defining term would be either more obscure or equally known.
C) Because they are not known at all.
D) Because they are intellectual concepts.

Key Takeaway
Sensibles and inner experiences are indefinable because any definition would be either more obscure or equally known, and they cannot be defined by intellectual concepts either.

Hadd vs Rasm: Unique Essence vs Multiple Descriptions
Distinguishes hadd from rasm and their properties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a real definition (hadd) and a descriptive definition (rasm) in Islamic logic and identify which one applies to a given definition of an Islamic concept.

Core Idea
A species has only one real definition but many descriptive definitions.

In this passage, the author distinguishes between two types of definition: al-ta'rif al-haddi (the real definition) and al-ta'rif al-rasmi (the descriptive definition). The real definition captures the essence of a species and is unique, while the descriptive definition can vary and includes accidents. This distinction is crucial in Islamic jurisprudence and theology when defining concepts such as 'ibadah or 'ilm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of definition
Find where the author mentions al-ta'rif al-haddi and al-ta'rif al-rasmi. Note that the real definition is intended for composites, while the descriptive definition includes simples.
The passage says: 'والمُراد من التعريف في هذه الصور هو التعريف الحدي؛ إذ التعريف الرسمي شامل للبسائط وغيرها.'
2
Compare uniqueness
The real definition is only one per species, just as the quiddity of a species is not subject to increase or decrease. In contrast, the descriptive definition can be multiple and can vary.
Check the phrase: 'والحد للنوع الواحد كما مرَّ من قبل واحد ليس إلا.'
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the definition of 'salah' (prayer). Its real definition (hadd) would be something like 'specific actions and words beginning with takbir and ending with taslim with the intention of worship' – that is one. But its descriptive definition (rasm) could be multiple, e.g., 'the pillar of Islam', 'a means of nearness to Allah', etc.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وكذلك المركب فيكون على أربعة أقسام: فالذي لا يتركب منه غيره من البسائط فلا يمكن تعريفه بالغير ولا تعريف الغير به كذلك. والذي يتركب منه غيره منها فلا يمكن تعريفه بالغير لكن يمكن تعريف الغير به. والذي لا يتركب منه غيره من المركبات فلا يمكن تعريف الغير به لكن يمكن تعريفه بالغير، والذي يتركب غيره منها فإنه مما يمكن تعريفه بالغير ويمكن تعريف الغير به كذلك. والمراد من التعريف في هذه الصور هو التعريف الحدي؛ إذ التعريف الرسمي شامل للبسائط وغيرها. والحد للنوع الواحد كما مرَّ من قبل واحد ليس إلا. وكما أن ماهية النوع لا تكون قابلة للزيادة والنقصان؛ فكذلك حده على خلاف الرسم فإنه يمكن أن يكون متعددًا، وقابلا للزيادة والنقصان على ما عرف. فما يكون غنيا عن التعريف من البسائط ويفهم أنه لا يكون غنيًّا فذلك متعدد غاية التعداد نحو المحسوسات من الألوان والأضواء والطعوم والروائح وغيرها، أو الوجدانيات كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة والشهوة والنفرة والألم واللذة والسرور والغضب وأمثالها فإنه لا يمكن تعريفها بغيرها؛ والغير أخفى منها لا محالة، ولا بالبعض منها كذلك؛ إذ هي متساوية في المعرفة ولا يقال يمكن أن يكون ذلك الغير من البديهيات؛ وذلك هو الأقوى؛ إذ البديهيات من المعقولات، والمحسوس لا يعرف بالمعقول. ثم التعريف بحسب حقيقة الشيء وماهيته لا بحسب الألفاظ الدالة عليها فإن من الممكن أن يكون الشيء معلوما من حيث هو ولا يكون معلوما من حيث اللغة أي لغة كانت فيسأل بما هو ويجاب بغير تلك اللغة؛ وذلك من الألفاظ ما هو أعرف عند السائل بالنسبة إلى غيره كما في الليث أنه أسد، وفي الغيث أنه مطر.
And likewise the composite is of four divisions: That which does not compose anything else from among the simples cannot be defined by another, nor can another be defined by it. That which composes something else from them cannot be defined by another, but another can be defined by it. That which does not compose anything else from among the composites cannot define another, but can be defined by another. And that which composes something else from them can be defined by another and can define another similarly. What is meant by definition in these cases is the real definition (al-ta'rif al-haddi); indeed the descriptive definition (al-ta'rif al-rasmi) includes simples and others. And the real definition for a single species, as previously mentioned, is only one. And just as the quiddity of a species is not susceptible to increase and decrease, so too its real definition, contrary to the descriptive definition, which can be multiple and susceptible to increase and decrease as is known. Thus what is independent of definition from among simples—and it is understood that it is not independent—is extremely numerous, such as sensibles like colors, lights, tastes, smells, and others, or internal sensations like knowledge, power, will, desire, aversion, pain, pleasure, joy, anger, and their likes, for they cannot be defined by something else; the other is necessarily more obscure than them, nor by some of them, because they are equal in knowledge. And it cannot be said that that other could be from among self-evident things; that is the strongest [objection], for self-evident things are among the intelligibles, and the sensible is not known by the intelligible. Then definition is according to the reality and quiddity of a thing, not according to the words indicating it, for it is possible that a thing is known in terms of itself and not known in terms of the language, whatever language it may be, so one asks about it as it is and answers in a different language. And among words, what is more known to the questioner relative to others, such as for 'lion' that it is 'asad', and for 'rain' that it is 'matar'.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage defines the types of definition by exploring which composites and simples can define or be defined. The author first divides composites into four categories based on whether they compose something else. For each, he specifies whether they can be defined by something else or define something else. The real definition (hadd) is intended here, not descriptive definition (rasm), which can apply to any thing. A real definition of a species is unique, just as the essence of a species is not subject to variation. In contrast, the descriptive definition can be multiple and admit variation. The author then explains that simples—like sensory qualities and internal sensations—cannot be defined at all, because any attempt would use something either more obscure or equally known, and they cannot be defined by self-evident intelligibles because the sensible is not known through the intelligible. Finally, he distinguishes definition by essence from definition by words: a thing may be inherently known but not known in a particular language, so one can answer using a different word that is more familiar to the questioner, like explaining 'lion' as 'asad'.

Key Terms
التعريف الحدي
التعريف الرسمي
الماهية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) One
B) Many
C) As many as its properties
D) None

Key Takeaway
A species has exactly one real definition capturing its essence, but can have many descriptive definitions including accidents.

Identify whether each of the following is a hadd or rasm: (a) 'Wudu is washing the face and hands to the elbows and wiping the head and feet' (b) 'Wudu is a purification ritual that requires water', and explain why referring to the passage.
