Why Simples Cannot Be Defined
Explains why simples cannot be defined and lists examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why simple entities like sensory qualities and internal sensations cannot be defined, and apply this reasoning to Islamic theological concepts.

Core Idea
Simples are known directly and cannot be defined because any definition would be less clear.

The passage explains that certain things—sensory qualities like colors and tastes, and internal sensations like knowledge and anger—cannot be defined. These are called 'simples' (basa'it). They are directly perceived or internally experienced, so any attempt to define them using other terms would either use something more obscure or equally known. This insight is important in Islamic theology, where concepts like 'qudra' (power) are often considered simple and known by experience, not by definition.

Guided Path
1
Identify the category of simples
Find where the author lists examples of simples: 'المحسوسات من الألوان والأضواء والطعوم والروائح' and 'الوجدانيات كالعلم والقدرة والإرادة...'
Simples are divided into sensory (hissiyyat) and internal (wijdaniyyat).
2
Understand why they cannot be defined
The author gives two reasons: (1) The defining term is necessarily more obscure (أخفى) than the simple, so it doesn't clarify. (2) Using part of the simple itself is impossible because the parts are equally known. Also, you cannot use self-evident intelligibles because the sensible is not known through the intelligible.
Key phrase: 'فإنه لا يمكن تعريفها بغيرها؛ والغير أخفى منها لا محالة'.
3
Apply to an Islamic example
Consider the concept of 'iman' (faith). It is a simple internal state. You cannot define it by something else (like 'belief' or 'trust') without making it obscure. Instead, it is directly known to the believer. Similarly, 'wajd' (spiritual ecstasy) is a simple sensation.

Source Text

And likewise the composite is of four divisions: That which does not compose anything else from among the simples cannot be defined by another, nor can another be defined by it. That which composes something else from them cannot be defined by another, but another can be defined by it. That which does not compose anything else from among the composites cannot define another, but can be defined by another. And that which composes something else from them can be defined by another and can define another similarly. What is meant by definition in these cases is the real definition (al-ta'rif al-haddi); indeed the descriptive definition (al-ta'rif al-rasmi) includes simples and others. And the real definition for a single species, as previously mentioned, is only one. And just as the quiddity of a species is not susceptible to increase and decrease, so too its real definition, contrary to the descriptive definition, which can be multiple and susceptible to increase and decrease as is known. Thus what is independent of definition from among simples—and it is understood that it is not independent—is extremely numerous, such as sensibles like colors, lights, tastes, smells, and others, or internal sensations like knowledge, power, will, desire, aversion, pain, pleasure, joy, anger, and their likes, for they cannot be defined by something else; the other is necessarily more obscure than them, nor by some of them, because they are equal in knowledge. And it cannot be said that that other could be from among self-evident things; that is the strongest [objection], for self-evident things are among the intelligibles, and the sensible is not known by the intelligible. Then definition is according to the reality and quiddity of a thing, not according to the words indicating it, for it is possible that a thing is known in terms of itself and not known in terms of the language, whatever language it may be, so one asks about it as it is and answers in a different language. And among words, what is more known to the questioner relative to others, such as for 'lion' that it is 'asad', and for 'rain' that it is 'matar'.
Source pages p. 310

Commentary
This passage defines the types of definition by exploring which composites and simples can define or be defined. The author first divides composites into four categories based on whether they compose something else. For each, he specifies whether they can be defined by something else or define something else. The real definition (hadd) is intended here, not descriptive definition (rasm), which can apply to any thing. A real definition of a species is unique, just as the essence of a species is not subject to variation. In contrast, the descriptive definition can be multiple and admit variation. The author then explains that simples—like sensory qualities and internal sensations—cannot be defined at all, because any attempt would use something either more obscure or equally known, and they cannot be defined by self-evident intelligibles because the sensible is not known through the intelligible. Finally, he distinguishes definition by essence from definition by words: a thing may be inherently known but not known in a particular language, so one can answer using a different word that is more familiar to the questioner, like explaining 'lion' as 'asad'.

Key Terms
البسائط
المحسوسات
الوجدانيات
التعريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why can simples not be defined by other things?
A) They are too complex
B) The other thing is necessarily more obscure or equally known
C) They are from the intelligible world
D) They are not real

Key Takeaway
Simples like sensory qualities and internal sensations are known directly and cannot be defined because any definition would be either more obscure or equally known.

Why Definition Is Not Demonstrable
States that definition is not demonstrable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a real definition (hadd haqiqi) cannot be proven through demonstration (burhan) and why it is immune to dialectical objections (man' and mu'arada).

Core Idea
Definition is immediate knowledge of essence, not a conclusion deduced from premises.

This passage establishes a key epistemological boundary: definition is not the kind of knowledge that can be acquired through demonstrative reasoning. It also clarifies why real definitions cannot be challenged by the standard dialectical methods of 'prevention' (man') and 'opposition' (mu'araḍa). Understanding this distinction prevents confusion between the tools of definition and the tools of proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central claim
Read the first sentences: 'As for definition, it is not capable of being acquired by demonstration.' This is a negative claim about the origin of definitional knowledge.
2
Understand why burhan cannot yield a definition
Burhan (demonstration) works by deducing a conclusion from premises. A definition, however, is a direct grasp of essence, not a conclusion. The author refers to a later discussion in the Book of Demonstration for the full proof.
3
Recognize immunity to man' and mu'araḍa
The author argues that real definitions are not subject to prevention or opposition because only compound definitions (assembled from parts) can be challenged that way. A real definition is not compound in the relevant sense.
4
Connect to the Islamic sciences
Consider an example from usul al-fiqh: the definition of 'ibada (worship) cannot be proven by a syllogism; it is known directly from Revelation and language. This illustrates why definition is distinct from demonstration.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في أن الحد فإنه من حيث هو هو لا يكون قابلاً لأن يكتسب بالبرهان ] أما الحد فإنه من حيث هو هو لا يكون قابلا لأن يكتسب بالبرهان؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد في كتاب البرهان، ولا يكون قابلا / للمنع أيضًا؛ إذ القابل هو [۸۱] المركب الحدي ليس إلا والحد ليس كذلك كما مر. وكذلك لا يكون قابلا للمنع والمعارضة؛ إذ القابل لا يكون حدًّا فلا اعتراض عليه إذن بطريقي المنع والمعارضة بل الاعتراض عليه وعلى غيره وهو الرسم على حسب مداخل الخلل فيهما لا على حسب الخلل ؛ إذ لا خلل فيهما إذا كان كل واحد منهما حقيقيا. .. ثم المداخل وهي مواضع الغلط متعددة فيهما على الخصوص في الحدود؛ إذ الحدود على القانون الذي تقرر من قبل وتحرر من جميع ما يكون من المقومات وترتيبها ترتيبًا خاصًا من الأمور التي يصعب إثباتها لما أن العثور على جميع المقومات من الأجناس والفصول وفصول الأجناس وأجناس الفصول في كل نوع متعذر فربما كان للنوع فصول فإذا وجد بعضها وفصل التمييز وقع الظن في الأكثر من الصور بأن لا فصل له غير ذلك، وكذلك الوقوف على الجنس القريب فربما يؤخذ البعيد على اعتقاد أنه قريب. وربما اشتبهت اللوازم البينة للنوع بذاتياته فيؤخذ بدل الذاتيات، وهذه كلها من جملة ما يستلزم الخلل في الحدود والأمور المستلزمة للخلل في الحدود بل فيها وفي الرسوم لا تكون مختصة بالألفاظ دون المعاني، أو بالعكس بل هي إما لفظية كما إذا كانت اللفطة مستعارة بعيدة عن الفهم أو غريبة وحشية، أو مجازية مثلا . فإن استعمال مثل هذه الألفاظ لا يكون مفيدا بل المفيد هو أن يستعمل من الألفاظ المعتادة المناسبة لما هو المقصود منها. وإن لم يوجد فليخترع من أشد الألفاظ مناسبة. وإما معنوية وإنها إما أن تكون مختصة
Section: [That definition, in itself, is not capable of being acquired by demonstration] As for definition, it is not capable of being acquired by demonstration; that is known later in the Book of Demonstration. It is also not subject to prevention; for what is subject is only the compound definition, but definition is not like that, as has passed. Likewise it is not subject to prevention and opposition; for what is subject is not a definition, so there is no objection to it via the methods of prevention and opposition. Rather, the objection is to it and to others, i.e., the descriptive definition, according to the entries of defect in them, not according to the defect itself; for there is no defect in them if each is real. Then the entries—which are the places of error—are numerous in both, especially in real definitions; for real definitions, according to the law established earlier and freed from all that are the constitutives and their arrangement in a particular arrangement, are among the things difficult to establish because finding all the constitutives from the genera and differentiae and the differentiae of genera and genera of differentiae in every species is impossible. So perhaps a species has differentiae, and if some of them are found and the distinguishing differentia, one thinks in most cases that it has no other differentia. Likewise, ascertaining the proximate genus: perhaps one takes the remote one believing it to be proximate. And perhaps the necessary concomitants of the species are confused with its essential properties, so they are taken in place of the essential properties. And all these are among the things that necessitate defect in definitions. And the things necessitating defect in definitions—rather in both definitions and descriptive definitions—are not confined to words without meanings or vice versa. Rather, they are either verbal, like when a word is a borrowed term far from understanding, or strange and unfamiliar, or metaphorical, for example. For using such words is not beneficial; rather, what is beneficial is to use customary words appropriate to what is intended. And if such are not found, let one invent from the most appropriate words. And either conceptual, and they are either specific...
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage first asserts that a real definition (hadd) cannot be obtained through demonstration (burhan), a point to be elaborated later. It also states that definition is not subject to 'prevention' (man') or 'opposition' (mu'araḍa) because only compound definitions can be challenged, and a real definition is not compound in the same way. Then the author enumerates sources of error in definitions, particularly real definitions. It is difficult to establish a correct definition because one must discover all the genera and differentiae, including the differentiae of genera and genera of differentiae, for every species—a daunting task. A common error is to assume a species has only one differentia after finding a partial one. Another error is mistaking the remote genus for the proximate one. A third is confusing necessary concomitants (lawazim bayyina) with essential properties (dhatiyyat). All these cause defects in definitions. Finally, the author classifies defects as either verbal or conceptual. Verbal defects involve using obscure, unfamiliar, or metaphorical words; instead, one should use customary and appropriate words. Conceptual defects are introduced but the text cuts off before explaining them fully.

البرهان
المنع
المعارضة

A) Because definitions are always compound statements.
B) Because definition is immediate and not a syllogistic conclusion.
C) Because demonstration only works on accidental properties.
D) Because definitions are arbitrary linguistic conventions.

Key Takeaway
Real definition is not demonstrable; it is known directly and not subject to dialectical objection.

Verbal and Conceptual Sources of Error in Definition
Lists verbal and conceptual sources of error in definitions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify verbal and conceptual sources of error that undermine definitions and distinguish between necessary concomitants (lawazim) and essential properties (dhatiyyat).

Core Idea
The greatest errors in defining a species come from mistaking the remote genus for the proximate, and confusing necessary properties with essential ones.

After establishing that definitions are not acquired through demonstration, the author turns to the practical challenges of constructing correct definitions. He lists several sources of error: the difficulty of discovering all genera and differentiae, mistaking the remote genus for the proximate, and confusing necessary concomitants with essential properties. These are classified into verbal and conceptual categories, though the conceptual section is truncated. This lesson helps you recognise these pitfalls.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main categories of error
The author states that 'the entries—which are the places of error—are numerous'. He groups them into verbal (alfaz) and conceptual (ma'ani). Verbal errors involve the words used; conceptual errors involve the meaning.
2
Understand verbal defects
Verbal defects include using rare, unfamiliar, or metaphorical words. The remedy is to use customary and appropriate words. If none exist, invent new ones that are as appropriate as possible.
3
Analyse conceptual defects: genus errors
One conceptual error is taking the remote genus as if it were proximate. Example: defining 'human' as 'living body' instead of 'rational animal'. The proximate genus is 'animal'.
4
Analyse conceptual defects: confusing concomitants with essentials
Another error is confusing necessary concomitant properties (lawazim bayyina) with essential properties (dhatiyyat). For instance, 'capable of laughter' is a necessary property of humans, not an essential differentia.
5
Apply to a real example
Take the definition of 'wine' (khamr) in fiqh. If one defines it as 'an intoxicating drink', the property of intoxication is a necessary concomitant, not the genus (which is 'drink made from grapes'). Recognising this prevents errors.

Why Definition Cannot Be Proven or Refuted Like a Syllogism
Author states that definition cannot be gained through demonstration and is not subject to certain objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why a definition (hadd) cannot be the conclusion of a demonstrative syllogism and why it is immune to two common dialectical objections.

Core Idea
Definitions are simple essences; demonstration works with composites, so definitions are neither proven nor refuted by syllogism.

You might think that every claim in the Islamic sciences can be proven or disproven by a logical demonstration. But the classical logicians held that definitions—the very building blocks of concepts—are not subject to such proof or refutation. In this lesson, we examine the author's argument that a true definition (hadd) is simply not the kind of thing that can be acquired by demonstration (burhan) nor attacked by prevention (man‘) or opposition (mu‘āraḍa).

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between simple and composite statements
The author says a definition is not composite (مركب) like a proposition. Recognize that a proposition has subject and predicate and can be true or false, while a definition gives the essence without asserting a truth value.
2
Understand the scope of demonstration
Demonstration (burhan) is a syllogism that yields certainty. It must have premises that are known and follow a valid form. Its conclusion is always a composite proposition, because a syllogism combines propositions.
3
Trace the author's argument: definitions are not demonstrable
Since a definition is simple, it cannot be the conclusion of a syllogism. The author says this will be clarified later in the book of demonstration. Accept it as a foundational principle.
4
Understand why definitions are immune to man‘ and mu‘āraḍa
Man‘ (prevention) denies a premise; mu‘āraḍa (opposition) provides a counter-example. Both target composite propositions. A definition is not composite, so these objections are inapplicable. However, definitions can be criticized for errors in content (e.g., wrong genus or differentia).
This is a key distinction between logical form and content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما الحد فإنه من حيث هو هو لا يكون قابلا لأن يكتسب بالبرهان؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد في كتاب البرهان، ولا يكون قابلا للمنع أيضًا؛ إذ القابل هو المركب الحدي ليس إلا والحد ليس كذلك كما مر. وكذلك لا يكون قابلا للمنع والمعارضة؛ إذ القابل لا يكون حدًّا فلا اعتراض عليه إذن بطريقي المنع والمعارضة بل الاعتراض عليه وعلى غيره وهو الرسم على حسب مداخل الخلل فيهما لا على حسب الخلل؛ إذ لا خلل فيهما إذا كان كل واحد منهما حقيقيا.
As for the definition, it in itself is not capable of being acquired through demonstration; that is known later in the book of demonstration. Nor is it capable of being prevented [by objection]; for that which is capable is the composite proposition, and the definition is not like that, as has passed. Likewise, it is not capable of being prevented or opposed; for that which is capable is not a definition, so there is no objection to it by the two methods of prevention and opposition. Rather, objection to it and to other things, which is the description, is according to the sources of error in them, not according to the error itself; for there is no error in them if each one of them is true.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage establishes a key epistemological claim: a definition (حد) cannot be obtained through demonstration (برهان). The author explains that demonstration deals with composite statements (قضايا مركبة), which are capable of being proven or refuted. A definition, being a simple account of essence, is not composite, so it cannot be the subject of a demonstrative proof. Likewise, it cannot be refuted by two common dialectical objections: prevention (منع), which denies a premise, and opposition (معارضة), which provides a counter-argument. These objections apply only to composite propositions. Since a definition is not composite, such objections are irrelevant. However, a definition can still be criticized based on errors (خلل) in its content, not in its logical form. The same applies to descriptions (رسوم). The author emphasizes that if both definition and description are true (حقيقي), there is no inherent error in them; any criticism must refer to external sources of error (مداخل الخلل).

Key Terms
حد
برهان
منع
معارضة
رسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author say that a definition (حد) cannot be acquired through demonstration (برهان)?
A) Because demonstration deals with composite propositions, while a definition is simple.
B) Because definitions are always known by intuition.
C) Because demonstration only applies to material objects.
D) Because definitions are impossible to attain.

Key Takeaway
A true definition (hadd) is a simple account of essence and therefore cannot be the product of a demonstrative syllogism nor be refuted by the dialectical moves of prevention or opposition; criticism must target the content, not the logical form.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Verbal Errors in Definitions
Author discusses verbal errors in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify verbal errors in definitions and correct them by choosing appropriate words.

Core Idea
A definition's words must be clear, customary, and appropriate.

Definitions can fail not only due to logical mistakes but also due to poor word choice. The author distinguishes verbal errors from conceptual ones and warns against using far-fetched metaphors, strange terms, or ambiguous expressions. This lesson focuses on recognizing and correcting such verbal errors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word type
Look at the words used in the definition. Is there a metaphor, a strange word, or a common word?
2
Assess clarity
Determine whether the word is understood by the intended audience. If it is obscure, it may be an error.
3
Determine if replaceable
Check if a more customary word can be used without changing the meaning.
4
Apply the rule
If the word is inappropriate, replace it with a customary term, or coin a new one if needed.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم المداخل وهي مواضع الغلط متعددة فيهما على الخصوص في الحدود؛ إذ الحدود على القانون الذي تقرر من قبل وتحرر من جميع ما يكون من المقومات وترتيبها ترتيبًا خاصًا من الأمور التي يصعب إثباتها لما أن العثور على جميع المقومات من الأجناس والفصول وفصول الأجناس وأجناس الفصول في كل نوع متعذر فربما كان للنوع فصول فإذا وجد بعضها وفصل التمييز وقع الظن في الأكثر من الصور بأن لا فصل له غير ذلك، وكذلك الوقوف على الجنس القريب فربما يؤخذ البعيد على اعتقاد أنه قريب. وربما اشتبهت اللوازم البينة للنوع بذاتياته فيؤخذ بدل الذاتيات، وهذه كلها من جملة ما يستلزم الخلل في الحدود والأمور المستلزمة للخلل في الحدود بل فيها وفي الرسوم لا تكون مختصة بالألفاظ دون المعاني، أو بالعكس بل هي إما لفظية كما إذا كانت اللفطة مستعارة بعيدة عن الفهم أو غريبة وحشية، أو مجازية مثلا. فإن استعمال مثل هذه الألفاظ لا يكون مفيدا بل المفيد هو أن يستعمل من الألفاظ المعتادة المناسبة لما هو المقصود منها. وإن لم يوجد فليخترع من أشد الألفاظ مناسبة. وإما معنوية وإنها إما أن تكون مختصة بالحدود أو مختصة بالرسوم أو لا تكون مختصة بهما بل هي مشتركة بينهما: أما مختصة بالحدود فإنها على وجوه؛ منها أن يوضع غير الجنس مكان الجنس فيؤخذ من اللوازم العامة مكان الجنس نحو الواحد أو الموجود مثلا مكان الجوهر والجسم وغير ذلك من الأجناس؛ أو يؤخذ العرض مكان الجنس في تعريف ما يكون تحت الكم أو الكيف أو غير ذلك من الأعراض، والعرض لا يكون لها كما علمت بل هو من اللوازم؛ أو يؤخذ الفصل مكان الجنس نحو الإفراط مكان المحبة فيقال في العشق إنه إفراط المحبة، والإفراط هو الفصل؛ أو يؤخذ النوع مكان الجنس فيقال في الشر إنه ظلم الناس، والظلم نوع من الشر؛ أو يؤخذ جنس مكان جنس نحو الكم مكان الكيف في الزوجية فيقال إنها كمية ينقسم بها المحل بقسمين متساويين، والزوجية ليست بكمية بل هي كيفية مختصة بالكمية كما مر. ومن هذا القبيل أيضًا إذا أخذت القوة بدل الملكة فيقال مثلا المتصف بصفة العفة هو الذي يقوى على اجتناب اللذات الشهوانية، أو الملكة بدل القوة كما يقال القادر على الظلم هو الذي من شأنه وطباعه النزوع إلى انتزاع ما ليس له من يد غيره: وهذا ملكة الظلم لا القدرة على الظلم؛ فإن القادر على الظلم قد يكون عادلا؛ أو تؤخذ المادة مكان الجنس فيقال في السيف إنه حديد ممتد يُقطع به، والحديد هو المادة؛ أو يؤخذ الجزء مكان الجنس فيقال في الحيوان إنه جسم ذو نفس والجسم جزء للحيوان لا جنس. ولا يظن أنه مناقض لما قيل من قبل إن الجسم جنس للحيوان فإنه لا يناقضه، وقد كان من الممكن أن يوجد الجسم باعتبار لا يكون بذلك الاعتبار إلا جزءا. وذلك لا يمكن أن يكون محمولاً على الحيوان لما أن الجزء لا يحمل على الكل البتة، فلا يكون جنسًا له إذن وإن يؤخذ باعتبار لا يكون بذلك الاعتبار إلا جنسًا.
Then the sources, which are the places of error, are many in both [definition and description], especially in definitions. For definitions, according to the law established before and freed from all that are the constitutives and their arrangement in a particular order, are among the things difficult to prove because finding all the constitutives—the genera, differentiae, differentiae of genera, and genera of differentiae—in every species is difficult. Perhaps the species has differentiae; if some of them are found and the differentiating differentia is found, most often the opinion falls upon the forms that it has no differentia other than that. Likewise, [difficulty in] ascertaining the proximate genus; perhaps the distant genus is taken in the belief that it is proximate. And sometimes the evident concomitants of the species are confused with its essential properties, so they are taken in place of the essential properties. All these are among the things that entail error in definitions. The things that entail error in definitions—nay, in them and in descriptions—are not confined to words without meanings, or vice versa. Rather, they are either verbal, as when the word is a far-fetched metaphor far from understanding, or strange and barbarous, or metaphorical, for example. For the use of such words is not beneficial; rather, the beneficial is to use the customary words appropriate to what is intended from them. And if [such words] are not found, then let one invent the most appropriate words. Or [they are] conceptual, and they are either specific to definitions, or specific to descriptions, or not specific to both but common between them. As for those specific to definitions, they are in several ways: among them is that non-genus is placed in the position of genus, so that the general concomitants are taken in place of genus, such as 'one' or 'existent' in place of 'substance' or 'body' and other genera; or the accident is taken in place of genus in the definition of what falls under quantity or quality or other accidents—and the accident is not genus for them, as you know, but is a concomitant; or the differentia is taken in place of genus, like 'excess' in place of 'love' so it is said about love-sickness that it is 'excess of love'—while 'excess' is the differentia; or the species is taken in place of genus, so it is said about evil that it is 'injustice to people'—while injustice is a species of evil; or one genus is taken in place of another genus, like quantity in place of quality in parity, so it is said that it is a quantity by which the subject is divided into two equal parts—while parity is not a quantity but a quality specific to quantity, as has passed. And of this kind also is when potential (quwwa) is taken in place of actuality (malaka), as it is said, for example, 'He who is described with the virtue of chastity is one who has the power to avoid sensual pleasures'—or actuality in place of potential, as it is said, 'He who is able to do injustice is one whose nature and disposition is to incline towards taking what is not his from the hand of another'—and this is the actuality of injustice, not the power to do injustice; for he who has the power to do injustice may be just. Or matter is taken in place of genus, as it is said about the sword that it is 'a stretched iron with which one cuts'—and iron is the matter. Or the part is taken in place of genus, as it is said about the animal that it is 'a body having a soul'—while body is a part of animal, not a genus. And let it not be thought that this contradicts what was said before that body is a genus of animal, for it does not contradict it. For it is possible that body is considered in a respect such that in that respect it is only a part. And such cannot be predicated of animal because the part is never predicated of the whole, so it is not a genus for it then, even if it is taken in a respect such that in that respect it is only a genus.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of errors that can occur in definitions (حدود) and descriptions (رسوم). The author begins by noting the difficulty in constructing correct definitions: one must identify all constitutive elements (genera, differentiae, etc.) and arrange them correctly. The practical challenges include: (1) failing to find all differentiae of a species, often mistaking the first found as the only one; (2) using a remote genus instead of the proximate genus; (3) confusing necessary concomitants (لوازم) with essential attributes (ذاتيات). These are general sources of error. The author then distinguishes between verbal errors (لفظية) and conceptual errors (معنوية). Verbal errors involve using inappropriate words: far-fetched metaphors, strange/uncouth terms, or vague expressions. The solution is to use customary and appropriate words, or invent new ones if needed. Conceptual errors are further divided into three categories: those specific to definitions, those specific to descriptions, and those common to both. For definitions, the author enumerates several specific mistakes: (a) placing a non-genus (like a general concomitant such as 'one' or 'existent') in place of the true genus; (b) placing an accident (عرض) in place of genus (e.g., defining a quantity-quality thing by a genus that is an accident); (c) placing the differentia (فصل) in place of genus (e.g., defining 'love-sickness' as 'excess of love'—excess is the differentia, not genus); (d) placing the species (نوع) in place of genus (e.g., defining 'evil' as 'injustice'—injustice is a species, not genus); (e) replacing one genus with another (e.g., using quantity instead of quality for parity); (f) replacing actuality (ملكة) with potential (قوة) or vice versa (e.g., defining chastity as 'ability to avoid desires'—but chastity is an actual state, not mere ability); (g) using matter (مادة) in place of genus (e.g., defining 'sword' as 'iron'—iron is matter, not genus); (h) using a part (جزء) in place of genus (e.g., defining 'animal' as 'body having a soul'—body is a part, not genus). The author clarifies that body can be considered both as genus (in respect of corporeal substance) and as part (in respect of animal), but when predicated as a part, it cannot be a genus. These examples illustrate the precision required in logical definitions.

Key Terms
لفظية
مستعارة
غريبة وحشية
مجازية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, what is the problem with using far-fetched metaphors in definitions?
A) They are acceptable if they are scientific.
B) They are not beneficial.
C) They should be used to impress.
D) They are the best kind.

Key Takeaway
Verbal errors in definitions arise from using words that are not customary; the solution is to use familiar, appropriate terms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Find a definition from a fiqh text (e.g., Ibn Rushd's Bidayat al-Mujtahid) that uses an unusual term and suggest a more customary synonym.

Conceptual Errors: Mistaking Non-genus for Genus
Author enumerates conceptual errors specific to definitions: using non-genus as genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify definitions where a non-genus (e.g., accident, species, differentia) is incorrectly used as the genus.

Core Idea
The genus must be the proper category; using a concomitant, accident, differentia, or species as genus renders the definition defective.

The author lists several ways in which the genus is replaced by something else: using a general concomitant like 'existent', an accident, a differentia, a species, or even another wrong genus. Each substitution makes the definition invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the supposed genus
Look at the term that is placed as the broader category in the definition.
2
Check if it is truly a genus
Is it a broader category that includes the definiendum? Compare with the list of common errors.
Common errors: using concomitant, accident, differentia, species, or wrong genus.
3
Categorize the error
Determine which type of non-genus is being used: concomitant (e.g., 'one'), accident (e.g., 'quantity' for quality), differentia (e.g., 'excess' for love), species (e.g., 'injustice' for evil), or a different genus (e.g., 'quantity' for parity).
4
Suggest the correct genus
Replace the incorrect term with the proper genus that accurately captures the broader category.

Conceptual Errors: Confusing Potential with Actuality
Author gives examples of confusing potential and actuality in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between potential (quwwa) and actuality (malaka) in definitions and identify when one is mistakenly used for the other.

Core Idea
A virtue is an actual disposition, not a mere capacity; confusing these leads to defining a virtue incorrectly.

The author highlights a specific error where definitions of virtues confuse the state of having a quality with the mere ability to perform it. For example, chastity should be defined as an actual disposition, not just the power to avoid desires.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Determine if the term being defined is a virtue or a character trait (like chastity, justice).
2
Examine the definition
Look for words indicating capacity (e.g., 'ability to', 'power to') vs. actual state (e.g., 'disposition', 'habit').
3
Test with counterexample
If the definition uses potential, consider someone who has the ability but does not act accordingly. Does that fit the definition? For example, a person with the power to do injustice may not be actually unjust.
4
Correct the definition
Replace the potential term with an actuality term to capture the settled disposition.

الجنس القريب فربما يؤخذ البعيد على اعتقاد أنه قريب. وربما اشتبهت اللوازم البينة للنوع بذاتياته فيؤخذ بدل الذاتيات، وهذه كلها من جملة ما يستلزم الخلل في الحدود والأمور المستلزمة للخلل في الحدود بل فيها وفي الرسوم لا تكون مختصة بالألفاظ دون المعاني، أو بالعكس بل هي إما لفظية كما إذا كانت اللفطة مستعارة بعيدة عن الفهم أو غريبة وحشية، أو مجازية مثلا. فإن استعمال مثل هذه الألفاظ لا يكون مفيدا بل المفيد هو أن يستعمل من الألفاظ المعتادة المناسبة لما هو المقصود منها. وإن لم يوجد فليخترع من أشد الألفاظ مناسبة. وإما معنوية وإنها إما أن تكون مختصة بالحدود أو مختصة بالرسوم أو لا تكون مختصة بهما بل هي مشتركة بينهما: أما مختصة بالحدود فإنها على وجوه؛ منها أن يوضع غير الجنس مكان الجنس فيؤخذ من اللوازم العامة مكان الجنس نحو الواحد أو الموجود مثلا مكان الجوهر والجسم وغير ذلك من الأجناس؛ أو يؤخذ العرض مكان الجنس في تعريف ما يكون تحت الكم أو الكيف أو غير ذلك من الأعراض، والعرض لا يكون لها كما علمت بل هو من اللوازم؛ أو يؤخذ الفصل مكان الجنس نحو الإفراط مكان المحبة فيقال في العشق إنه إفراط المحبة، والإفراط هو الفصل؛ أو يؤخذ النوع مكان الجنس فيقال في الشر إنه ظلم الناس، والظلم نوع من الشر؛ أو يؤخذ جنس مكان جنس نحو الكم مكان الكيف في الزوجية فيقال إنها كمية ينقسم بها المحل بقسمين متساويين، والزوجية ليست بكمية بل هي كيفية مختصة بالكمية كما مر. ومن هذا القبيل أيضًا إذا أخذت القوة بدل الملكة فيقال مثلا المتصف بصفة العفة هو الذي يقوى على اجتناب اللذات الشهوانية، أو الملكة بدل القوة كما يقال القادر على الظلم هو الذي من شأنه وطباعه النزوع إلى انتزاع ما ليس له من يد غيره: وهذا ملكة الظلم لا القدرة على الظلم؛ فإن القادر على الظلم قد يكون عادلا؛ أو تؤخذ المادة مكان الجنس فيقال في السيف إنه حديد ممتد يُقطع به، والحديد هو المادة؛ أو يؤخذ الجزء مكان الجنس فيقال في الحيوان إنه جسم ذو نفس والجسم جزء للحيوان لا جنس. ولا يظن أنه مناقض لما قيل من قبل إن الجسم جنس للحيوان فإنه لا يناقضه، وقد كان من الممكن أن يوجد الجسم باعتبار لا يكون بذلك الاعتبار إلا جزءا. وذلك لا يمكن أن يكون محمولاً على الحيوان لما أن الجزء لا يحمل على الكل البتة، فلا يكون جنسًا له إذن وإن يؤخذ باعتبار لا يكون بذلك الاعتبار إلا جنسًا.
Then the sources, which are the places of error, are many in both [definition and description], especially in definitions. For definitions, according to the law established before and freed from all that are the constitutives and their arrangement in a particular order, are among the things difficult to prove because finding all the constitutives—the genera, differentiae, differentiae of genera, and genera of differentiae—in every species is difficult. Perhaps the species has differentiae; if some of them are found and the differentiating differentia is found, most often the opinion falls upon the forms that it has no differentia other than that. Likewise, [difficulty in] ascertaining the proximate genus; perhaps the distant genus is taken in the belief that it is proximate. And sometimes the evident concomitants of the species are confused with its essential properties, so they are taken in place of the essential properties. All these are among the things that entail error in definitions. The things that entail error in definitions—nay, in them and in descriptions—are not confined to words without meanings, or vice versa. Rather, they are either verbal, as when the word is a far-fetched metaphor far from understanding, or strange and barbarous, or metaphorical, for example. For the use of such words is not beneficial; rather, the beneficial is to use the customary words appropriate to what is intended from them. And if [such words] are not found, then let one invent the most appropriate words. Or [they are] conceptual, and they are either specific to definitions, or specific to descriptions, or not specific to both but common between them. As for those specific to definitions, they are in several ways: among them is that non-genus is placed in the position of genus, so that the general concomitants are taken in place of genus, such as 'one' or 'existent' in place of 'substance' or 'body' and other genera; or the accident is taken in place of genus in the definition of what falls under quantity or quality or other accidents—and the accident is not genus for them, as you know, but is a concomitant; or the differentia is taken in place of genus, like 'excess' in place of 'love' so it is said about love-sickness that it is 'excess of love'—while 'excess' is the differentia; or the species is taken in place of genus, so it is said about evil that it is 'injustice to people'—while injustice is a species of evil; or one genus is taken in place of another genus, like quantity in place of quality in parity, so it is said that it is a quantity by which the subject is divided into two equal parts—while parity is not a quantity but a quality specific to quantity, as has passed. And of this kind also is when potential (quwwa) is taken in place of actuality (malaka), as it is said, for example, 'He who is described with the virtue of chastity is one who has the power to avoid sensual pleasures'—or actuality in place of potential, as it is said, 'He who is able to do injustice is one whose nature and disposition is to incline towards taking what is not his from the hand of another'—and this is the actuality of injustice, not the power to do injustice; for he who has the power to do injustice may be just. Or matter is taken in place of genus, as it is said about the sword that it is 'a stretched iron with which one cuts'—and iron is the matter. Or the part is taken in place of genus, as it is said about the animal that it is 'a body having a soul'—while body is a part of animal, not a genus. And let it not be thought that this contradicts what was said before that body is a genus of animal, for it does not contradict it. For it is possible that body is considered in a respect such that in that respect it is only a part. And such cannot be predicated of animal because the part is never predicated of the whole, so it is not a genus for it then, even if it is taken in a respect such that in that respect it is only a genus.
Source pages p. 311

Commentary
This passage provides a comprehensive classification of errors that can occur in definitions (حدود) and descriptions (رسوم). The author begins by noting the difficulty in constructing correct definitions: one must identify all constitutive elements (genera, differentiae, etc.) and arrange them correctly. The practical challenges include: (1) failing to find all differentiae of a species, often mistaking the first found as the only one; (2) using a remote genus instead of the proximate genus; (3) confusing necessary concomitants (لوازم) with essential attributes (ذاتيات). These are general sources of error. The author then distinguishes between verbal errors (لفظية) and conceptual errors (معنوية). Verbal errors involve using inappropriate words: far-fetched metaphors, strange/uncouth terms, or vague expressions. The solution is to use customary and appropriate words, or invent new ones if needed. Conceptual errors are further divided into three categories: those specific to definitions, those specific to descriptions, and those common to both. For definitions, the author enumerates several specific mistakes: (a) placing a non-genus (like a general concomitant such as 'one' or 'existent') in place of the true genus; (b) placing an accident (عرض) in place of genus (e.g., defining a quantity-quality thing by a genus that is an accident); (c) placing the differentia (فصل) in place of genus (e.g., defining 'love-sickness' as 'excess of love'—excess is the differentia, not genus); (d) placing the species (نوع) in place of genus (e.g., defining 'evil' as 'injustice'—injustice is a species, not genus); (e) replacing one genus with another (e.g., using quantity instead of quality for parity); (f) replacing actuality (ملكة) with potential (قوة) or vice versa (e.g., defining chastity as 'ability to avoid desires'—but chastity is an actual state, not mere ability); (g) using matter (مادة) in place of genus (e.g., defining 'sword' as 'iron'—iron is matter, not genus); (h) using a part (جزء) in place of genus (e.g., defining 'animal' as 'body having a soul'—body is a part, not genus). The author clarifies that body can be considered both as genus (in respect of corporeal substance) and as part (in respect of animal), but when predicated as a part, it cannot be a genus. These examples illustrate the precision required in logical definitions.

Key Terms
قوة
ملكة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the error when someone defines 'chastity' as 'the power to avoid sensual pleasures'?
A) It uses actuality instead of potential.
B) It uses potential instead of actuality.
C) It uses a wrong genus.
D) It is correct.

Key Takeaway
Do not define actual virtues as mere capacities; a virtuous person has the actual disposition, not just the ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Find a definition of 'chastity' from an Islamic ethics text and check whether it describes potential or actuality. Correct it if necessary.

Conceptual Errors: Using Matter or Part as Genus
Author mentions using matter or part as genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify definitions where matter or a part is incorrectly used as the genus.

Core Idea
Matter is not the genus; it is the substrate. A part cannot be predicated of the whole as a genus.

The author warns against using the material (e.g., iron for sword) or a component part (e.g., body for animal) as the genus in a definition. Such substitutions make the definition too broad or incorrectly structured.

Guided Path
1
Identify the supposed genus
Look at the term used as the broader category in the definition.
2
Check if it is matter or a part
Determine if the term refers to the material constituent (e.g., iron, water) or a part of the whole (e.g., body, leg).
3
Test predication
Can the supposed genus be truly predicated of the definiendum? For a part, it cannot: 'animal is a body' is false because animal is not merely a body; body is a part. For matter, 'sword is iron' is false because sword is not just iron; it is a weapon made of iron.
4
Replace with proper genus
Suggest the correct genus that encompasses the definiendum (e.g., 'weapon' for sword, 'substance' or 'living body' for animal).

Errors in Definitions: Substituting Genus with Non-Genus
First part of the author's enumeration: errors where non-genus is placed as genus.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify instances where an inappropriate term (accident, differentia, species, or general concomitant) is used in place of the genus in a definition.

Core Idea
The genus must be the true universal category, not any synonym or concomitant.

The author lists several common errors in logical definitions, beginning with the substitution of the genus by terms that are not actually the genus. These include using general concomitants like 'one' or 'existent', accidents, differentiae, species, or even another genus. Recognizing these errors is crucial for constructing correct definitions in Islamic sciences, where precision in defining key terms like 'iman', 'kufr', or 'ibadat' is essential.

Guided Path
1
Identify the term being defined
Look at the definition and find the term (definiendum) that the definition attempts to define. For example, in the passage, 'passionate love' (al-‘ishq) is being defined as 'excess of love'.
2
Extract the genus used in the definition
Determine what the definition presents as the broader category. In 'excess of love', the word 'excess' is given as the genus, but it is not—it is actually the differentia.
3
Check if the genus is correct
Compare the used genus with the true genus by asking: Is this the universal category under which the definiendum falls? For 'passionate love', the true genus is 'love' (mahabbah), not 'excess'.
4
Classify the type of error
Based on the passage, determine which error is committed: using a general concomitant (e.g., 'one' for substance), an accident (e.g., 'quantity' for evenness), a differentia, a species, or a wrong genus.

بكمية بل هي كيفية مختصة بالكمية كما مر. ومن هذا القبيل أيضًا إذا أخذت القوة بدل الملكة فيقال مثلا المتصف بصفة العفة هو الذي يقوى على اجتناب اللذات الشهوانية، أو الملكة بدل القوة كما يقال القادر على الظلم هو الذي من شأنه وطباعه النزوع إلى انتزاع ما ليس له من يد غيره: وهذا ملكة الظلم لا القدرة على الظلم؛ فإن القادر على الظلم قد يكون عادلا؛ أو تؤخذ المادة مكان الجنس فيقال في السيف «إنه حديد ممتد يُقطع به»، والحديد هو المادة؛ أو يؤخذ الجزء مكان الجنس فيقال في الحيوان إنه جسم ذو نفس والجسم جزء للحيوان لا جنس. ولا يظن أنه مناقض لما قيل من قبل إن الجسم جنس للحيوان فإنه لا يناقضه، وقد كان من الممكن أن يوجد الجسم باعتبار لا يكون بذلك الاعتبار إلا جزءا. وذلك لا يمكن أن يكون محمولاً على الحيوان لما أن الجزء لا يحمل على الكل البتة، فلا يكون جنسًا له إذن وإن يؤخذ باعتبار لا يكون بذلك الاعتبار إلا جنسًا. أما اعتباره كونه جزءًا فهو أن يجعل معناه أنه جوهر مركب من هيولى وصورة لا غير. فلا يلتفت إلى ما هو الزائد عليه أصلا؛ إذ هو بهذا الاعتبار جزء لما أن الحيوان ليس هو هذا القدر فقط. فأما اعتبار كونه جنسًا فهو أن لا يجعل معناه مقصورًا على هذا القدر بل يوجد هذا القدر مطلقًا غير مشروط بشرط الاقتصار عليه أو الزيادة فيه ولا يستراب في أن الحيوان جسم بهذا المعنى؛ إذ هو أحد الأنواع التي يمكن دخولها تحت ذلك الجنس، وذلك الجسم على إطلاقه دون شرط الاقتصار على كونه جوهرًا كذا وكذا محمول عليه، وإنه من جملة ما يصح إيراده في حد الحيوان على خلاف ذلك. وعلى هذا في الفصل فإنه على وجوه أيضًا وقد يؤخذ غير الفصل مكان الفصل والغير متعدد فيؤخذ العرض اللازم مكان الفصل نحو المتحرك بالطبع مكان المتحرك بالإرادة فيقال في الحيوان إنه حساس متحرك بالطبع؛ أو يؤخذ الجنس مكان الفصل نحو الحيوان مكان الناطق مثلا، وذلك خطأ قد مرّ ذكره من قبل فإن الجنس أعرف لكونه أعم، والأعرف أقدم؛ أو يؤخذ الفصل مكان الفصل كما يقال في نوع الملكة والحال إنه كيفية يحصل بواسطتها الكمال؛ أو يؤخذ الخاصة مكان الفصل كالعاقل مكان الناطق. ثم إن بحسب الانفعالات فصولاً، والانفعالات إذا اشتدت بطل الشيء، والفصول إذا اشتدت ثبت الشيء.
As for what is specific to definitions, it has several aspects: among them is that non-genus is placed in the position of genus, so general concomitants are taken in the place of genus, such as 'one' or 'existent' instead of substance, body, and other genera; or accident is taken in the place of genus in defining what is under quantity or quality or other accidents, and accident is not a genus for them as you know, but rather it is among concomitants; or differentia is taken in the place of genus, such as 'excess' instead of love, so it is said in defining passionate love that it is excess of love, and excess is the differentia; or species is taken in the place of genus, such as saying about evil that it is injustice to people, while injustice is a species of evil; or one genus is taken in the place of another genus, such as quantity instead of quality in defining evenness, so it is said that it is a quantity by which the subject is divided into two equal parts, whereas evenness is not a quantity but rather a quality specific to quantity as previously mentioned. Also of this kind is when potency is taken instead of habit, such as saying that one described with the attribute of chastity is he who has the potency to avoid sensual pleasures; or habit instead of potency, as it is said that the one capable of injustice is he whose nature and disposition is to incline to take what is not his from another's hand—this is the habit of injustice, not the potency for injustice, for the one potent for injustice may be just; or matter is taken in the place of genus, such as saying about the sword that it is extended iron with which one cuts, and iron is the matter; or part is taken in the place of genus, such as saying about animal that it is a body possessing a soul, while body is a part of animal, not a genus. And one should not think that this contradicts what was said earlier that body is a genus for animal, for it does not contradict it, and it is possible for body to be considered in an aspect such that it is only a part in that aspect. That (part) cannot be predicated of animal because the part is never predicated of the whole, so it is not a genus for it then, even if it is taken in an aspect such that it is only a genus in that aspect. As for considering it as a part, it is to make its meaning that it is a substance composed of matter and form only, so no attention is paid to what is additional to it at all, for in this consideration it is a part because animal is not only this amount. As for considering it as a genus, it is to not restrict its meaning to this amount, but rather this amount exists absolutely, not conditioned by restriction to it or increase in it, and there is no doubt that animal is a body in this sense, for it is one of the species that can fall under that genus, and that body in its absolute sense without the condition of restriction to being such-and-such a substance is predicated of it, and it is among what is valid to mention in the definition of animal contrary to that. And likewise regarding the differentia, it also has several aspects: non-differentia may be taken in the place of differentia, and the 'other' is multiple; so the necessary accident is taken in the place of differentia, such as 'naturally moving' instead of 'moving by will', so it is said about animal that it is sensing and naturally moving; or the genus is taken in the place of differentia, such as 'animal' instead of 'rational', for example, and that is an error mentioned before, for the genus is more known because it is more general, and the more known is prior; or the differentia is taken in the place of differentia, as is said about the species of habit and state that it is a quality by which perfection is attained; or the property is taken in the place of differentia, such as 'rational' instead of 'rational'. Then, according to affections, there are differentiae, and affections when intensified destroy the thing, while differentiae when intensify the thing.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage, from a commentary on logical definitions, catalogs the various errors that occur specifically in definitions (hudud). The author begins by stating that errors in definitions have multiple forms. The first major category is placing something other than the genus in the position of the genus. Examples include using a general concomitant like 'one' or 'existent' instead of the proper genus (e.g., substance, body); using an accident instead of the genus when defining something under quantity or quality; using the differentia instead of the genus, as in defining 'passionate love' as 'excess of love' where excess is the differentia; using the species instead of the genus, like defining 'evil' as 'injustice' while injustice is a species of evil; and using one genus in place of another, such as defining 'evenness' as a quantity when it is actually a quality. The author then notes another set of errors: taking potency (quwwa) in place of habit (malaka), or habit instead of potency; taking matter instead of genus, as in defining a sword as 'extended iron'; and taking a part instead of the genus, as in defining animal as 'a body possessing a soul' where body is a part. The author clarifies that there is no contradiction with the earlier statement that body is a genus for animal, because body can be considered in two aspects: as a part (when restricted to material composition) or as a genus (when taken absolutely). The discussion then turns to errors in the differentia (fasl). These include taking a necessary accident instead of the differentia (e.g., 'naturally moving' for 'moving by will'); taking the genus instead of the differentia (e.g., 'animal' for 'rational'); taking the differentia itself correctly; or taking a property instead of the differentia (e.g., 'rational' instead of 'rational'). Finally, the author notes that according to affections (infi‘alat), there are differentiae, but affections when intensified destroy the thing, whereas differentiae when intensified establish the thing.

Key Terms

الجنس
الفصل
العرض اللازم
النوع
اللوازم العامة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following definitions illustrates the error of placing the differentia in the position of the genus?
A) Defining 'salah' as 'bowing and prostrating' (bowing and prostrating are differentiae, not the genus).
B) Defining 'zakat' as 'obligatory charity' (genus: charity, differentia: obligatory — correct).
C) Defining 'hajj' as 'pilgrimage to Mecca' (genus: pilgrimage, differentia: to Mecca — correct).
D) Defining 'iman' as 'belief' (genus correct).

Key Takeaway
When constructing a definition, ensure that the term used as the genus is truly the universal category, not an accident, differentia, species, or concomitant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which definition error is present in the following: 'Salah is movements and utterances.' (Movements and utterances are general concomitants, not the genus; the correct genus is 'a specific act of worship'.)
?
In the definition of 'iman' as 'belief in the heart', check if 'belief' is the correct genus. According to the passage, what error would it be if 'belief' were actually a differentia?

Errors with Potency, Habit, and Matter in Definitions
Second set of errors: potency/habit and matter substitutions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify errors where potency (quwwa) is substituted for habit (malaka), habit for potency, or matter for genus in a definition.

Core Idea
Potency and habit are distinct; confusing them leads to incorrect definitions of moral and physical attributes.

The passage continues with more specific errors: using potency (quwwa) in place of habit (malaka) or vice versa, and using matter (e.g., iron) instead of the proper genus (e.g., weapon). These errors often occur in defining moral qualities and physical objects. Understanding these distinctions is vital in Islamic ethics and jurisprudence.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the pair: potency and habit
In the definition of a quality, check whether the term used refers to a mere capacity (quwwa) or an acquired disposition (malaka). For example, 'one who is chaste' should be defined by the habit of chastity, not by the potency to avoid pleasures.
The passage warns: mixing these yields an incorrect definition.
2
Identify the definiendum and the substituted term
For the first error, the author gives 'one described with chastity' being defined as 'having the potency to avoid sensual pleasures.' Here, potency is wrongly used for habit.
3
Check for matter substituted for genus
In the example 'sword is extended iron with which one cuts', 'iron' is matter, not the genus. The true genus of a sword is 'weapon' or 'tool'.
4
Determine the type of error
Classify the error as quwwa/malaka confusion or matter substitution.

The Part as Genus Error and the Clarification on Body
Error of part as genus and clarification on body as genus vs. part.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between considering body as a part of animal vs. as a genus, and identify the error of taking a part as the genus.

Core Idea
A part cannot be predicated of the whole, so it cannot serve as the genus; body can be either a part or a genus depending on how it is considered.

The author introduces the error of taking a part as the genus, using the example of defining animal as 'a body possessing a soul', where body is a part of animal, not its genus. He then clarifies that this does not contradict earlier statements that body is a genus for animal, because body can be considered in two different aspects: as a part (restricted to material composition) or as a genus (taken absolutely). This distinction is crucial for understanding how definitions work.

Guided Path
1
Identify the error: part substituted for genus
In the definition 'animal is a body possessing a soul', 'body' is a part of animal, not its genus. The true genus of animal is 'substance' or 'living substance'.
The passage warns: a part cannot be predicated of the whole.
2
Understand the two considerations of body
Body can be considered as a part when its meaning is restricted to 'substance composed of matter and form only'. In that sense, body is a part of animal. But if body is taken absolutely, without restriction, it can be a genus under which animal falls as a species.
3
Apply the distinction
When evaluating a definition, ask: Is the term used as a part or as a genus? If it is a part, it cannot be the genus.

Source Text

بكمية بل هي كيفية مختصة بالكمية كما مر. ومن هذا القبيل أيضًا إذا أخذت القوة بدل الملكة فيقال مثلا المتصف بصفة العفة هو الذي يقوى على اجتناب اللذات الشهوانية، أو الملكة بدل القوة كما يقال القادر على الظلم هو الذي من شأنه وطباعه النزوع إلى انتزاع ما ليس له من يد غيره: وهذا ملكة الظلم لا القدرة على الظلم؛ فإن القادر على الظلم قد يكون عادلا؛ أو تؤخذ المادة مكان الجنس فيقال في السيف «إنه حديد ممتد يُقطع به»، والحديد هو المادة؛ أو يؤخذ الجزء مكان الجنس فيقال في الحيوان إنه جسم ذو نفس والجسم جزء للحيوان لا جنس. ولا يظن أنه مناقض لما قيل من قبل إن الجسم جنس للحيوان فإنه لا يناقضه، وقد كان من الممكن أن يوجد الجسم باعتبار لا يكون بذلك الاعتبار إلا جزءا. وذلك لا يمكن أن يكون محمولاً على الحيوان لما أن الجزء لا يحمل على الكل البتة، فلا يكون جنسًا له إذن وإن يؤخذ باعتبار لا يكون بذلك الاعتبار إلا جنسًا. أما اعتباره كونه جزءًا فهو أن يجعل معناه أنه جوهر مركب من هيولى وصورة لا غير. فلا يلتفت إلى ما هو الزائد عليه أصلا؛ إذ هو بهذا الاعتبار جزء لما أن الحيوان ليس هو هذا القدر فقط. فأما اعتبار كونه جنسًا فهو أن لا يجعل معناه مقصورًا على هذا القدر بل يوجد هذا القدر مطلقًا غير مشروط بشرط الاقتصار عليه أو الزيادة فيه ولا يستراب في أن الحيوان جسم بهذا المعنى؛ إذ هو أحد الأنواع التي يمكن دخولها تحت ذلك الجنس، وذلك الجسم على إطلاقه دون شرط الاقتصار على كونه جوهرًا كذا وكذا محمول عليه، وإنه من جملة ما يصح إيراده في حد الحيوان على خلاف ذلك. وعلى هذا في الفصل فإنه على وجوه أيضًا وقد يؤخذ غير الفصل مكان الفصل والغير متعدد فيؤخذ العرض اللازم مكان الفصل نحو المتحرك بالطبع مكان المتحرك بالإرادة فيقال في الحيوان إنه حساس متحرك بالطبع؛ أو يؤخذ الجنس مكان الفصل نحو الحيوان مكان الناطق مثلا، وذلك خطأ قد مرّ ذكره من قبل فإن الجنس أعرف لكونه أعم، والأعرف أقدم؛ أو يؤخذ الفصل مكان الفصل كما يقال في نوع الملكة والحال إنه كيفية يحصل بواسطتها الكمال؛ أو يؤخذ الخاصة مكان الفصل كالعاقل مكان الناطق. ثم إن بحسب الانفعالات فصولاً، والانفعالات إذا اشتدت بطل الشيء، والفصول إذا اشتدت ثبت الشيء.
As for what is specific to definitions, it has several aspects: among them is that non-genus is placed in the position of genus, so general concomitants are taken in the place of genus, such as 'one' or 'existent' instead of substance, body, and other genera; or accident is taken in the place of genus in defining what is under quantity or quality or other accidents, and accident is not a genus for them as you know, but rather it is among concomitants; or differentia is taken in the place of genus, such as 'excess' instead of love, so it is said in defining passionate love that it is excess of love, and excess is the differentia; or species is taken in the place of genus, such as saying about evil that it is injustice to people, while injustice is a species of evil; or one genus is taken in the place of another genus, such as quantity instead of quality in defining evenness, so it is said that it is a quantity by which the subject is divided into two equal parts, whereas evenness is not a quantity but rather a quality specific to quantity as previously mentioned. Also of this kind is when potency is taken instead of habit, such as saying that one described with the attribute of chastity is he who has the potency to avoid sensual pleasures; or habit instead of potency, as it is said that the one capable of injustice is he whose nature and disposition is to incline to take what is not his from another's hand—this is the habit of injustice, not the potency for injustice, for the one potent for injustice may be just; or matter is taken in the place of genus, such as saying about the sword that it is extended iron with which one cuts, and iron is the matter; or part is taken in the place of genus, such as saying about animal that it is a body possessing a soul, while body is a part of animal, not a genus. And one should not think that this contradicts what was said earlier that body is a genus for animal, for it does not contradict it, and it is possible for body to be considered in an aspect such that it is only a part in that aspect. That (part) cannot be predicated of animal because the part is never predicated of the whole, so it is not a genus for it then, even if it is taken in an aspect such that it is only a genus in that aspect. As for considering it as a part, it is to make its meaning that it is a substance composed of matter and form only, so no attention is paid to what is additional to it at all, for in this consideration it is a part because animal is not only this amount. As for considering it as a genus, it is to not restrict its meaning to this amount, but rather this amount exists absolutely, not conditioned by restriction to it or increase in it, and there is no doubt that animal is a body in this sense, for it is one of the species that can fall under that genus, and that body in its absolute sense without the condition of restriction to being such-and-such a substance is predicated of it, and it is among what is valid to mention in the definition of animal contrary to that. And likewise regarding the differentia, it also has several aspects: non-differentia may be taken in the place of differentia, and the 'other' is multiple; so the necessary accident is taken in the place of differentia, such as 'naturally moving' instead of 'moving by will', so it is said about animal that it is sensing and naturally moving; or the genus is taken in the place of differentia, such as 'animal' instead of 'rational', for example, and that is an error mentioned before, for the genus is more known because it is more general, and the more known is prior; or the differentia is taken in the place of differentia, as is said about the species of habit and state that it is a quality by which perfection is attained; or the property is taken in the place of differentia, such as 'rational' instead of 'rational'. Then, according to affections, there are differentiae, and affections when intensified destroy the thing, while differentiae when intensify the thing.
Source pages p. 312

Commentary
This passage, from a commentary on logical definitions, catalogs the various errors that occur specifically in definitions (hudud). The author begins by stating that errors in definitions have multiple forms. The first major category is placing something other than the genus in the position of the genus. Examples include using a general concomitant like 'one' or 'existent' instead of the proper genus (e.g., substance, body); using an accident instead of the genus when defining something under quantity or quality; using the differentia instead of the genus, as in defining 'passionate love' as 'excess of love' where excess is the differentia; using the species instead of the genus, like defining 'evil' as 'injustice' while injustice is a species of evil; and using one genus in place of another, such as defining 'evenness' as a quantity when it is actually a quality. The author then notes another set of errors: taking potency (quwwa) in place of habit (malaka), or habit instead of potency; taking matter instead of genus, as in defining a sword as 'extended iron'; and taking a part instead of the genus, as in defining animal as 'a body possessing a soul' where body is a part. The author clarifies that there is no contradiction with the earlier statement that body is a genus for animal, because body can be considered in two aspects: as a part (when restricted to material composition) or as a genus (when taken absolutely). The discussion then turns to errors in the differentia (fasl). These include taking a necessary accident instead of the differentia (e.g., 'naturally moving' for 'moving by will'); taking the genus instead of the differentia (e.g., 'animal' for 'rational'); taking the differentia itself correctly; or taking a property instead of the differentia (e.g., 'rational' instead of 'rational'). Finally, the author notes that according to affections (infi‘alat), there are differentiae, but affections when intensified destroy the thing, whereas differentiae when intensified establish the thing.

Key Terms
الجزء
الجسم
الجنس
الكل
الهيولى
الصورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, how can 'body' be considered a genus for 'animal' without contradicting the statement that 'body is a part of animal'?
A) Body cannot be both a part and a genus; the author contradicts himself.
B) Body as a part is restricted to material composition; body as a genus is taken absolutely, without restriction.
C) Body as a part includes the soul; as a genus it excludes the soul.
D) The two considerations are the same; the error is only in the wording.

Key Takeaway
A part cannot substitute for the genus; but one term can serve as a part in one aspect and a genus in another, depending on how it is defined.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Consider the definition of 'insan' (human) as 'a rational animal'. Is 'animal' used here as a part or a genus? Explain using the distinction from the passage.
?
The definition 'human is a body that thinks' – is this an error of part as genus? Why or why not?

Errors in Differentia: Substitutions and Correct Usage
Errors in differentia: substitutions and note on affections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify errors where the differentia is replaced by a necessary accident, genus, or property, and understand the correct use of differentia.

Core Idea
The differentia must be essential and intrinsic, not a concomitant or a broader category like genus.

The author turns to errors specific to the differentia (fasl). He lists several substitutions: taking a necessary accident instead of the differentia (e.g., 'naturally moving' for 'moving by will'), taking the genus instead of the differentia (e.g., 'animal' for 'rational'), or taking a property (khassa) instead of the differentia. He also notes that there are differentiae based on affections (infi‘alat), but warns that affections when intensified destroy the thing, whereas differentiae when intensified affirm it. This distinction helps in evaluating definitions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the differentia in the definition
Look for the term that is supposed to distinguish the species within its genus. For example, in 'human is rational animal', 'rational' is the differentia.
2
Detect substitution errors
Check if the differentia is replaced by: a necessary accident (like 'naturally moving'), the genus (like 'animal' for human), or a property (like 'laughing' for 'rational'). Each is an error.
The passage gives examples: for animal, 'sensing and naturally moving' uses 'naturally moving' (a necessary accident) instead of 'moving by will' (the proper differentia).
3
Understand the affections vs. differentiae
Some differentiae are based on affections (e.g., being hot or cold). However, affections when intensified can destroy the subject (e.g., extreme heat destroys fire), while differentiae intensify the essence (e.g., greater rationality makes a human more human).

Which of the following is an example of taking a necessary accident in the place of the differentia?

Three Errors in Descriptions (Rusum)
Author lists errors specific to descriptions (rusum), such as using less known terms or general accidents.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and correct three common errors in descriptions (rusum): using a term less known than the defined, substituting a general accident for a specific property, and using non-famous properties.

Core Idea
A good description must be more known, use the specific property, and be widely recognized.

In the construction of descriptions (rusum), three key errors must be avoided. The author of the matn highlights that a descriptor must be better known than the thing described, must employ the specific property (khāṣṣa) rather than a general accident, and must rely on well-known (mashhūra) properties. In this lesson, we will unpack these errors using the examples given: 'laughing animal' for man, 'knower of intellectual matters' instead of 'rational', and 'the one who joins by potency' instead of 'the one who separates by potency'. By mastering these criteria, you will be able to evaluate and refine descriptions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
List the three errors from the passage
Read the Arabic carefully and extract the three categories of errors mentioned: (1) description not more known than the described, (2) using a general accident in place of the specific property, (3) using non-famous properties instead of famous ones.
These are the three distinct faults. Each has its own example.
2
Check if the descriptor is more known than the defined
For the first error, compare the descriptor ('laughing animal') with the thing defined ('man'). Is 'laughing' more commonly understood than 'man'? The author asserts it is not, because we have direct sensory knowledge of man. The descriptor must be more familiar to the audience.
The principle: ma yu'raf bihi al-shay' yajibu an yakūna a'rafa minhu.
3
Distinguish specific property from general accident
For the second error, examine the example: 'knower of intellectual matters' (al-‘ālim bi al-umūr al-‘aqliyya) vs. 'rational' (al-‘āqil). The former is a general accident shared by angels and others, not unique to man. Identify whether the property used is truly specific (khāṣṣa) or a common accident (‘araḍ ‘āmm).
The khāṣṣa must be exclusive to the species, not shared.
4
Evaluate the fame of the property
For the third error, consider the pair: 'the one who joins by potency' (al-wāṣil bi al-quwwa) vs. 'the one who separates by potency' (al-fāṣil bi al-quwwa). The author indicates the latter is more famous and thus appropriate. Determine if the property you are using is well-known in the discourse community.
Fame (shuhra) here means common recognition among specialists.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المختصة بالرسوم فإنها على وجوه أيضًا منها أن الرسم لا يكون أعرف من المرسوم فيقال للإنسان حيوان متعجب مثلا، والإنسان المحسوس أعرف منه. ومنها أن يؤخذ العرض العام مكان الخاصة كالعالم بالأمور العقلية مكان العاقل. أو يؤخذ من الخواص الغير المشهورة مكان المشهورة كالواصل بالقوة مكان الفاصل بالقوة والواصل هو العالم بعلم الصنعة، والفاصل هو
As for what is specific to descriptions, it also has several aspects: among them is that the description is not more known than the described, such as saying about man that he is a laughing animal, while sensible man is more known than that. And among them is that a general accident is taken in the place of the property, such as 'knower of intellectual matters' instead of 'rational'. Or non-famous properties are taken in the place of famous ones, such as 'the one who joins by potency' instead of 'the one who separates by potency', and 'the one who joins' is the knower of the science of craftsmanship, and 'the one who separates' is...
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage addresses errors specific to descriptions (rusum), which are definitions that use properties (khawass) that are not essential but are distinguishing. The author lists three types of errors. The first is that the description used is not more known (a‘raf) than the thing being described. For example, defining man as 'a laughing animal' is problematic because 'laughing' is not more known than man himself; we have direct sensory knowledge of man. The second error is taking a general accident (al-‘arad al-‘amm) in place of the specific property (khassa). For instance, describing man as 'knower of intellectual matters' rather than 'rational' – the former is a common accident of many beings (angels, etc.), not specific to man. The third error is using non-famous properties instead of famous ones. The example given is describing a craftsman as 'the one who joins by potency' rather than 'the one who separates by potency'. The 'joiner' (al-wāsil) here refers to the one who knows the science of the craft, while 'the separator' (al-fāsil) refers to the one who applies the craft. The text breaks off at this point.

Key Terms
الرسم
الخاصة
العرض العام
المشهورة

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Defining man as 'a laughing animal'
B) Defining man as 'a rational animal'

D) Defining a craftsman as 'the one who joins by potency'

Key Takeaway
A sound description must use a property that is more known than the thing described, specific to it, and widely recognized.
