Genus: Part of Definition or Universal Category?
Author contrasts two valid uses of genus in definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between considering a genus as a part of a definition and as a universal genus, and explain the consequences for definition.

Core Idea
A genus can be either a restricted part or an unrestricted universal — both are valid in different contexts.

This passage clarifies a crucial nuance in definition theory: the same term, like 'animal', can be taken either as a component of the species' quiddity or as a broader genus under which the species falls. Understanding this distinction is essential for constructing accurate definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two ways of considering genus
The author presents 'as a part' (juz') and 'as a genus' (jins). Note the difference: as part it is restricted to a specific composition (matter and form only); as genus it is unrestricted, absolute body.
2
Apply to the example 'animal'
When considered as a part, 'animal' means 'a substance composed of matter and form only'. When considered as a genus, 'animal' means 'a body in an absolute sense' that includes additional determinations.
3
Understand the consequence for definition
Taking genus as part limits the definition to the essential components; taking it as genus allows the definition to be more general. This affects how precise the definition is.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما اعتباره كونه جزءًا فهو أن يجعل معناه أنه جوهر مركب من هيولى وصورة لا غير. فلا يلتفت إلى ما هو الزائد عليه أصلا؛ إذ هو بهذا الاعتبار جزء لما أن الحيوان ليس هو هذا القدر فقط. فأما اعتبار كونه جنسًا فهو أن لا يجعل معناه مقصورًا على هذا القدر بل يوجد هذا القدر مطلقًا غير مشروط بشرط الاقتصار عليه أو الزيادة فيه ولا يستراب في أن الحيوان جسم بهذا المعنى؛ إذ هو أحد الأنواع التي يمكن دخولها تحت ذلك الجنس، وذلك الجسم على إطلاقه دون شرط الاقتصار على كونه جوهرًا كذا وكذا محمول عليه، وإنه من جملة ما يصح إيراده في حد الحيوان على خلاف ذلك.
وعلى هذا في الفصل فإنه على وجوه أيضًا وقد يؤخذ غير الفصل مكان الفصل والغير متعدد فيؤخذ العرض اللازم مكان الفصل نحو المتحرك بالطبع مكان المتحرك بالإرادة فيقال في الحيوان إنه حساس متحرك بالطبع؛ أو يؤخذ الجنس مكان الفصل نحو الحيوان مكان الناطق مثلا، وذلك خطأ قد مرّ ذكره من قبل فإن الجنس أعرف لكونه أعم، والأعرف أقدم؛ أو يؤخذ الفصل مكان الفصل كما يقال في نوع الملكة والحال إنه كيفية يحصل بواسطتها الكمال؛ أو يؤخذ الخاصة مكان الفصل كالعاقل مكان الناطق. ثم إن بحسب الانفعالات فصول، والانفعالات إذا اشتدت بطل الشيء، والفصول إذا اشتدت ثبت الشيء.
As for considering it as a part, it is to make its meaning that it is a substance composed of matter and form only. So no attention is paid to what is additional to it at all; because it is by this consideration a part since the animal is not only this amount. As for considering it as a genus, it is to not restrict its meaning to this amount but rather this amount exists absolutely, not conditioned by the condition of restriction to it or increase in it, and there is no doubt that the animal is a body in this sense; since it is one of the species that can enter under that genus, and that body in its absolute sense without the condition of restricting it to being such-and-such a substance is predicated of it, and it is among what is valid to be included in the definition of animal contrary to that.
And similarly for the differentia, it also has several aspects. Sometimes something other than the differentia is taken in place of the differentia, and that other is diverse. So the necessary accident is taken in place of the differentia, like 'naturally moving' in place of 'voluntarily moving' — it is said of the animal that it is sensitive, naturally moving. Or the genus is taken in place of the differentia, like 'animal' in place of 'rational', for example. That is an error that has been mentioned before, because the genus is more known by virtue of being more general, and the more known is prior. Or the differentia is taken in place of the differentia, as is said of the species of disposition and state: it is a quality through which perfection is attained. Or the property is taken in place of the differentia, like 'rational' in place of 'rational'. Then, there are differentia according to affections, and affections when intensified destroy the thing, while differentia when intensified strengthen the thing.
Source pages p. 313

Commentary
This passage discusses the two ways of considering a genus (jins) in definition: as a part (juz') of the definition when it is considered as a component of the species, or as a universal genus when it is considered as a broader category under which the species falls. The author uses the example of 'animal' (hayawan) to illustrate: when 'animal' is taken as a part, it means a substance composed of matter and form; when taken as a genus, 'animal' is not restricted to that specific composition but is a body in an absolute sense. The passage then moves to the differentia (fasl) and enumerates various misuses: using a necessary accident instead of a true differentia (e.g., 'naturally moving' instead of 'voluntarily moving'), using the genus instead of the differentia (e.g., 'animal' instead of 'rational'), which is an error because the genus is more known due to its broader extension; and using the differentia correctly for species like disposition (malaka). It concludes by noting that differentia according to affections (infi'alat) behave oppositely to affections: affections when intensified destroy the thing, while differentia when intensified confirm the thing.

Correct and Incorrect Substitutes for Differentia
Author enumerates correct and incorrect uses of differentia.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify correct and incorrect substitutes for the differentia in a definition, and explain why certain substitutions are errors.

Core Idea
Only the true differentia strengthens the species; substitutes like accident or genus weaken or invalidate the definition.

The passage lists several ways the differentia can be misused: taking a necessary accident in place of the differentia, taking the genus in place of the differentia, and also correct uses like taking the property (خاصة) in place of the differentia. Understanding these distinctions is crucial for evaluating definitions in Islamic logic.

Guided Path
1
List the types of substitutions mentioned
The author mentions: 1) necessary accident (عرض لازم) like 'naturally moving' instead of 'voluntarily moving', 2) genus (جنس) like 'animal' instead of 'rational', 3) correct differentia (فصل) as in disposition/state, 4) property (خاصة) like 'rational' instead of 'rational' (correct).
2
Identify which are errors
The text explicitly says taking the genus instead of differentia is an error. For necessary accident, it is implied to be an error because it is not the true differentia.
3
Understand the reasoning behind the error
The genus is more general and more known, so using it as differentia makes the definition imprecise. The necessary accident does not capture the essence.
4
Note the final remark about differentia according to affections
Differentia according to affections (infi'alat) strengthen the thing when intensified, unlike affections which destroy the thing.

Errors Specific to Descriptive Definitions (Rasm)
Author classifies errors specific to descriptive definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify three specific errors in descriptive definitions: using a term equally known, using a general accident instead of a proper property, and using an obscure property.

Core Idea
A descriptive definition must be more known than the definiendum and use a distinctive property.

In this passage, the author outlines errors specific to descriptive definitions (rasm). These occur when the definiens fails to clarify because it is not more known, uses a general accident, or employs an obscure property. Let's learn to spot and avoid these mistakes.

Guided Path
1
Check if the definiens is more known
For each proposed rasm, ask: Is the property used more familiar than the definiendum? For example, defining man as 'animal that wonders' fails because 'sensible human' is more known than 'wonder'.
The definiens must be clearer than the definiendum.
2
Verify the property is a proper property (khassa)
Ensure the property belongs exclusively to the species and is not a general accident. 'Knower of intellectual things' applies to angels and others, so it is not proper; 'rational' is proper to humans.
A general accident ('ard 'amm) like 'knower' is not distinctive enough.
3
Ensure the property is well-known
Avoid obscure or technical properties that are less familiar. 'Connector through potentiality' is obscure compared to 'divider through potentiality' in the sciences.
The property should be common and easily understood.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المختصة بالرسوم فإنها على وجوه أيضًا منها أن الرسم لا يكون أعرف من المرسوم فيقال للإنسان حيوان متعجب مثلا، والإنسان المحسوس أعرف منه. ومنها أن يؤخذ العرض العام مكان الخاصة كالعالم بالأمور العقلية مكان العاقل. أو يؤخذ من الخواص الغير المشهورة مكان المشهورة كالواصل بالقوة مكان الفاصل بالقوة والواصل هو العالم بعلم الصنعة، والفاصل هو العالم بفواصل العلوم. وأما المشتركة بينهما فإنها قد تكون لفظية كما مر، وقد تكون معنوية : منها هو التعريف بنفس الشيء كما يقال الحركة هي النقلة والعلم المعرفة، أو بما هو المساوي في المعرفة كما يقال في العلم ما يعلم به غيره وفي العدد كثرة مجتمعة من الآحاد أو بما هو الأخفى منه كما يقال للحادث ما تعلقت به القدرة، والنار هي الأسطقس الشبيه بالنفس، فإن العلم بالحادث أظهر بالنسبة إلى العلم بالقدرة وتعلقها به وكذلك العلم بالنار بالنسبة إلى الأسطقس والنفس وكونه شبيهًا بها، أو بما لا يعرف إلا به؛ إما صريحًا كما يقال إن الكيفية ما به تقع المشابهة، والمشابهة تعرف بأنها اتفاق في الكيفية، وإما مضمرا كما يقال الاثنين زوج أولا . ثم الزوج يعرف بأنه عدد ينقسم بمتساويين ثم المتساويان بأنهما شيئان واحد منهما مطابق للآخر ثم الشيئان بأنهما اثنان؛ ولا بد من استعمال الاثنينية في حد الشيئين من حيث هما شيئان.
As for what is specific to the descriptive definitions (rasm), they also have several aspects. Among them is that the definiens should not be more known than the definiendum, so it is said of man 'animals that wonder', for example, but the sensible human is more known than that. Among them is that a general accident is taken in place of the proper property, like 'knower of intellectual things' in place of 'rational'. Or obscure properties are taken in place of well-known ones, like 'the connector through potentiality' in place of 'the divider through potentiality' — and 'the connector' is the one who knows the science of the craft, while 'the divider' is the one who knows the divisions of the sciences. As for what is common between them (essential and descriptive definitions), they can be verbal, as previously mentioned, or conceptual. Among the conceptual ones are: definition by the thing itself, as in 'motion is transfer' and 'knowledge is cognition'; or by what is equal in knowledge, as in 'knowledge is that through which something else is known' and 'number is a plurality composed of units'; or by what is more obscure than it, as in 'the originated thing is that which power is attached to' and 'fire is the element similar to the soul' — for knowledge of the originated is more apparent relative to knowledge of power and its attachment, and similarly knowledge of fire relative to the element and the soul and its being similar to it; or by what is not known except through it, either explicitly as in 'quality is that through which similarity occurs' — and similarity is known to be agreement in quality; or implicitly as in 'two is an even number first' — then 'even' is known as a number that divides into two equal halves, then 'equal halves' are two things one of which matches the other, then 'two things' are two; and it is necessary to use duality in the definition of 'two things' insofar as they are two things.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage deals with the conditions and errors in descriptive definitions (rasm). It first mentions that the definiens must be more known than the definiendum; for example, defining man as 'animals that wonder' fails because the sensible human is more known. Then it lists errors: using a general accident instead of a proper property (e.g., 'knower of intellectual things' for 'rational'), or using obscure properties instead of well-known ones (e.g., 'the connector through potentiality' instead of 'the divider through potentiality' in defining sciences). The passage then turns to errors common to both essential and descriptive definitions: these can be verbal (like synonymy) or conceptual. Conceptual errors include: (1) defining a thing by itself (e.g., 'motion is transfer'), which is circular; (2) defining by what is equally known (e.g., 'knowledge is that through which something else is known'); (3) defining by what is more obscure (e.g., 'the originated is what power is attached to', where 'power' is less known than 'originated'); and (4) defining by something that is only known through the definiendum, either explicitly (e.g., 'quality' defined via 'similarity', which itself is defined via 'quality') or implicitly (e.g., defining 'two' using 'even', which leads back to 'two' — an infinite regress). The passage ends emphasizing the necessity of avoiding such circularity.

رسم
خاصة
عرض عام

A descriptive definition must use a term more known than the definiendum and a property that is proper and well-known.

Common Errors in All Definitions: Circularity and Obscurity
Author classifies errors common to all definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to detect circular definitions and definitions that use terms equally or less known than the definiendum.

Core Idea
A definition must not define the thing by itself, by something equally unknown, or by something only known through the definiendum.

Beyond errors specific to rasm, both essential and descriptive definitions can suffer from conceptual errors like circularity, using equally known terms, or using terms more obscure than the definiendum. This passage details these common mistakes and shows how they lead to infinite regress.

Guided Path
1
Identify definitions by the thing itself
Look for definitions where the definiens restates the definiendum without advance. Example: 'motion is transfer' – 'transfer' is synonymous with motion.
This is the most obvious circularity.
2
Check for definitions using equally known terms
If the definiens is as unknown as the definiendum, no clarification occurs. Example: 'knowledge is that through which something else is known' – the definiens itself needs definition.
The definiens must be more familiar.
3
Detect definitions using more obscure terms
When the definiens introduces concepts less known than the definiendum, the definition fails. Example: 'the originated is that which power attaches to' – 'power' is more abstract than 'originated'.
The definiens should be simpler.
4
Spot definitions that depend on the definiendum
If the definiens can only be understood through the definiendum, it is circular. Explicit form: 'quality is that through which similarity occurs' – similarity is defined via quality. Implicit form: 'two is an even number first' – even is defined via two, leading to regress.
This creates an endless loop or requires a core concept.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما المختصة بالرسوم فإنها على وجوه أيضًا منها أن الرسم لا يكون أعرف من المرسوم فيقال للإنسان حيوان متعجب مثلا، والإنسان المحسوس أعرف منه. ومنها أن يؤخذ العرض العام مكان الخاصة كالعالم بالأمور العقلية مكان العاقل. أو يؤخذ من الخواص الغير المشهورة مكان المشهورة كالواصل بالقوة مكان الفاصل بالقوة والواصل هو العالم بعلم الصنعة، والفاصل هو العالم بفواصل العلوم. وأما المشتركة بينهما فإنها قد تكون لفظية كما مر، وقد تكون معنوية : منها هو التعريف بنفس الشيء كما يقال الحركة هي النقلة والعلم المعرفة، أو بما هو المساوي في المعرفة كما يقال في العلم ما يعلم به غيره وفي العدد كثرة مجتمعة من الآحاد أو بما هو الأخفى منه كما يقال للحادث ما تعلقت به القدرة، والنار هي الأسطقس الشبيه بالنفس، فإن العلم بالحادث أظهر بالنسبة إلى العلم بالقدرة وتعلقها به وكذلك العلم بالنار بالنسبة إلى الأسطقس والنفس وكونه شبيهًا بها، أو بما لا يعرف إلا به؛ إما صريحًا كما يقال إن الكيفية ما به تقع المشابهة، والمشابهة تعرف بأنها اتفاق في الكيفية، وإما مضمرا كما يقال الاثنين زوج أولا . ثم الزوج يعرف بأنه عدد ينقسم بمتساويين ثم المتساويان بأنهما شيئان واحد منهما مطابق للآخر ثم الشيئان بأنهما اثنان؛ ولا بد من استعمال الاثنينية في حد الشيئين من حيث هما شيئان.
As for what is specific to the descriptive definitions (rasm), they also have several aspects. Among them is that the definiens should not be more known than the definiendum, so it is said of man 'animals that wonder', for example, but the sensible human is more known than that. Among them is that a general accident is taken in place of the proper property, like 'knower of intellectual things' in place of 'rational'. Or obscure properties are taken in place of well-known ones, like 'the connector through potentiality' in place of 'the divider through potentiality' — and 'the connector' is the one who knows the science of the craft, while 'the divider' is the one who knows the divisions of the sciences. As for what is common between them (essential and descriptive definitions), they can be verbal, as previously mentioned, or conceptual. Among the conceptual ones are: definition by the thing itself, as in 'motion is transfer' and 'knowledge is cognition'; or by what is equal in knowledge, as in 'knowledge is that through which something else is known' and 'number is a plurality composed of units'; or by what is more obscure than it, as in 'the originated thing is that which power is attached to' and 'fire is the element similar to the soul' — for knowledge of the originated is more apparent relative to knowledge of power and its attachment, and similarly knowledge of fire relative to the element and the soul and its being similar to it; or by what is not known except through it, either explicitly as in 'quality is that through which similarity occurs' — and similarity is known to be agreement in quality; or implicitly as in 'two is an even number first' — then 'even' is known as a number that divides into two equal halves, then 'equal halves' are two things one of which matches the other, then 'two things' are two; and it is necessary to use duality in the definition of 'two things' insofar as they are two things.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage deals with the conditions and errors in descriptive definitions (rasm). It first mentions that the definiens must be more known than the definiendum; for example, defining man as 'animals that wonder' fails because the sensible human is more known. Then it lists errors: using a general accident instead of a proper property (e.g., 'knower of intellectual things' for 'rational'), or using obscure properties instead of well-known ones (e.g., 'the connector through potentiality' instead of 'the divider through potentiality' in defining sciences). The passage then turns to errors common to both essential and descriptive definitions: these can be verbal (like synonymy) or conceptual. Conceptual errors include: (1) defining a thing by itself (e.g., 'motion is transfer'), which is circular; (2) defining by what is equally known (e.g., 'knowledge is that through which something else is known'); (3) defining by what is more obscure (e.g., 'the originated is what power is attached to', where 'power' is less known than 'originated'); and (4) defining by something that is only known through the definiendum, either explicitly (e.g., 'quality' defined via 'similarity', which itself is defined via 'quality') or implicitly (e.g., defining 'two' using 'even', which leads back to 'two' — an infinite regress). The passage ends emphasizing the necessity of avoiding such circularity.

رسم
خاصة
عرض عام

Key Takeaway
A correct definition must not be circular, use equally or less known terms, or depend on the definiendum for its own understanding.

The Fallacy of Defining Correlatives by Each Other
Author exposes the fallacy of defining correlatives by each other.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the fallacy of circular definition in correlatives and explain why it violates the priority condition for valid definitions.

Core Idea
Defining one correlative by the other is circular—they are known together, not prior.

This passage exposes a common mistake: assuming that knowledge of one correlative (e.g., father) automatically gives knowledge of the other (son), so each can be used to define the other. The author clarifies that correlatives are co-relative—they exist together in the mind and reality—so neither is prior in knowledge. Using one to define the other violates the essential condition that the definiens must be more known and prior.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken claim
Find the assertion that knowledge of one correlative yields knowledge of the other, allowing each to be taken in the definition of the other.
The text says: 'من ظن بأن العلم بأحد المتضايفين مما يوجب العلم بكل واحد منهما'
2
Recognize the condition for valid definition
A valid definiens must be prior in mental existence (more known) than the definiendum. The text mentions this condition: 'ما يعرف به الشيء كان متقدما في الوجود الذهني'.
The definiens must be better known conceptually.
3
Understand the nature of correlatives
Correlatives (mutadā'ifān) exist together in mind and reality; neither is prior to the other. The text states: 'إذا وجدا وجدا معا في الذهن والخارج'.
They are co-relative and simultaneous.
4
Distinguish necessary condition from definiens
The author distinguishes between 'that without which a thing is not known' (a necessary condition) and 'that by which a thing is known' (the definiens). Confusing these is the root error.
One correlative is necessary to know the other, but not sufficient as a definiens.
5
Conclude why the attempted definition fails
Because neither correlative is prior, using one to define the other violates the priority condition, making the definition invalid.
The fallacy is a glaring error (خطأ فاحش).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: [ في أن من الناس من ظن بأن العلم بأحد المتضايفين مما يوجب العلم بكل واحد منهما ]
إن من الناس من ظن بأن العلم بأحد المتضايفين مما يوجب العلم بكل واحد منهما فيؤخذ كل واحد منهما في تعريف الآخر جهلًا بالفرق بين ما لا يعلم الشيء إلا معه وبين ما لا يعلم الشيء إلا به وذلك خطأ فاحش لما أن المعرفة بكل واحد منهما على السواء. فلا مجال لتقدم أحدهما على الآخر في المعرفة بل إذا وجدا وجدا معا في الذهن والخارج، وإذا عدما عدما معا كذلك. وقد كان من الشرائط أن ما يعرف به الشيء كان متقدما في الوجود الذهني وهو المعرفة فلا وجود لذلك الشرط إذن ولا للتعريف كذلك لكنه من جملة ما يتوهم أنه لا يكون خطئًا فاحشًا. فإن المضاف هو الذي ماهيته معقولة بالقياس إلى غيره فتعريفه إذا كان بيانًا لماهيته كان من الواجب أن يعتبر فيه
Section: Concerning that among people are those who thought that knowledge of one of the two correlatives necessitates knowledge of each of them, so each one is taken in the definition of the other, out of ignorance of the distinction between that without which a thing is not known and that by which a thing is known. That is a glaring error, because knowledge of each of them is on an equal footing. So there is no room for the priority of one over the other in knowledge; rather, if they exist, they exist together in the mind and externally, and if they are absent, they are absent together likewise. And it was among the conditions that that by which a thing is known be prior in mental existence, which is knowledge. So there is no existence for that condition then, nor for the definition in that manner. But it is among what is thought to be not a glaring error. For the relative (al-mudaf) is that whose quiddity is conceived in relation to another, so its definition, if it is an explanation of its quiddity, necessarily considers...
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This section critiques a common mistake: the belief that if you know one correlative (e.g., father and son), you automatically know both, and therefore you can use each to define the other. The author explains that this is erroneous because correlatives are co-relative: they are known simultaneously, neither is prior to the other in knowledge. The definition of the relative (al-mudaf) must take into account its relation to the other, but using the other as the definiens creates a circularity because each requires the other for its definition. The condition for a valid definition is that the definiens must be more known and prior in mental existence. Since correlatives are equal in knowledge, one cannot be prior. The author distinguishes between 'that without which a thing is not known' (a necessary condition) and 'that by which a thing is known' (the definiens). Confusing the two leads to this fallacy. The text is cut off at the end, but the point is clear: a proper definition of a relative must not rely on its correlative in a circular manner.

Types of Definition: Shared Conceptual
Author classifies shared conceptual definitions and warns against flawed ones.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify definitions into the four types of shared conceptual definitions and identify invalid ones using the criteria from the passage.

Core Idea
Not all definitions clarify; some are obscure or circle back.

The author of al-Mantiq al-Kabir classifies definitions based on how the definer relates to the defined in terms of knowledge. Some definitions use near synonyms, some use equally known terms, but others fail by using more obscure terms or falling into circularity. Understanding this classification helps you evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences critically.

Guided Path
1
Identify the classification
The author lists four types under 'shared conceptual definitions' (المشتركة المعنوية). Locate each type in the Arabic passage.
The first type is definition by the thing itself; second by coextensive knowledge; third by what is more obscure; fourth by what is not known except through it (circular).
2
Distinguish definition by itself from coextensive knowledge
Definition by itself uses near synonyms like 'motion is transition.' Coextensive knowledge uses terms equally known, like 'knowledge is that by which other things are known.' Compare their examples.
Both are valid, but the first adds no new knowledge.
3
Recognize definition by what is more obscure
Example: 'The originated is that to which power is attached.' The definer (power) is less known than the defined (originated). Such definitions are invalid because they fail to clarify.
This violates the purpose of definition.
4
Detect circular definitions
Circular definitions use the defined in the definer or lead back to it. Explicit: 'quality is that by which similarity occurs' and 'similarity is agreement in quality.' Implicit: The chain from 'two' to 'even' to 'equal parts' to 'two things' to 'two'.
Implicit circularity is harder to spot but equally invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العالم بفواصل العلوم. وأما المشتركة بينهما فإنها قد تكون لفظية كما مر، وقد تكون معنوية: منها هو التعريف بنفس الشيء كما يقال الحركة هي النقلة والعلم المعرفة، أو بما هو المساوي في المعرفة كما يقال في العلم ما يعلم به غيره وفي العدد كثرة مجتمعة من الآحاد أو بما هو الأخفى منه كما يقال للحادث ما تعلقت به القدرة، والنار هي الأسطقس الشبيه بالنفس، فإن العلم بالحادث أظهر بالنسبة إلى العلم بالقدرة وتعلقها به وكذلك العلم بالنار بالنسبة إلى الأسطقس والنفس وكونه شبيهًا بها، أو بما لا يعرف إلا به؛ إما صريحًا كما يقال إن الكيفية ما به تقع المشابهة، والمشابهة تعرف بأنها اتفاق في الكيفية، وإما مضمرا كما يقال الاثنين زوج أولا. ثم الزوج يعرف بأنه عدد ينقسم بمتساويين ثم المتساويان بأنهما شيئان واحد منهما مطابق للآخر ثم الشيئان بأنهما اثنان؛ ولا بد من استعمال الاثنينية في حد الشيئين من حيث هما شيئان.
The world through the divisions of sciences. As for what is shared between them, it may be verbal as previously, or conceptual: among them is definition by the thing itself, as it is said motion is transition and knowledge is cognition; or by what is equivalent in knowledge, as it is said of knowledge that by which other things are known, and of number a multitude of units combined; or by what is more obscure than it, as it is said of the originated that which power is attached to, and fire is the element resembling the soul, for knowledge of the originated is more apparent relative to knowledge of power and its attachment, and likewise knowledge of fire relative to the element and the soul and its resemblance to it; or by what is not known except through it: either explicitly, as it is said quality is that by which similarity occurs, and similarity is known as agreement in quality; or implicitly, as it is said two is an even number. Then the even is defined as a number divisible into equal parts, then the equal parts as two things one of which corresponds to the other, then the two things as two; and the use of twoness in the definition of the two things insofar as they are two is unavoidable.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a classification of definitions (ta`rifat) based on the epistemic relation between the definer (al-mu`arrif) and the defined (al-mu`arraf). The author begins by noting that shared terms between two things can be either verbal (لفظية) or conceptual (معنوية). The conceptual type includes: (1) Definition by the thing itself (التعريف بنفس الشيء), e.g., 'motion is transition' and 'knowledge is cognition'—these are near synonyms. (2) Definition by something coextensive in knowledge (المساوي في المعرفة), e.g., 'knowledge is that by which other things are known' and 'number is a multitude of units'—the definer is equally known as the defined. (3) Definition by what is more obscure (الأخفى), e.g., 'the originated is that to which power is attached' and 'fire is the element resembling the soul'—the definer is less known than the defined, which is a flaw. (4) Definition by what is not known except through the defined itself (ما لا يعرف إلا به): this is circular. Explicit circularity: 'quality is that by which similarity occurs' and 'similarity is agreement in quality.' Implicit circularity: defining 'two' as 'even number,' then 'even' as 'divisible into equal parts,' then 'equal parts' as 'two things,' then 'two things' as 'two'—this goes in a circle, requiring twoness in the definition of 'two things.' The lesson warns against such invalid definitions.

Key Terms
تعريف
مشتركة
التعريف بنفس الشيء
المساوي في المعرفة
الأخفى
ما لا يعرف إلا به

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Definition by the thing itself
B) Definition by what is coextensive in knowledge
C) Implicit circular definition
D) Definition by what is more obscure

Key Takeaway
A valid definition must use terms that are better known or at least equally known, and must not circle back to the defined term.

Refutation of Mutual Definition of Correlatives
Author refutes mutual definition of correlatives

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the vicious circularity in defining correlatives by each other and explain why it violates the condition of priority in definition.

Core Idea
Defining correlatives by each other is circular; the relatum is necessary but not part of the essence.

In this passage, the author refutes a common mistake: thinking that because two terms are correlatives (like father and son), each can be defined by the other. He shows this leads to circularity and explains the correct approach: the relatum is not part of the essence, so it should not be explicitly included in the definition. Let's see how he dismantles this error.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mistaken view
Find the opinion being refuted: that knowledge of one correlative gives knowledge of the other, so each is used to define the other.
2
Understand the author's objection
He distinguishes between 'that without which a thing is not known' (necessary condition) and 'that through which a thing is known' (definitional cause). The mistake confuses these.
3
Grasp the condition of priority
Definitions require the definer to be prior in mental existence. Correlatives are simultaneous, so this condition fails.
4
Analyze the correct definition of a relative
The relatum (mudaf ilayh) is not part of the essence of the relative (mudaf); it is external but necessary. The definition should allude to it subtly without explicit inclusion.
Example: 'father' defined as 'a living being from whose semen another of its species is generated.'
5
Examine the critique of Porphyry
Porphyry's definition of genus and species as correlatives is flawed because the species in that definition is real, not relative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل : [ في أن من الناس من ظن بأن العلم بأحد المتضايفين مما يوجب العلم بكل واحد منهما ] إن من الناس من ظن بأن العلم بأحد المتضايفين مما يوجب العلم بكل واحد منهما فيؤخذ كل واحد منهما في تعريف الآخر جهلًا بالفرق بين ما لا يعلم الشيء إلا معه وبين ما لا يعلم الشيء إلا به وذلك خطأ فاحش لما أن المعرفة بكل واحد منهما على السواء. فلا مجال لتقدم أحدهما على الآخر في المعرفة بل إذا وجدا وجدا معا في الذهن والخارج، وإذا عدما عدما معا كذلك. وقد كان من الشرائط أن ما يعرف به الشيء كان متقدما في الوجود الذهني وهو المعرفة فلا وجود لذلك الشرط إذن ولا للتعريف كذلك لكنه من جملة ما يتوهم أنه لا يكون خطئًا فاحشًا. فإن المضاف هو الذي ماهيته معقولة بالقياس إلى غيره فتعريفه إذا كان بيانًا لماهيته كان من الواجب أن يعتبر فيه ذلك. لكنا نقول: إن المضاف إليه وإن كان لازمًا للمضاف فلا يكون داخلا في حقيقته حتى يعتبر ذكره في التعريف فإنه إذا كان داخلا فيها كان متقدمًا عليها بالذات وحينئذ ينقطع ارتباط المعية بيناهما. وبالجملة فإنه وإن لم يعتبر فيه ذكره صريحًا فقد كان فيه ما يشعر به ذلك، وإنه ضرب من التلطف فيقال في تعريف الأب، مثلا إنه حيوان تولد آخر من نوعه من نطفته من حيث هو كذلك. ثم إذا عثَرتَ إلى ما أشرتُ إليه فلا تلتفت إلى ما هو المنقول عن صاحب الإيساغوجي [Eisegoge] وهو فرفوريوس [Porphyrios] أنه أخذ المضاف في حد المضاف على ظن أرسطو كان يأخذ المضاف في تعريف الجنس والنوع، / فقال: «الجنس هو الكلي المقول على كثيرين مختلفين بالنوع في جواب ما [۸۲] هو والنوع هو الذي يقال عليه وعلى غيره الجنس»، فإن النوع في تعريف الجنس هو الحقيقي لا الإضافي الذي يؤخذ في تعريفه الجنس. ومن هذا القبيل ما إذا أخذ الضد في تعريف الضد، أو ما يساوي النقيض في تعريف ما يساوى به كما يقال الزوج عدد يزيد على الفرد بواحد : ثم يقال الفرد عدد ينقص من الزوج بواحد. وأما التكرار فإنه بدون الضرورة كما هي في الإضافيات خطأ؛ فلا يقال إن الإنسان حيوان جسماني ناطق، والحيوان مأخوذ في حده الجسم فيقال إنه جسم ذو نفس حساس متحرك بالإرادة: فاعتبر بما عرفت فإن التعريف الحدي من أصعب الأمور عند الجمهور.
Section: [On the fact that among people are those who think that knowledge of one of the two correlatives necessitates knowledge of each of them.] Indeed, among people are those who think that knowledge of one of the two correlatives necessitates knowledge of each of them, so each is taken in the definition of the other, ignorant of the difference between that without which a thing is not known and that through which a thing is known, and that is a gross error because knowledge of each of them is equal. So there is no room for one to precede the other in knowledge; rather, if they exist, they exist together in the mind and outside, and if they are absent, they are absent together likewise. And it was among the conditions that that by which a thing is known is prior in mental existence, and that is knowledge; so there is no existence for that condition, nor for the definition likewise, but it is among what is imagined not to be a gross error. For the relative (al-mudaf) is that whose quiddity is understood in relation to something else, so its definition, if it is an explanation of its quiddity, must consider that. But we say: the relative to it (al-mudaf ilayh), although necessary for the relative, is not included in its essence such that its mention in the definition is considered; for if it were included in it, it would be prior to it in essence, and then the connection of simultaneity between them would be severed. In short, even if its mention is not considered explicitly, there is in it what indicates it, and it is a kind of subtlety; for example, in the definition of 'father' it is said: a living being from whose semen another of its species is generated, insofar as it is like that. Then when you have reached what I have indicated, pay no attention to what is transmitted from the author of the Eisagoge, Porphyry, that he took the relative in the definition of the relative on the assumption that Aristotle would take the relative in the definition of genus and species, saying: 'Genus is the universal predicated of many differing in species in answer to what it is; and species is that which is predicated of itself and of which genus is predicated.' For the species in the definition of genus is the real (haqiqi) species, not the relative (idafi) species that is taken in the definition of genus. And of this type is when the opposite is taken in the definition of the opposite, or what equals the contradictory in the definition of what equals it, as it is said: 'even is a number that exceeds the odd by one,' then it is said: 'odd is a number that falls short of the even by one.' As for repetition, it is an error unless necessary, as is the case with relationals; so it should not be said: 'man is a rational animal body,' and 'animal' in its definition includes 'body,' so it is said: 'it is a body possessing a sensitive soul that moves by will.' So consider what you have known, for definition by limit (al-ta`rif al-haddi) is among the most difficult things for the majority.
Source pages p. 314

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the mistaken view that correlatives (mutadayifan) must be defined by each other. The author begins by stating the misguided opinion: some think knowledge of one correlative yields knowledge of the other, so each is used in the other's definition, confusing 'that without which a thing is not known' (ma la yu`lam illa ma`ahu) with 'that through which a thing is known' (ma la yu`lam illa bihi). This is a grave error because both are equally known; neither precedes the other in knowledge. They exist and are absent simultaneously in mind and reality. A condition of definition is that the definer be prior in mental existence, but here that condition fails, making the definition impossible. The author then explains: a relative (mudaf) such as 'father' has its essence understood in relation to something else, but the relatum (mudaf ilayh) is not part of its essence. If it were part, it would be prior by essence, destroying simultaneity. The correct definition subtly alludes to the relation without including it explicitly, e.g., 'father is a living being from whose semen another of its species is generated.' The author then criticizes Porphyry (citing Aristotle) who defined genus and species as correlatives, but the species in that definition is the real species, not the relative one. Similarly, defining 'even' with 'odd' and vice versa is circular repetition, which is invalid unless necessary. The lesson concludes that definition (al-ta`rif al-haddi) is extremely difficult.

Key Terms
متضايفين (mutadayifan): Two correlatives, each of which cannot be understood without the other, e.g., father and son, cause and effect.
مضاف (mudaf): A relative term whose quiddity is understood in relation to something else (the mudaf ilayh).
مضاف إليه (mudaf ilayh): The term to which a relative is related; it is necessary for the relative but not part of its essence.
تعريف حدي (ta`rif haddi): A definition that states the essence (hadd) of a thing, as opposed to a descriptive definition (rasm).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) It is a case of verbal shared definition
C) It is circular and violates the condition of priority in mental existence
D) It is a valid definition of correlatives

Key Takeaway
Circularity in definition arises when the definiendum and definiens are mutually dependent; proper definitions require priority of the definiens in mental existence.

How Negation is Defined Through Affirmation: The Sight-Blindness Analogy
Author explains that negation is defined through affirmation, analogous to blindness through sight.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why negation requires affirmation in its definition and why this does not entail logical contradiction.

Core Idea
Negation is understood only via the affirmation it denies, just as blindness is known only through sight.

This passage tackles a subtle point: how can we define a negative concept without being self-contradictory? The author uses the analogy of blindness, defined as the absence of sight, to show that negation is conceptually dependent on affirmation, yet the two are not simultaneously true of the same thing. This lesson will walk through the reasoning and the analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Problem
The author states that negation is only known through affirmation, unlike affirmation itself. Think: why must a negative concept depend on a positive one?
2
Examine the Analogy: Sight and Blindness
The author gives the example of blindness being defined as 'absence of sight in what is normally sighted.' Notice that sight is part of the definition but not part of the reality of blindness.
3
Apply the Analogy to Affirmation and Negation
Just as sight is part of the definition of blindness, affirmation is part of the definition of negation. The term 'negation' includes the meaning that is negated, but only in definition, not as a real constituent.
4
Address the Misunderstanding of Coexistence
The author stresses that including affirmation in the definition of negation does not mean both are true of the same thing. The even/odd analogy shows how concepts can be parts of each other's definitions without being identical or coexisting.
5
Resolve the Question of Nobility
The author concludes that neither affirmation nor negation is inherently nobler; it depends on context. Reflect on why this matters for defining terms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التصور إلى الإيجاب على معنى أنه لا يعرف إلا به على خلاف الإيجاب. ولا يظن أنه إذا كان عارضًا على الإيجاب كان الإيجاب فيه أو معه فإن من المحال أن يوجد الإيجاب مع السلب بل الشيء الذي لو انفرد كان إيجابًا هو موجود في تعريف السلب كما قيل إن البصر موجود في تعريف العمى ولا يعني به أن البصر موجود مع العمى بل يعنى به أن العمى لا يعرف إلا بأن يذكر عدم البصر . فيقال إنه عبارة عن عدم البصر فيما من شأنه أن يبصر فيكون البصر جزءًا بما يعرفه لا جزءًا منه كذلك الإيجاب يذكر في تعريف السلب فيكون جزءا لما يعرفه لا جزءًا منه . والمعنى الذي يسلب هو موجود في لفظ السلب وإن لم يكن موجودًا في ذوات الأمور. فلو قلت زيدٌ ليس هو عالمًا فقولك هو عالم إيجاب علي زيد لولا حرف السلب؛ فالسلب إذن أمر يرفع المعنى الذي هو الإيجاب فيكون الإيجاب معه في التصور وإن لم يكن داخلا فيه لا بالقوة ولا بالفعل. ولا يلزم منه أن يجتمع الإيجاب والسلب معا: ألا ترى أن الزوج موجود في الفرد والفرد في الزوج كذلك. ولا يلزم أن يكون الزوج فردا والفرد زوجًا حتى يكون الشيء الواحد زوجا وفردًا؛ إذ الزوج جزء الفرد والفرد جزء الزوج لا هو بعينه ولا أن شيئًا واحدًا متصفًا بهما؛ وإنهما من جملة ما يستحيل وجوده على خلاف ذلك، وهو أن يكون الشيء جزءا لشيء أو جزء ما يعرفه كما مر . وليس إذا كان الشيء جزءًا مما يعرفه أن يكون هذا ذلك بعينه بل يلزم منه أن يكون معه في الذهن. وما قيل في أن الإيجاب أشرف أم السلب؛ فالحق فيه أن هذا لا يكون أشرف من ذلك من حيث هو هو، ولا ذلك من هذا كذلك بل يختلف باختلاف المحل إن أريد بكونه أشرف أن يكون أشمل على المصلحة / اللهم إلا أن يراد به غيره وذلك من جملة ما يتوقف الحكم عليه إلى أن يتعلم.
Conception [leads] to affirmation in the sense that [negation] is only known through it, contrary to affirmation. And do not think that if [affirmation] is an accident upon affirmation [?] that affirmation is in it or with it, for it is impossible for affirmation to exist with negation. Rather, the thing which, if isolated, would be affirmation, exists in the definition of negation, just as it has been said that sight exists in the definition of blindness, and it is not meant by that that sight exists with blindness; rather it is meant that blindness is only known by mentioning the absence of sight. So it is said that it is an expression for the absence of sight in what is apt to see, so sight becomes a part of what defines it, not a part of it. Likewise, affirmation is mentioned in the definition of negation, so it becomes a part of what defines it, not a part of it. And the meaning which is negated exists in the word of negation, even if it does not exist in the essences of things. So if you say 'Zayd is not knowledgeable,' then your saying 'he is knowledgeable' is an affirmation upon Zayd were it not for the particle of negation. Negation then is a thing that removes the meaning which is affirmation, so affirmation is with it in conception, even if it is not included in it either potentially or actually. It does not follow from that that affirmation and negation coexist: do you not see that the even exists in the odd and the odd in the even similarly. It does not follow that the even is odd and the odd is even, so that one thing would be both even and odd; for the even is part of the odd and the odd is part of the even, not identical to it, nor is one thing described by both; and they are among those for which it is impossible to exist contrary to that [i.e., to be both], namely that a thing is a part of a thing or a part of what defines it as previously mentioned. And if a thing is part of what defines it, it does not mean that this is identical to that; rather it follows that it is with it in the mind. And regarding what has been said about whether affirmation is nobler than negation: the truth is that this one is not nobler than that one in itself, nor is that one nobler than this one; rather it differs according to the subject, if by 'nobler' is meant that it is more comprehensive in benefit, unless something else is intended; and that is among matters on which judgment depends until learned.
Source pages p. 319

Commentary
This passage discusses the conceptual relationship between affirmation (ijab) and negation (salb), specifically how negation is defined through the affirmation it denies. The author begins by stating that negation is only known through affirmation, contrary to affirmation itself. He warns against thinking that if affirmation is an accident upon affirmation, that affirmation would be within negation; rather, affirmation is present in the definition of negation but not as a constituent part. The analogy of sight and blindness is used: blindness is defined as the absence of sight in what is apt to see; sight is part of the definition (part of what defines it) but not part of the reality of blindness. Similarly, affirmation is mentioned in the definition of negation as a part of what defines it, not as a part of negation itself. The author then clarifies that the negated meaning exists in the term 'negation' even if it does not exist in external reality. For example, saying 'Zayd is not knowledgeable' implies the affirmation 'Zayd is knowledgeable' that would hold if not for the negation particle. Thus, negation removes the meaning of affirmation, so affirmation is present in the conception of negation but not included in it either potentially or actually. The author insists that this does not entail the coexistence of affirmation and negation, using the analogy of even and odd: the concept of 'even' is part of the definition of 'odd' (as not-even), but a number cannot be both even and odd. Likewise, affirmation is part of the definition of negation but does not make the thing both affirmed and negated. Finally, the author addresses the question of whether affirmation or negation is nobler (ashraf). He concludes that neither is inherently nobler; it depends on the context (mahall) and what is meant by 'nobler'—e.g., which is of greater benefit. This question remains dependent on judgment. This passage is a careful logical analysis of the relation between opposites in conceptual definition, preventing misunderstandings about co-presence.

The Four Types of Categorical Propositions by Plurality
Classification of categorical propositions by plurality in wording

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any categorical proposition into one of four types based on whether its subject and predicate are single or plural in wording.

Core Idea
Every categorical proposition is one of four kinds: single subject/single predicate, plural both, or mixed.

When we look at a categorical proposition like 'Zayd and 'Amr are scholars,' we notice its subject contains two names. The classical logicians classified such propositions based on whether the subject and predicate are expressed as single terms or multiple terms. This lesson unpacks that four-fold division, a foundational tool for later discussions on truth conditions and compound propositions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate
In any categorical proposition, locate the subject (what is being spoken about) and the predicate (what is affirmed or denied). For example, in 'Zayd and 'Amr are scholars,' the subject is 'Zayd and 'Amr' and the predicate is 'scholars'.
2
Check each for plurality in wording
Determine whether the expression of the subject contains multiple items (e.g., two names connected by 'and') and similarly for the predicate. If the subject is one word, it is single; if multiple words indicating multiple individuals, it is plural. Do the same for the predicate.
3
Apply the four-fold scheme
Combine the results: (1) neither plural – both single; (2) both plural; (3) subject plural, predicate single; (4) subject single, predicate plural. This covers all possibilities.
The passage states that a proposition is single only if the subject is one in terms of meaning (even if plural in wording it may still be considered one if it refers to a single group).
4
Test with examples
Take an Islamic example: 'Allāh is One' has a single subject and single predicate (type 1). 'Mūsā and Hārūn are prophets' has a plural subject and single predicate (type 3). 'The prophets are messengers and warners' has a single subject and plural predicate (type 4). 'Zayd and 'Amr are standing and sitting' has both plural (type 2).
Note that the logician's interest is whether the proposition is considered one or many for truth evaluation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في تعريف حال القضايا المتكثرة واللامتكثرة اللاتي تختلف حال صدقها وكذبها بحسب التفريق والجمع واللاتي لا تختلف. أما القضية الحملية فإنها على أربعة أقسام بحسب التكثر في اللفظ وعدم التكثر وذلك لأنها مشتملة على الموضوع والمحمول لا محالة. وإنه لا يخلو من أن لا يتكثر أحدهما أصلا أو يتكثر كل واحد منهما أو يتكثر هذا دون ذلك أو بالعكس لكنها لا تكون واحدة إلا وأن يكون الموضوع واحدا من حيث هو
Chapter: On defining the state of manifold and non-manifold propositions whose truth and falsehood differ according to distribution and aggregation, and those which do not differ. As for the categorical proposition, it is of four kinds according to plurality in wording and lack thereof, because it necessarily contains a subject and a predicate. And it is inevitable that either neither of them is plural, or each of them is plural, or one is plural without the other, or vice versa. But it is not single unless the subject is one in terms of...
Source pages p. 346

Commentary
This passage opens a new chapter on manifold propositions (القضايا المتكثرة) versus non-manifold propositions (اللامتكثرة). The author explains that categorical propositions can be classified into four types based on whether their subject and predicate are expressed as single terms or multiple terms (e.g., 'Zayd and 'Amr are standing' has a plural subject). The classification: (1) neither subject nor predicate is plural; (2) both are plural; (3) subject plural but predicate single; (4) subject single but predicate plural. However, a proposition is considered a single proposition only if the subject is one in terms of ... (the text cuts off, likely 'one in terms of meaning' or 'one in terms of reference'). This sets up the discussion on how truth conditions change when composition is involved.

Collective and Individual Predication: The Three Types
Author classifies predication into three types based on whether predicates apply collectively or individually.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any predication into one of three types: valid both collectively and individually, valid only individually, or valid only collectively, based on the conditions given in the passage.

Core Idea
Not all true predicates can be combined into a single true predicate.

In logic, when we say something about a subject, we often combine predicates. But not all combinations are valid. The author distinguishes three modes of predication: some predicates can be said both individually and together, some only individually, and some only together. Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise reasoning, especially in defining terms.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and its predicates
Look at the examples in the passage: man, Zayd, al-Buraq, 'Anqa. What are the predicates being applied?
2
Check if each predicate holds individually
For each predicate, ask: can this predicate be truly said of the subject by itself?
3
Check if the combined predicate holds
After verifying individually, test if the combination of all predicates into one predicate is true.
4
Classify the predication type
Based on the results: if both hold, it's type 1; if only individual holds, type 2; if only collective holds, type 3.
The passage also warns about figurative and erroneous speech.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم من الأشياء ما يحمل كل واحد منه فردًا فردًا؛ وذلك إما أن يصح حمله جملة وفرادى، كما يقال «الإنسان حيوان، الإنسان ناطق، ويقال أيضًا الإنسان حيوا حیوان ناطق». وعلى هذا في سائر الحدود. وإما أن لا يصح جملة وفرادى بل يصح فرادى كما يقال زيد طبيب وفاره وبصير حال ما يكون طبيبًا دون الوسط وفارها في الخياطة وبصيرًا بالعين، فإنه يصح ولا يصح جملة فلا يقال زيد طبيب فاره بصير بأن يؤخذ الكل محمولا واحدا. فإن هذا يكون نعتاً إياه بأنه طبيب فاره في الطب وبصير فيه؛ ولا يكون كذلك فيكذب صراحًا. ومن هذا القبيل ما إذا قيل الإنسان حيوان جسم أو حيوان حساس فإنه وإن لم يكن كذبًا كما هو عند البعض؛ فلا خفاء في أنه هذيان. ومن الأشياء أيضًا ما يحمل جملة وإنه إما أن يصح حمله فرادى وهو الأول من الأول مثالا؛ وإما أن لا يصح، وإنه إما أن يكون بينها منافاة، كما يقال « » البراقس إنه أسود لا أسود أو أسود أبيض ، ولا يقال إنه أسود أو لا أسود؛ وإما أن لا يكون بينها منافاة كما يقال للعنقاء، إنه موجود في التوهم ولا يقال إنه موجود. وبالجملة يجب على العاقل أن ينظر في هذه الأشياء، ويقول ما يوجبه الحق؛ فإن منها ما يقال بالتجوز ، ومنها ما يقال بالغلط كما علمت.
Then among things are those that are predicated of each one individually; that is: either its predication is valid collectively and individually, as it is said 'Man is an animal, man is rational, and also it is said man is a rational animal'. And upon this are the rest of definitions. Or it is not valid collectively and individually but is valid individually, as it is said 'Zayd is a doctor, a tailor, and an oculist' while he is a doctor without the middle and a tailor in sewing and an oculist with the eye, for it is valid individually and not valid collectively, so it is not said 'Zayd is a doctor, tailor, oculist' taking the whole as one predicate. For this would be describing him as a doctor, tailor in medicine and oculist in it; and it is not so, so it is plainly false. And of this kind is when it is said 'Man is an animal body' or 'an animal sensitive', for even if it is not false as it is according to some, there is no doubt that it is nonsense. And among things also are those that are predicated collectively, and either its predication individually is valid, as the first of the first example; or it is not valid, and either there is incompatibility between them, as is said of al-Buraq that it is black not black or black white, and it is not said that it is black or not black; or there is no incompatibility between them, as is said of the 'Anqa, that it exists in imagination and it is not said that it exists. And in general, it is incumbent upon the intelligent person to look into these things and say what truth requires; for among them are things said figuratively, and among them are things said erroneously, as you know.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage classifies the different ways predicates can be combined in a single subject. The author identifies three main categories. First, there are predicates that can be said both collectively and individually: for example, 'Man is an animal' and 'Man is rational' individually, and also 'Man is a rational animal' collectively. This is the normal case in definitions. Second, there are predicates that can be said individually but not collectively: for instance, Zayd can be a doctor, a tailor, and an oculist, but these apply to different aspects (medical profession, tailoring, eye specialization) and cannot be combined into a single predicate 'doctor-tailor-oculist' without falsehood. The author notes that even if such a combined predicate is not outright false, it is nonsense. Third, there are predicates that are said collectively but not individually. This subdivides into two: either there is incompatibility between the individual components, like describing al-Buraq (a mythical steed) as 'black not black' or 'black white'—these cannot be said individually because they are contradictory, but collectively they might be used figuratively; or there is no incompatibility but individual predication fails because the subject does not exist in reality, like the 'Anqa (a mythical bird) which 'exists in imagination' collectively but 'exists' individually is false. The passage concludes with a general warning to the rational person to carefully distinguish between literal truth, figurative speech, and error.

When Collective Predication Fails: Incompatibility and Non-Existence
Author subdivides the third type of predication (valid only collectively) into cases with and without incompatibility between predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases where a predicate is valid only collectively, and distinguish between predicational failure due to incompatibility of components and due to the non-existence of the subject.

Core Idea
Some predicates can only be used together because separately they are false or contradictory.

Building on the three types of predication, this lesson focuses on the third type: predicates that are valid only when combined. The author distinguishes two subcases: one where the individual components are incompatible (like 'black not black'), and another where the subject does not exist in reality (like the 'Anqa bird). These examples highlight the importance of considering both the logical compatibility of predicates and the ontological status of the subject.

Guided Path
1
Identify the predicate that is said collectively but not individually
From the passage, find examples where the combined predicate is used but individual ones are not (al-Buraq, 'Anqa).
2
Determine if there is incompatibility between the individual components
Check if the individual predicates contradict each other (e.g., black and not black).
3
If no incompatibility, check the existence of the subject
If the subject is fictitious, individual predication may be false even if components are compatible (e.g., 'Anqa exists in imagination' but 'exists' individually is false).
4
Classify the reason: incompatibility or non-existence
This helps in evaluating whether such predications are figurative or erroneous.

ثم من الأشياء ما يحمل كل واحد منه فردًا فردًا؛ وذلك إما أن يصح حمله جملة وفرادى، كما يقال «الإنسان حيوان، الإنسان ناطق، ويقال أيضًا الإنسان حيوا حیوان ناطق». وعلى هذا في سائر الحدود. وإما أن لا يصح جملة وفرادى بل يصح فرادى كما يقال زيد طبيب وفاره وبصير حال ما يكون طبيبًا دون الوسط وفارها في الخياطة وبصيرًا بالعين، فإنه يصح ولا يصح جملة فلا يقال زيد طبيب فاره بصير بأن يؤخذ الكل محمولا واحدا. فإن هذا يكون نعتاً إياه بأنه طبيب فاره في الطب وبصير فيه؛ ولا يكون كذلك فيكذب صراحًا. ومن هذا القبيل ما إذا قيل الإنسان حيوان جسم أو حيوان حساس فإنه وإن لم يكن كذبًا كما هو عند البعض؛ فلا خفاء في أنه هذيان. ومن الأشياء أيضًا ما يحمل جملة وإنه إما أن يصح حمله فرادى وهو الأول من الأول مثالا؛ وإما أن لا يصح، وإنه إما أن يكون بينها منافاة، كما يقال « » البراقس إنه أسود لا أسود أو أسود أبيض ، ولا يقال إنه أسود أو لا أسود؛ وإما أن لا يكون بينها منافاة كما يقال للعنقاء، إنه موجود في التوهم ولا يقال إنه موجود. وبالجملة يجب على العاقل أن ينظر في هذه الأشياء، ويقول ما يوجبه الحق؛ فإن منها ما يقال بالتجوز ، ومنها ما يقال بالغلط كما علمت.
Then among things are those that are predicated of each one individually; that is: either its predication is valid collectively and individually, as it is said 'Man is an animal, man is rational, and also it is said man is a rational animal'. And upon this are the rest of definitions. Or it is not valid collectively and individually but is valid individually, as it is said 'Zayd is a doctor, a tailor, and an oculist' while he is a doctor without the middle and a tailor in sewing and an oculist with the eye, for it is valid individually and not valid collectively, so it is not said 'Zayd is a doctor, tailor, oculist' taking the whole as one predicate. For this would be describing him as a doctor, tailor in medicine and oculist in it; and it is not so, so it is plainly false. And of this kind is when it is said 'Man is an animal body' or 'an animal sensitive', for even if it is not false as it is according to some, there is no doubt that it is nonsense. And among things also are those that are predicated collectively, and either its predication individually is valid, as the first of the first example; or it is not valid, and either there is incompatibility between them, as is said of al-Buraq that it is black not black or black white, and it is not said that it is black or not black; or there is no incompatibility between them, as is said of the 'Anqa, that it exists in imagination and it is not said that it exists. And in general, it is incumbent upon the intelligent person to look into these things and say what truth requires; for among them are things said figuratively, and among them are things said erroneously, as you know.
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage classifies the different ways predicates can be combined in a single subject. The author identifies three main categories. First, there are predicates that can be said both collectively and individually: for example, 'Man is an animal' and 'Man is rational' individually, and also 'Man is a rational animal' collectively. This is the normal case in definitions. Second, there are predicates that can be said individually but not collectively: for instance, Zayd can be a doctor, a tailor, and an oculist, but these apply to different aspects (medical profession, tailoring, eye specialization) and cannot be combined into a single predicate 'doctor-tailor-oculist' without falsehood. The author notes that even if such a combined predicate is not outright false, it is nonsense. Third, there are predicates that are said collectively but not individually. This subdivides into two: either there is incompatibility between the individual components, like describing al-Buraq (a mythical steed) as 'black not black' or 'black white'—these cannot be said individually because they are contradictory, but collectively they might be used figuratively; or there is no incompatibility but individual predication fails because the subject does not exist in reality, like the 'Anqa (a mythical bird) which 'exists in imagination' collectively but 'exists' individually is false. The passage concludes with a general warning to the rational person to carefully distinguish between literal truth, figurative speech, and error.

When a combined predicate is not valid individually, the reason may be incompatibility of its parts or the non-existence of the subject, and each requires careful logical analysis.

Three Categories of Predication: Collective and Individual Validity
Author analyzes conditions for valid predication individually and collectively in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to categorize any given composite predicate as valid both collectively and individually, valid individually only, valid collectively only, or invalid, based on the attributes' existence in the subject.

Core Idea
A composite predicate is valid collectively only if its components coexist in the subject in the same respect.

In Islamic logic, predication (haml) is the act of affirming a term of a subject. When multiple terms are predicated together, the logician must distinguish between collective and individual validity. The passage provides a systematic way to test such predications, using examples from everyday descriptions and from the definition of man. This skill is essential for constructing sound definitions and for evaluating arguments in fiqh and kalam.

Guided Path
1
Identify the composite predicate
Look for a multi-term predicate that could be applied as a whole. Examples: 'rational animal', 'physician-horseman', 'black-not-black'.
The composite may be a definition or a description.
2
Test individual predication
Check if each term can be independently predicated of the subject. For Zayd: 'Zayd is a physician' and 'Zayd is a horseman' are true individually with their qualifications.
Qualified individual predication is still valid individually.
3
Test collective predication
Check if the combination can be predicated as one unit. The author says that for Zayd, 'Zayd is a physician-horseman' is false if the attributes are in different respects.
Collective predication implies a single composite attribute.
4
Determine the category
Based on the two tests, classify the predicate into one of four categories: both valid, individual only, collective only, or neither.
Refer to the passage's examples for each category.

ما يقال بالغلط كما علمت. وأما ما لا يصح حمله أصلا لا جملة ولا فرادى فذلك كثير التعداد فلا يقال «الإنسان فرس، الإنسان صهال»، ولا يقال أيضًا «الإنسان فرس صهال». وعلى هذا بالنسبة إلى غير الفرس من أنواع الحيوان والنبات. ثم لقائل أن يقول ما يصح أن يحمل على الشيء جملة وجب أن يصح حمله فرادى وبالعكس لأنه لا يحمل عليه إلا وأن يوجد فيه. غير أنه لا يكون كذلك إلا وأن يوجد فيه جملة ما يوجد فيه مفردًا وبالعكس. فأما إذا لم يكن فلا؛ وقد كان من الأشياء ما يوجد في الشيء جملة لا فردًا، أو فردًا لا جملة كما مر من قبل. والحق فيه أن حمل الشيء على الشيء لاعتبار ما يوجد فيه مطلقا غير حمله عليه باعتبار ما يوجد فيه مقيدا كما أن زيدًا إذا كان طبيبًا دون الوسط وفارها في الخياطة يصح أن يقال إنه طبيب مطلقا ويصح إنه فاره كذلك. وكذلك يصح إنه طبيب دون الوسط مقيدا، وإنه فاره في الخياطة أيضًا ولا يصح أن يقال إنه طبيب فاره: فإن من العادة أن يفهم منه إنه فاره في الطب على ما عرف. اللهم إلا أن يراد به أنه طبيب دون الوسط وفاره في الخياطة فإنه وإن صح كما يصح في التصريح دفعة فلا يصح أن يحمل عليه جملة؛ إذ هذا محمول على Then among things, there are those that are predicated of each individually; either its predication is valid collectively and individually, like saying 'Man is an animal, man is rational, and also man is a rational animal.' And this applies to all definitions. Or it is not valid collectively and individually but valid individually, like saying Zayd is a physician, a horseman, and sharp-sighted while he is a physician without the middle [part], a horseman in tailoring, and sharp-sighted in the eye; for that is valid [individually] and not valid collectively, so it is not said Zayd is a physician-horseman-sharp-sighted by taking the whole as one predicate. For that would be describing him as a physician-horseman in medicine and sharp-sighted in it; and it is not so, and thus it is plainly false. And of this kind is when it is said that man is an animal-body or a sentient-animal; for even if it is not false according to some, there is no doubt it is nonsense. And among things also are those that are predicated collectively, and either their predication is valid individually, which is the first of the first example; or it is not valid, and either there is repugnance between them, as is said of 'al-buraqis' that it is black-not-black or black-white, and it is not said that it is black or not-black; or there is no repugnance, as is said of the phoenix, that it exists in imagination and it is not said that it exists. In sum, the intelligent person must consider these matters and say what truth requires; for among them are those that are said metaphorically, and among them are those that are said erroneously, as you know. As for what is not valid to be predicated at all, neither collectively nor individually, that is numerous: so it is not said 'Man is a horse, man is neighing,' nor is it said 'Man is a neighing horse.' And similarly for other kinds of animals and plants. Then someone may say: What is valid to be predicated of a thing collectively must be valid individually and vice versa, because it is not predicated of it except as it exists in it. However, it is not so except when what exists in it collectively is what exists in it individually, and vice versa. But when it is not, then it is not; and there are things that exist in a thing collectively but not individually, or individually but not collectively, as previously mentioned. The truth of the matter is that predicating a thing of a thing by consideration of what exists in it absolutely is other than predicating it by consideration of what exists in it qualifiedly. For example, when Zayd is a physician without the middle [part] and a horseman in tailoring, it is valid to say he is a physician absolutely, and it is valid he is a horseman similarly. And likewise it is valid that he is a physician without the middle qualifiedly, and that he is a horseman in tailoring also; but it is not valid to say he is a physician-horseman, for it is customary to understand from it that he is a horseman in medicine, according to what is known. Except if what is meant is that he is a physician without the middle and a horseman in tailoring, for even if it is valid as when stated separately, it is not valid to predicate it of him collectively, since each predicate is predicated separately. And if Zayd were a physician in some respect and a horseman in some thing, it would be valid to say he is a physician absolutely and he is a horseman similarly, but it would not be valid to say he is a physician without the middle or a horseman in tailoring, neither individually nor collectively. Nor is it valid to say he is a physician-horseman for the reason mentioned. So the conclusion is: what exists in a thing qualifiedly is predicated of it absolutely and qualifiedly, but it is not valid to predicate it collectively unless it can be said he is a physician-horseman when he is a physician without the middle and a horseman in tailoring. And what exists in it absolutely is valid to predicate of it absolutely but not qualifiedly, whether it is valid to predicate collectively or not; for if it is found in Zayd that he is a physician absolutely and a horseman similarly, it can be that he is a complete physician and a horseman in medicine; and then it is valid to [predicate collectively?]
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage examines the rules governing predication (al-haml) in definitions and descriptions, specifically when predicates can be applied individually (furada) and collectively (jumla). The author begins by outlining three categories: (1) predicates valid both collectively and individually, such as 'animal' and 'rational' for man, where 'human is a rational animal' is valid; (2) predicates valid individually but not collectively, exemplified by Zayd being a physician (in a limited sense), a horseman (in tailoring), and sharp-sighted (in eye) – the collective 'physician-horseman-sharp-sighted' would be false because it implies all attributes apply in the same respect; (3) predicates valid collectively but not individually, including contradictory combinations like 'black-not-black' (repugnance) or imaginary existence like 'phoenix exists in imagination' (no repugnance). The author warns against fallacious predications, such as saying 'man is an animal-body', which may not be strictly false but is nonsensical. He then addresses a potential objection: if a predicate is valid collectively, it should be valid individually and vice versa. He clarifies that this holds only when the attributes exist in the subject in the same mode. However, there are cases where an attribute exists collectively but not individually (e.g., contradictory attributes only as a whole) and vice versa (e.g., separate qualified attributes). The key distinction is between absolute (mutlaq) and qualified (muqayyad) existence of attributes. If Zayd is a physician without the middle part of the body and a horseman in tailoring, he can be called 'physician' absolutely and 'horseman' absolutely, but not 'physician-horseman' collectively, because that would imply he is a horseman in medicine. If he were a physician and horseman absolutely, he could be called both collectively if they are both in the same field (e.g., medicine). The author emphasizes that the truth requires careful consideration of how attributes exist in the subject, whether metaphorically or erroneously.

Absolute vs Qualified Predication: The Key Distinction
Author analyzes conditions for valid predication individually and collectively in definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between absolute (mutlaq) and qualified (muqayyad) existence of an attribute and determine which types of predication (individual, collective, absolute, qualified) are valid based on that distinction.

Core Idea
An attribute that exists only qualifiedly in a subject can be predicated absolutely, but collective predication requires the qualifications to align.

The passage delves deeper into the conditions for predication by distinguishing whether an attribute exists in the subject absolutely (mutlaq) or with a qualification (muqayyad). This distinction is crucial: Zayd may be a physician without the middle part of the body and a horseman in tailoring – his physician-ness is qualified, but he can still be called a physician absolutely. However, combining them into 'physician-horseman' fails because the qualifications differ. Understanding this prevents errors in logical analysis and in the application of legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the attribute and its mode
Determine if the attribute exists in the subject without restriction (absolute) or with a specific context (qualified). Example: Zayd as physician without middle part is qualified.
Absolute existence is without any qualification; qualified existence is tied to a specific condition or part.
2
Test absolute predication
Can the attribute be predicated without the qualification? The author says yes: 'Zayd is a physician' is true absolutely even if he is only a physician without the middle part.
Absolute predication is valid as long as the attribute exists in some way.
3
Test qualified predication
Can the attribute be predicated with the qualification? Yes: 'Zayd is a physician without the middle part' is valid.
Qualified predication specifies the exact mode of existence.
4
Test collective predication with another attribute
Can two qualified attributes be combined into one composite? Only if the qualifications are consistent (e.g., both in medicine). Otherwise, the collective predicate is false.
The author's example: physician-horseman fails because one qualification is 'without middle' and the other is 'in tailoring'.
