مقيدا كما أن زيدًا إذا كان طبيبًا دون الوسط وفارها في الخياطة يصح أن يقال إنه طبيب مطلقا ويصح إنه فاره كذلك. وكذلك يصح إنه طبيب دون الوسط مقيدا، وإنه فاره في الخياطة أيضًا ولا يصح أن يقال إنه طبيب فاره: فإن من العادة أن يفهم منه إنه فاره في الطب على ما عرف. اللهم إلا أن يراد به أنه طبيب دون الوسط وفاره في الخياطة فإنه وإن صح كما يصح في التصريح دفعة فلا يصح أن يحمل عليه جملة؛ إذ هذا محمول على حده وذلك كذلك. ولو كان زيد طبيبًا بوجه ما وفارها في شيء ما يصح أن يقال إنه طبيب مطلقا ويصح إنه فاره كذلك، ولا يصح أن يقال إنه طبيب دون الوسط أو فاره في الخياطة لا فردًا ولا جملة. ولا يصح أيضًا إنه طبيب فاره لما مر . فالحاصل أن ما يوجد في الشيء مقيدًا يحمل عليه مطلقًا ومقيدًا لكن لا يصح أن يحمل عليه جملة حتى يصح أن يقال إنه طبيب فاره فيما يكون طبيبًا دون الوسط وفارها في الخياطة وما يوجد فيه مطلقا يصح أن يحمل عليه مطلقا لا مقيدًا سواء صح أن يحمل عليه جملة أو لم يصح؛ فإنه إذا وجد في زيد أنه طبيب على الإطلاق وفاره كذلك يمكن أن يكون طبيبًا كاملا وفارها في الطب؛ وحينئذ يصح أن
Then among things, there are those that are predicated of each individually; either its predication is valid collectively and individually, like saying 'Man is an animal, man is rational, and also man is a rational animal.' And this applies to all definitions. Or it is not valid collectively and individually but valid individually, like saying Zayd is a physician, a horseman, and sharp-sighted while he is a physician without the middle [part], a horseman in tailoring, and sharp-sighted in the eye; for that is valid [individually] and not valid collectively, so it is not said Zayd is a physician-horseman-sharp-sighted by taking the whole as one predicate. For that would be describing him as a physician-horseman in medicine and sharp-sighted in it; and it is not so, and thus it is plainly false. And of this kind is when it is said that man is an animal-body or a sentient-animal; for even if it is not false according to some, there is no doubt it is nonsense. And among things also are those that are predicated collectively, and either their predication is valid individually, which is the first of the first example; or it is not valid, and either there is repugnance between them, as is said of 'al-buraqis' that it is black-not-black or black-white, and it is not said that it is black or not-black; or there is no repugnance, as is said of the phoenix, that it exists in imagination and it is not said that it exists. In sum, the intelligent person must consider these matters and say what truth requires; for among them are those that are said metaphorically, and among them are those that are said erroneously, as you know. As for what is not valid to be predicated at all, neither collectively nor individually, that is numerous: so it is not said 'Man is a horse, man is neighing,' nor is it said 'Man is a neighing horse.' And similarly for other kinds of animals and plants. Then someone may say: What is valid to be predicated of a thing collectively must be valid individually and vice versa, because it is not predicated of it except as it exists in it. However, it is not so except when what exists in it collectively is what exists in it individually, and vice versa. But when it is not, then it is not; and there are things that exist in a thing collectively but not individually, or individually but not collectively, as previously mentioned. The truth of the matter is that predicating a thing of a thing by consideration of what exists in it absolutely is other than predicating it by consideration of what exists in it qualifiedly. For example, when Zayd is a physician without the middle [part] and a horseman in tailoring, it is valid to say he is a physician absolutely, and it is valid he is a horseman similarly. And likewise it is valid that he is a physician without the middle qualifiedly, and that he is a horseman in tailoring also; but it is not valid to say he is a physician-horseman, for it is customary to understand from it that he is a horseman in medicine, according to what is known. Except if what is meant is that he is a physician without the middle and a horseman in tailoring, for even if it is valid as when stated separately, it is not valid to predicate it of him collectively, since each predicate is predicated separately. And if Zayd were a physician in some respect and a horseman in some thing, it would be valid to say he is a physician absolutely and he is a horseman similarly, but it would not be valid to say he is a physician without the middle or a horseman in tailoring, neither individually nor collectively. Nor is it valid to say he is a physician-horseman for the reason mentioned. So the conclusion is: what exists in a thing qualifiedly is predicated of it absolutely and qualifiedly, but it is not valid to predicate it collectively unless it can be said he is a physician-horseman when he is a physician without the middle and a horseman in tailoring. And what exists in it absolutely is valid to predicate of it absolutely but not qualifiedly, whether it is valid to predicate collectively or not; for if it is found in Zayd that he is a physician absolutely and a horseman similarly, it can be that he is a complete physician and a horseman in medicine; and then it is valid to [predicate collectively?]
Source pages p. 349

Commentary
This passage examines the rules governing predication (al-haml) in definitions and descriptions, specifically when predicates can be applied individually (furada) and collectively (jumla). The author begins by outlining three categories: (1) predicates valid both collectively and individually, such as 'animal' and 'rational' for man, where 'human is a rational animal' is valid; (2) predicates valid individually but not collectively, exemplified by Zayd being a physician (in a limited sense), a horseman (in tailoring), and sharp-sighted (in eye) – the collective 'physician-horseman-sharp-sighted' would be false because it implies all attributes apply in the same respect; (3) predicates valid collectively but not individually, including contradictory combinations like 'black-not-black' (repugnance) or imaginary existence like 'phoenix exists in imagination' (no repugnance). The author warns against fallacious predications, such as saying 'man is an animal-body', which may not be strictly false but is nonsensical. He then addresses a potential objection: if a predicate is valid collectively, it should be valid individually and vice versa. He clarifies that this holds only when the attributes exist in the subject in the same mode. However, there are cases where an attribute exists collectively but not individually (e.g., contradictory attributes only as a whole) and vice versa (e.g., separate qualified attributes). The key distinction is between absolute (mutlaq) and qualified (muqayyad) existence of attributes. If Zayd is a physician without the middle part of the body and a horseman in tailoring, he can be called 'physician' absolutely and 'horseman' absolutely, but not 'physician-horseman' collectively, because that would imply he is a horseman in medicine. If he were a physician and horseman absolutely, he could be called both collectively if they are both in the same field (e.g., medicine). The author emphasizes that the truth requires careful consideration of how attributes exist in the subject, whether metaphorically or erroneously.

مطلق
مقيد

B) 'Zayd is a physician' (absolute) and 'Zayd is a horseman' (absolute) individually
C) 'Zayd is a physician without the middle part' and 'Zayd is a horseman in tailoring' individually
D) Both B and C are valid.

Key Takeaway
Attributes that exist in a subject in a qualified manner can be predicated absolutely on their own, but to combine them into a collective predicate, they must exist in the same respect.

Two Meanings of al-Mumkin and Refutation of a Contradiction
Author distinguishes potential and necessary possible, refutes a claim about the necessary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between general and specific possibility (al-imkan al-'amm and al-imkan al-khass) and explain why the necessary (wajib) does not lead to contradiction.

Core Idea
Possible has two senses; confusing them creates false contradictions about the necessary.

This passage tackles a potential confusion: if the necessary (wajib) is possible, can it not exist? That would contradict necessity. The author shows that the problem dissolves once we distinguish two meanings of 'possible' (mumkin). You will learn to navigate this distinction and avoid the fallacy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two meanings of mumkin
Find where the author defines al-mumkin bi al-quwwa (potential possible) and al-mumkin bi al-darura (necessary possible). The first admits both existence and non-existence; the second admits existence only.
2
Understand the opponent's argument
The opponent argues: if wajib is possible, it could be not necessary (contradiction); if it is not possible, it is impossible (also contradiction). Trace this reasoning in the Arabic.
Look for phrases like 'فالواجب ممكن أن لا يكون' and 'فالواجب ممتنع'.
3
See the author's resolution
The author points out that 'possible' is ambiguous. The necessary is possible in the general sense (al-imkan al-'amm) but not in the specific sense (al-imkan al-khass). Thus, no contradiction follows.
Note the distinction between 'إمكان عام' and 'إمكان خاص'.
4
Apply the distinction
Practice explaining why negating specific possibility from the necessary does not make it impossible. Use the author's own example: the potential possible is denied of the necessary possible, but that does not entail impossibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والممكن أن يكون ممكنا أن لا يكون فالواجب ممكن أن لا يكون؛ هذا خلف. وإن لم يكن ممكنا، واللا ممكن ممتنع فالواجب ممتنع؛ وهذا خلف أيضًا؛ إذ الواجب ممكن بالإمكان العام، وغير ممكن بالإمكان الخاص؛ فلا يلزم من كونه ممكنا أن لا يكون واجبا، ولا يلزم من عدم كونه ممكنا أن يكون ممتنعا . فإذن قوله إن الواجب لا يخلو إما في المتعين منهما؛ وإنه لا يتم أولاً في المتعين»، وذلك باطل لما أنه ممكن ، ولا يمكن أيضًا كما مرَّ. فالممكن اسم يطلق على ما بالقوة وعلى ما بالضرورة أيضًا: والأول يصدق فيه ممكن أن يكون وممكن أن لا يكون على خلاف الثاني فإنه لا يصدق فيه ممكن أن لا يكون. وعلى هذا إن ما يسلب عنه الإمكان فلا يلزم أن يكون ممتنعا . فإن الأول وهو الممكن الذي بالقوة يسلب عن الثاني وهو الممكن الذي بالضرورة ولا يلزم منه أنه ممتنع ثم الممكن يطلق على الضروري من حيث إنه عام يعم الضروري وغيره / لا من حيث إنه اسمه بوجه. ثم ههنا بحث آخر
And the possible being such that it is possible that it not be, then the necessary is possible that it not be; this is a contradiction. And if it is not possible, and the non-possible is impossible, then the necessary is impossible; and this is also a contradiction. For the necessary is possible in the general sense of possibility, but not possible in the specific sense. So it does not follow from its being possible that it is not necessary, nor does it follow from its not being possible that it is impossible. Thus his statement that the necessary is not free from either of the two [determinates]... and that it is not established first in the determinate... is false because it is possible, and it cannot be as well, as has passed. So the term 'possible' is applied to that which is potential and also to that which is necessary: the first is one of which it is true that it is possible to be and possible not to be, unlike the second, for which it is not true that it is possible not to be. Accordingly, if possibility is denied of something, it does not necessarily follow that it is impossible. For the first, which is the potential possible, is denied of the second, which is the necessary possible, and it does not follow from that that it is impossible. Then 'possible' is applied to the necessary insofar as it is general, encompassing the necessary and other than it, not insofar as it is its name in a certain respect. Then here there is another investigation.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage defines two meanings of 'possible' (mumkin) and refutes an argument about the necessary (wajib). The author begins by stating the consequences of treating 'possible' as univocal: if the necessary is possible, it could be not necessary (contradiction); if not possible, it would be impossible (also contradiction). The resolution is that 'possible' has two senses: general (al-imkan al-'amm) and specific (al-imkan al-khass). The necessary is possible in the general sense but not in the specific sense, so no contradiction. Then the author distinguishes the two types: al-mumkin bi al-quwwa (potential possible) admits both existence and non-existence, while al-mumkin bi al-darura (necessary possible) admits existence but not non-existence. He clarifies that negating possibility from something does not entail impossibility, because the potential possible is negated from the necessary possible. Finally, he notes that 'possible' is applied to the necessary only in a general sense, not as its specific name. This sets the stage for the next investigation.

Key Takeaway
The term 'possible' has two meanings: potential (both existence and non-existence) and necessary (existence only); confusing them leads to false contradictions about the necessary being.

Defining Potentiality (Quwwa) and Its Condition
Author defines potentiality and anticipates an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the condition that distinguishes potentiality (quwwa) from non-necessary possibility.

Core Idea
Potentiality requires actual nonexistence; it is narrower than mere possibility.

In this passage, the author makes a crucial terminological distinction. He tells us that potentiality (quwwa) is a subset of possibility but with a strict condition: the thing must not exist currently. This separates potentiality from mere logical possibility, which can apply to existing things as well. Let's unpack this definition.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition
Find where the author says 'quwwa is a name more specific than the possible that is not necessary.' Identify the genus: al-mumkin alladhi laysa bi daruri (the possible that is not necessary).
2
Identify the differentia
The author adds a condition: the thing in potentiality must be non-existent. Contrast this with non-necessary possibility, which can be either existent or non-existent.
3
Recognize the objection
The author raises an objection: if something exists, it is necessary; if non-existent, it is impossible. So anything non-existent would be impossible, not possible. This seems to undermine potentiality. Restate in your own words.
The objector uses an immediate inference: existence implies necessity, non-existence implies impossibility.
4
Preview the response
Note that the author does not abandon potentiality; he introduces a distinction (essential vs. accidental modality) to preserve it. We will cover that in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهو أن القوة اسم أخص من الممكن الذي ليس بضروري: فإن الشيء الذي في القوة شرطه أن يكون معدوما، والذي ليس بضروري لا يكون مشروطًا بهذا الشرط فلا يبعد أن يكون موجودًا في الحال أو لا يكون موجودًا. فإن قلت الشيء إذا كان موجودًا في الحال كان واجبا من حيث إنه موجود فكذلك إذا كان معدوما؛ إذ هو ممتنع من حيث إنه معدوم. فالذي في القوة على ما مر كان ممتنعا لا ممكنا. فنقول: الواجب قد يكون بالذات وهو الدائم الوجود، وقد لا يكون بالذات بل بالغير كالواجب بشرط وقت أو حال. وكذلك الممتنع قد يكون بالذات وهو الدائم العدم، وقد لا يكون بالذات بل بالغير كالممتنع بشرط وقت أو حال أيضًا. وعلى هذا في الممكن من حيث إنه ممكن فإنه قد يكون بالذات وقد يكون بالغير؛ وذلك يعرف من بعد.
And that is that potentiality (quwwa) is a name more specific than the possible that is not necessary. For the thing that is in potentiality has the condition that it be non-existent, while that which is not necessary is not conditioned by this condition, so it is not unlikely that it be existent at present or not existent. For if you say that a thing, if it is existent at present, is necessary insofar as it exists, similarly if it is non-existent, it is impossible insofar as it is non-existent. So that which is in potentiality, as has passed, was impossible, not possible. So we say: The necessary may be by essence (bi al-dhat), which is eternal existence, and may be not by essence but by other (bi al-ghayr), like the necessary conditioned by a time or state. Similarly, the impossible may be by essence, which is eternal non-existence, and may be not by essence but by other, like the impossible conditioned by a time or state as well. And accordingly, the possible, insofar as it is possible, may be by essence and may be by other; and that is known later.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage opens the second investigation (bahth akhar) concerning potentiality (quwwa). The author defines potentiality as a more specific term than the possible that is not necessary (al-mumkin alladhi laysa bi daruri). The key condition of potentiality is that it must be non-existent; in contrast, a non-necessary possible can be either existent or non-existent at present. The author then anticipates an objection: if something is existent, it is necessary by virtue of existence; if non-existent, it is impossible. Thus, something merely potential would be impossible, not possible. The response is to introduce a distinction between essential (bi al-dhat) and accidental (bi al-ghayr) necessity and impossibility. The necessary by essence is eternally existent (like the necessary existent), while the necessary by other is conditioned by a time or state (e.g., a specific change). Similarly, the impossible by essence is eternally non-existent, and the impossible by other is conditioned. The possible itself can also be by essence or by other. This distinction resolves the apparent conflict and shows that potentiality is a valid concept despite the objection.

قوة
ممكن
ضروري

C) It must be necessary.
D) It must be impossible.

Key Takeaway
Potentiality is a restricted form of possibility that requires the thing to be nonexistent.

Essential vs. Accidental Modality: Wajib, Mumtani', and Mumkin
Author distinguishes modalities by essence and by other to resolve an objection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish essential necessity/impossibility from accidental necessity/impossibility and apply this distinction to the possible.

Core Idea
Modality can be intrinsic to a thing's essence or dependent on external conditions.

After defining potentiality, the author addresses the objection that everything non-existent would be impossible. His solution is profound: we must distinguish between modality by essence and modality by other. This allows us to say that a potential thing may be impossible now (by other) but not essentially impossible. Let's explore this crucial distinction.

Guided Path
1
Locate the response
Find where the author introduces the categories: 'al-wajib qad yakunu bi al-dhat...' He defines necessary by essence (eternal existence), necessary by other (conditional), impossible by essence (eternal non-existence), impossible by other (conditional). Write each definition.
2
Define each category
Using the passage, write a one-sentence definition for each: wajib bi al-dhat, wajib bi al-ghayr, mumtani' bi al-dhat, mumtani' bi al-ghayr.
3
Apply to potentiality
Consider a specific potential thing, like a building before construction. At present, it is non-existent, so it might be considered impossible. But is it impossible by essence? No, because it can exist later. So it is impossible by other (due to current conditions). Explain how this resolves the objection.
The potential thing is impossible by other but possible by essence.
4
Extend to the possible
The author says 'al-mumkin... qad yakunu bi al-dhat wa qad yakunu bi al-ghayr.' Speculate on what 'possible by other' might mean, using the pattern from wajib and mumtani'.
5
Synthesize
How does the essential/accidental distinction resolve the initial objection? (Answer: The potential thing is impossible by other, but possible by essence, so it is not absolutely impossible.)
This preserves the concept of potentiality.

Consider the statement: 'Zayd's existence is impossible before his creation.' Is this impossibility by essence or by other? Explain using the distinction.
